Commentary
This passage presents a foundational rasm al-mufti rule regarding multiple narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa. It begins with a poetic verse stating that from Abu Hanifa came many narrations (riwayat), some he chose, and others were chosen by his companions. The key point: no one else (i.e., no later mujtahid outside the madhhab) has the authority to give a different answer; the companions have settled on these narrations. The commentary then explains the rule: generally, it is not permissible for a mujtahid to have two contradictory opinions on one issue because of contradiction. If the later of the two opinions is known, it is considered a revocation of the earlier. If not, a later mujtahid (like the companions of Abu Hanifa) must prefer one by the testimony of his heart. The footnote (2) expands that this rule establishes that the statements of the companions are part of the Hanafi madhhab because they only transmitted what the Imam had said and later retracted, so following them is following the Imam. Footnote (3) notes there are three opinions on this matter, to be discussed later. This rule is crucial for a mufti: when multiple narrations exist from Abu Hanifa, the later ones abrogate, and the companions' choices are authoritative.

Key Terms
المجتهد (al-mujtahid): A scholar qualified to derive rulings from the sources; here, primarily Abu Hanifa and his companions.
قولان (qawlan): Two contradictory statements or opinions on the same issue.
رجوع (ruju'): Revocation or retraction of an earlier opinion by a later one.

Resolving Conflicting Rulings from Imam Abu Hanifa
Ruling on resolving multiple transmissions from Imam Abu Hanifa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi hierarchy for resolving conflicting transmissions from Imam Abu Hanifa and identify the appropriate method for each level of inquirer.

Core Idea
Conflicting transmissions from the Imam are real; resolve them by chronology, tarjih, or following later scholars — never arbitrarily.

This passage addresses a practical problem for muftis and students: what to do when two contradictory rulings are transmitted from Imam Abu Hanifa? While some argue that such contradiction is impossible, the Hanafi tradition acknowledges the existence of multiple narrations and provides a structured method for resolution. The key is to determine chronology or, failing that, to apply tarjih based on evidence or inner conviction. The passage then outlines who should do what based on the inquirer's level of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Determine chronology
Check whether the later saying is known. If yes, it abrogates the earlier one and becomes the relied-upon ruling.
This is based on the principle that a mujtahid can change his opinion; the later view is a retraction.
2
If chronology unknown, check for internal strength
See if one transmission has supporting evidence that strengthens it. If so, that is the correct one according to the Imam.
This refers to the 'that which strengthens it' mentioned in the text.
3
If no clear strength, apply tarjih by the mujtahid within the madhhab
A mujtahid fil-madhhab should use the preponderators (marjihāt) or, if none exist, choose by inner conviction (shahadat al-qalb).
This is for those who have reached ijtihad within the Hanafi school.
4
If the inquirer is a commoner, direct to a qualified mufti
The commoner ('ammi) must follow the fatwa of the most pious and knowledgeable mufti; he cannot perform tarjih himself.
The commoner relies on taqlid of a living mufti.
5
If the inquirer is a mutafaqqih, follow the later scholars
A student of fiqh (mutafaqqih) should follow the later Hanafi scholars (mutā'akhkhirīn) and act on what is most correct and cautious according to them.
The mutafaqqih does not have the authority to perform tarjih independently.
6
Consider reconciliation before tarjih
As per Kashmiri, attempt to reconcile the two sayings (tathiq) before resorting to tarjih, especially if one is supported by hadith.
Reconciliation preserves both rulings; tarjih is only when reconciliation is impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Know that from Abu Hanifa came narrations that became lofty. He selected some of them, and the rest of the companions selected from him. So there was no answer from anyone else, as the companions swore upon it. Know that what is transmitted from the general scholars in the books of usul is that it is not valid for a mujtahid to have two sayings in one issue due to contradiction. If the later one is known, then it is definitely a retraction. Otherwise, it is obligatory for the later mujtahid to give preponderance (tarjih) by the testimony of his heart, as in some famous Hanafi books. And in some of them: if the date is not known, then if in one of the two sayings there is something that strengthens it, then that is the correct one according to him. Otherwise, if there is a follower (muttabi') who reached the level of ijtihad in the madhhab, he gives preponderance by the previously mentioned preponderators if found, otherwise he acts by whichever he wishes by the testimony of his heart. If he is a commoner, he follows the fatwa of the most pious and knowledgeable mufti in that matter. If he is a mutafaqqih (one who studies fiqh but not a mujtahid), he follows the later scholars and acts by what is most correct and most cautious according to him. The leader of the age, Allamah Anwar Shah al-Kashmiri, said: 'Reconciliation is better than tarjih because it involves acting on both sayings, and acting on them is better than neglecting one, especially if the other saying is supported by hadith. Unless reconciliation is impossible or evidence indicates otherwise, then tarjih is necessary, as is the practice of muhaddithin with conflicting hadiths.' And if the statement of the commentator 'tarjih is obligatory' is interpreted as when reconciliation is impossible, then there is no conflict between the two sayings.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage opens with a poem affirming that multiple narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa exist and that his companions selected some. The commentary then addresses a key usul rule: a mujtahid cannot have two contradictory sayings on the same issue because they would conflict. However, the rule provides a method for resolution: if the later saying is known, it is a retraction of the earlier; otherwise, a later mujtahid must perform tarjih (preponderance) based on his inner conviction (shahadat al-qalb). The text then categorizes how different types of people should handle conflicting rulings without known dates or strengths: a mujtahid fil-madhhab (one who has attained ijtihad within the school) can use tarjih or choose; a commoner ('ammi) must follow the most pious and knowledgeable mufti; a mutafaqqih (student of fiqh) follows the later scholars (mutā'akhkhirīn) and acts on what is most correct and cautious. The quote from Anwar Shah Kashmiri emphasizes that reconciliation (tathiq) between conflicting reports is preferable to mere tarjih, as it preserves both, unless impossible. This passage guards against arbitrary selection of weak rulings and establishes a hierarchical method for resolving apparent contradictions within the Hanafi tradition.

Key Terms
قولان (qawlān): Two contradictory sayings attributed to a single mujtahid on the same issue.
ترجيح (tarjīḥ): The act of giving preponderance to one of two conflicting transmissions based on evidence or inner conviction.
متفقه (mutafaqqih): A person who studies fiqh but has not reached the level of ijtihad; in this context, someone who follows the later Hanafi scholars (mutā'akhkhirīn).

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Choose whichever ruling he prefers
D) Follow the later scholars (mutā'akhkhirīn)

Key Takeaway
When transmissions conflict, apply the Hanafi hierarchy: chronology, tarjih, then follow the later scholars for the non-mujtahid, and always consider reconciliation first.

Handling Conflicting Qawls: Three Categories of Seekers
Ruling on conflicting qawls for three categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the correct procedure for a mufti when a layman, mutafaqqih, or mujtahid fil-madhhab faces conflicting qawls with unknown dates.

Core Idea
When a madhhab has two views without known date, the procedure differs based on the seeker's legal competence.

This passage from Rasm al-Mufti addresses a common dilemma: the mufti encounters two contradictory rulings from the same imam, with no clear chronological order. The solution depends entirely on who is asking: a fully qualified mujtahid fil-madhhab, a student (mutafaqqih), or a layperson. The rules ensure each category applies the correct level of ijtihad or taqlid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of seekers
The passage classifies people facing conflicting qawls into three types: mujtahid fil-madhhab (capable of tarjih), mutafaqqih (student of fiqh), and layman. Each has a distinct ruling.
The text adds that if the mujtahid has indicated a preference, that saying is taken as correct.
2
Apply the ruling for a mujtahid fil-madhhab
If the seeker is a mujtahid fil-madhhab and no indication from the imam exists, he uses the established preponderance factors (murajjihāt) to weigh the two views. If none exist, he follows his heart's conviction (bi-shahādati qalbihi).
The marginal note clarifies that 'by the testimony of his heart' mirrors the method for conflicting qiyases.
3
Apply the ruling for a mutafaqqih
A mutafaqqih, not capable of independent tarjih, must follow the later scholars (muta'akhkhirūn) and act with what he considers more correct (aswab) and more cautious (ahwat). If later scholars differ, he chooses one; if no opinion from later scholars exists, he can exercise ijtihad after consulting fiqh experts.
The note references al-Tahrir by Ibn al-Humam.
4
Apply the ruling for a layman
A layman (āmmī) must follow the fatwa of the mufti who is most pious and knowledgeable (al-atqā wa al-a‘lam). He cannot arbitrarily select any mufti's opinion.
The condition 'most pious and most knowledgeable' ensures the layman seeks the most reliable authority.
5
Summarize the hierarchy
The three rulings form a clear hierarchy: the most competent seeker uses tarjih, the intermediate follows later scholars, and the layperson follows the best mufti. This preserves the integrity of the madhhab's transmission hierarchy.
This hierarchy is foundational in Hanafi fatwa methodology.

If he does not know the date, and if something that strengthens one of the two sayings is narrated from him, then it is correct in his view; otherwise (2): If a follower who has reached ijtihad in the madhhab is found, he gives preponderance using the preponderance factors previously mentioned if found; otherwise he acts with whichever he wishes by the testimony of his heart. (4) If he is a mutafaqqih, he follows the later scholars, and acts with what he considers more correct and more cautious. The first is to give preponderance to one of the two sayings. The imam of the age, the scholar Anwar Shah al-Kashmiri (may Allah have mercy on him) said: "Applying both is better than giving preponderance, because it involves using both sayings, and using them is better than neglecting one of them, especially if the other saying is supported by hadith; unless reconciliation is impossible or evidence indicates otherwise, then preponderance becomes obligatory, just as the muhaddithin do with conflicting hadiths." Refer to Fayd al-Bari: 1/357. If the commentator's statement "the mujtahid must give preponderance" is interpreted as applying when reconciliation is impossible, then there is no contradiction between the two sayings. (5) His statement: "by the testimony of his heart" as in the case of conflicting analogies. (Maz) (1) His statement: "it" i.e., the strengthened one. (Maz) (2) His statement: "otherwise" i.e., if the mujtahid has not narrated anything that indicates strengthening of one of the two sayings, such as saying: this is more beneficial for the people, or more suitable for the time, or more like correctness, etc. Then the people are of three types: a mujtahid in the madhhab, a mutafaqqih who has not reached the level of ijtihad like most fatwa-givers of our time, and a pure layman; each has an independent ruling. (3) His statement: "in it" i.e., in those issues where there are two sayings and he does not know the later one, nor what strengthens one of them, and no preponderance factor is found. And 'most pious' and 'most knowledgeable' are attributes of the mufti. (4) His statement: "follows the later scholars" and if the later scholars differ, he chooses one of them; and if nothing from the later scholars is found, he exercises ijtihad with his own opinion if he knows the aspects of fiqh, and he consults the people of fiqh. (Maz) Similarly in al-Tahrir by the investigator Ibn al-Humam.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on how a mufti should deal with conflicting sayings (qawls) when the date of the earlier and later rulings is unknown. The author first addresses the case where the mujtahid himself has indicated a preference for one saying (e.g., by saying 'this is more suitable'), then that saying is taken as correct. Otherwise, the mufti examines the enquirer: (1) If the enquirer is a mujtahid fil-madhhab (one capable of tarjih within the Hanafi school), he uses the preponderance factors. (2) If a mere mutafaqqih (a student not at ijtihad level), he follows the later scholars' opinion and acts with what is more correct and cautious. (3) If a layman, he follows the fatwa of the most pious and learned mufti. The commentator adds a note from Anwar Shah Kashmiri that whenever possible, applying both contradictory sayings (e.g., choosing one for one situation and the other for another) is better than discarding one entirely, unless reconciliation is impossible. This mirrors the muhaddithin's method with conflicting hadiths. The marginal notes clarify terms: 'otherwise' (wa illa) means no specific strengthening indication; 'by the testimony of his heart' refers to following one's inner conviction when preponderance factors are absent.

Key Terms
مجتهد في المذهب (mujtahid fī al-madhhab): A person who has reached the level of ijtihad within the Hanafi school, capable of weighing and preferring one opinion over another using the school's principles.
متفقه (mutafaqqih): A student of fiqh who has not reached the level of ijtihad; relies on the rulings of later scholars and exercises caution.
عامي (ʿāmmī): A layperson without specialized fiqh knowledge; obligated to follow the fatwa of the most pious and knowledgeable mufti.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what should a mutafaqqih do when the date of conflicting qawls is unknown and no later scholar's opinion is found?
A) Follow the most pious mufti
B) Exercise ijtihad after consulting fiqh experts
C) Wait until a dating is discovered
D) Follow his heart's testimony

Key Takeaway
When conflicting qawls have no known date, the mufti's procedure depends on the seeker's competence: mujtahid uses tarjih, mutafaqqih follows later scholars, and layman follows the most pious and knowledgeable mufti.

Distinguishing Ikhtilaf al-Qawl from Ikhtilaf al-Riwaya
Methodological distinction for muftis

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between ikhtilaf al-qawl and ikhtilaf al-riwaya and identify the source of each type of conflict.

Core Idea
A difference in the mujtahid's own statements is a qawl; a difference in narrations is a riwaya.

As a mufti, you must know whether a conflicting report originates from the imam himself or from a narrator's error. This distinction determines how you resolve the conflict.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of ikhtilaf
The passage distinguishes between ikhtilaf al-qawl (difference in the mujtahid's own statements) and ikhtilaf al-riwaya (difference in narrations reporting the mujtahid's statements).
2
Attribute the conflict to its source
Qawls are attributed directly to the mujtahid; riwayas are attributed to the narrator. Thus, a conflict in qawls is a real difference from the imam, while a conflict in riwayas is due to narrator issues.
3
Understand the practical implication
When two riwayas conflict, the preponderant (rajih) riwaya is treated as the qawl; the discarded (marjuh) is not considered the imam's position. This is a separate rule mentioned in the margin.
4
Recognize the potential for confusion
Ulama sometimes use 'qawl' and 'riwaya' interchangeably, which can cause confusion. The commentator warns about this, especially in al-Bulayqi's usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مطلب فى الفرق بين اختلاف القول واختلاف الرواية واعلم أن اختلاف الروايتين ليس من باب اختلاف القولين؛ لأن القولين نص المجتهد عليهما بخلاف الروايتين، فالاختلاف في القولين من جهة المنقول عنه لا الناقل، والاختلاف في الروايتين بالعكس، كما ذكره المحقق ابن أمير حاج في شرح التحرير " . مطلب فى بيان الوجوه الخمسة لاختلاف الرواية لكن ذكر بعده (۲) عن الإمام أبي بكر البليغي في "الدرر (۳): "أن الاختلاف في الرواية عن أبي حنيفة من وجوه : { منها : الغلط في السماع، كأن يجيب بحرف النفي إذا سئل عن حادثة ، (۱) . قوله : "بالعكس " أى من جهة الناقل لا من جهة المنقول عنه . Section on the difference between difference in qawl and difference in riwaya. Know that difference in the two riwayas is not of the category of difference in the two qawls; because the two qawls are explicitly stated by the mujtahid, unlike the two riwayas. So the difference in qawls is from the side of the one reported from (the mujtahid), not the reporter; and the difference in riwayas is the opposite, as mentioned by the investigator Ibn Amir Hajj in his commentary on al-Tahrir. Section on explaining the five aspects of difference in riwaya. But he mentioned after it (2) from Imam Abu Bakr al-Bulayqi in al-Durar (3): "Difference in riwaya from Abu Hanifa is from aspects: among them: error in hearing, such as he answers with a negative particle when asked about an incident." (1) His statement: "the opposite" i.e., from the side of the reporter, not from the side of the one reported from. (Maz) Another difference will come: that the preponderant is a qawl, and the discarded is a riwaya. Then know that although they agreed on that, they use one in place of the other broadly, as occurred in the statement of Imam al-Bulayqi, which became a source of confusion for the commentator, so understand. (2) His statement: "but he mentioned after it" is a correction of what the investigator Ibn Hajj mentioned regarding the difference between difference of qawl and riwaya, and its clarification will come in his statement: "I say: aside from the first aspect..." Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is a methodological distinction between two types of conflict in Hanafi fiqh: ikhtilaf al-qawl (difference in the mujtahid's own statements) and ikhtilaf al-riwaya (difference in narrations reporting his statements). The key difference: qawls are directly from the mujtahid (Imam Abu Hanifa or his disciples), while riwayas are reports from narrators. Hence, a conflict in qawls is attributed to the source (imam), whereas a conflict in riwayas is due to the narrators' errors or discrepancies. The author then introduces a section on the five causes of ikhtilaf al-riwaya, citing Imam Abu Bakr al-Bulayqi in al-Durar. Only the first cause—error in hearing (ghalat fi al-sama')—is given in the surviving text, with an example: the imam answered with a negative but the narrator misheard. The marginal notes add another distinction: the preponderant opinion (rajih) is considered a qawl, while the discarded (marjuh) is considered a riwaya. However, ulama sometimes interchange these terms, causing confusion. The commentator notes that al-Bulayqi's usage led to the problem. The note also corrects a possible book title variance: al-Durar vs. al-Ghurar.

Key Terms
اختلاف القول (ikhtilāf al-qawl): A difference in the statements (qawls) attributed directly to the mujtahid, where he explicitly expressed multiple opinions.
اختلاف الرواية (ikhtilāf al-riwāya): A difference in the reports (riwayas) about what the mujtahid said, caused by narrator issues rather than the imam's own statements.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One is from the mujtahid, the other from the narrator
B) One is about hadith, the other about fiqh
C) One is preponderant, the other is discarded
D) One is in 'ibadat, the other in mu'amalat

Key Takeaway
Ikhtilaf al-riwaya does not indicate a real difference from the imam; it is a narrator problem.

The Five Causes of Ikhtilaf al-Riwaya (First Cause: Error in Hearing)
Enumeration of causes for riwaya conflict

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the five causes of ikhtilaf al-riwaya according to al-Bulayqi, and explain the first cause (error in hearing) with an example.

Core Idea
Ikhtilaf al-riwaya arises from five types of narrator errors, starting with mishearing the imam's answer.

The great Hanafi scholar al-Bulayqi identified five reasons why narrators might report different statements from Abu Hanifa. The first and most common is mishearing (ghalat fi al-sama'). Understanding these causes helps muftis evaluate conflicting reports.

Guided Path
1
List the five causes
Al-Bulayqi in al-Durar states that the difference in riwaya from Abu Hanifa has five aspects. However, only the first is given in this passage; the rest are not reproduced here.
The text is incomplete; only the first cause is present. In future sources, the full list can be studied.
2
Explain the first cause: error in hearing
The narrator mishears what the imam said. Example: the imam answered with a negative (e.g., 'no'), but the narrator heard it as an affirmative (or vice versa).
3
Understand the practical impact
Such errors lead to contradictory reports. The mufti must examine the reliability and transmission of each report to identify possible hearing errors.
4
Note the interchangeability of terms
The marginal text notes that ulama sometimes use 'qawl' and 'riwaya' loosely, causing confusion. This is especially relevant when discussing riwaya differences.

Distinguishing Ikhtilaf al-Riwaya from Ikhtilaf al-Qawl
Listing causes of conflicting narrations and distinguishing from ikhtilaf al-qawl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four causes of conflicting narrations from Abu Hanifa and evaluate which causes belong to ikhtilaf al-riwaya versus ikhtilaf al-qawl according to Hanafi rasm al-mufti.

Core Idea
Only mistaken hearing is a true riwaya cause; retraction, qiyas vs. istihsan, and fatwa vs. taqwa are actually qawl differences.

As a mufti, you will often encounter contradictory narrations from Imam Abu Hanifa. Are these genuine differences in transmission, or do they reflect different opinions he held? This lesson unpacks the Hanafi classification of such conflicts, helping you determine when to treat a contradiction as a transmission issue and when to treat it as a substantive difference in the ruling itself.

Guided Path
1
List the four causes
Read the opening statement to identify the four causes of ikhtilaf al-riwaya: mistaken hearing, unknown retraction, qiyas vs. istihsan, and fatwa vs. taqwa.
These are attributed to Imam al-Bulghiti in al-Tahbir.
2
Classify each cause
Determine which causes specifically involve transmission error (riwaya) and which reflect differences in the substance of the opinion (qawl). The commentator argues only the first is pure riwaya.
The last two causes can also occur when the narrator is a single person.
3
Evaluate the single-narrator scenario
Consider the case where both narrations come from one reliable narrator (e.g., Imam Muhammad). The first two causes become implausible, so the difference must be an ikhtilaf al-qawl.
This is the key proof for the commentator's distinction.
4
Apply the distinction as a mufti
When you find conflicting reports from the same narrator, treat them as possible differences in qawl and prefer based on qiyas vs. istihsan or fatwa vs. taqwa. When from different narrators, consider transmission error or retraction.
This methodology prevents misattribution of contradictions.

فتبصر .

(۳) قوله : " ويبعد الوجه الثاني " أى عدم العلم بالرجوع؛ لأن الواحد إذا روى روايتين فبعيد أن يقال : إن إحداهما مرجوع عنه ولم يعلم الراوي بالرجوع، خصوصا إذا
Aspects of difference in narration. And he says: 'It is not permissible', so the narrator becomes confused and transmits what he heard. And among them (the causes): that he has an opinion from which he retracted, and some of those who frequent him know of his retraction, so they narrate the second (opinion), while others do not know it, so they narrate the first. And among them: that he said one of them based on qiyas, and the other based on istihsan, and each one hears one of them, so he transmits as he heard. End of quote. And among them: that the answer in a matter came from two angles: from the angle of the ruling, and from the angle of precaution, so each transmits as he heard. I said: So aside from the first aspect, the difference in the two narrations is also from the perspective of the one from whom it is transmitted; because the difference in them is based on the difference of the two narrated opinions, so they become of the same category. And it is supported by the fact that the transmitter of the two narrations may be one person, for one of the two narrations may be in a book of the core works and the other in the books of nawadir, rather both may be in the core works, and all are from one compiler who is Imam Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him. This contradicts the first aspect and makes the second remote. So the apparent view is to restrict to the last two aspects. It is not hidden that what is meant is that whatever contains two narrations does not exit from one of these sources, not that every instance of that is derived from each one. Therefore, there is no problem with the lack of universality of each in every instance that contains two narrations; for the apparent is that every instance containing two narrations is suitable for one of them, and that is the objective. And Allah knows best. Refer to: al-Taqrir wa al-Tahbir 3/425-426, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyya, Beirut.
Source pages p. 87

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti text explaining the causes of contradictory narrations (riwayat) attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa. It begins by listing four causes: (1) the narrator makes a mistake in hearing, (2) Imam Abu Hanifa had an opinion he later retracted, and some knew of the retraction while others did not, (3) one statement was based on qiyas (analogy) and the other on istihsan (juristic preference), and (4) the answer was given from two perspectives: one based on the formal ruling and the other on precaution (taqwa). The commentator then critiques this classification, arguing that aside from the first cause (mistaken hearing), the other three actually concern differences in opinions (aqwal), not differences in narrations (riwayat). He supports this by noting that the same narrator, like Imam Muhammad, may transmit both narrations, which contradicts the idea of a hearing error. Therefore, the commentator suggests that only the last two causes are valid for explaining differences in narrations, and these are not contradictory but merely reflect different aspects. The discussion underscores the methodological point that many apparent contradictions in the Hanafi school are not real conflicts but arise from transmission dynamics or different contexts.

Key Terms
اختلاف الرواية (ikhtilaf al-riwaya): Difference in transmitted narrations from a single imam (here Abu Hanifa) due to various transmission-related causes.
اختلاف القول (ikhtilaf al-qawl): Difference in the actual legal opinions (sing. qawl) attributed to Abu Hanifa, often as a result of different ijtihad contexts.
الوجه الأول (al-wajh al-awwal): The first cause listed: the narrator makes a mistake in hearing (غلط في السماع).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, which of the following is the ONLY cause that is specific to ikhtilaf al-riwaya (as opposed to ikhtilaf al-qawl)?
A) The narrator mistook one statement for another.
B) Abu Hanifa retracted an earlier opinion.
C) The ruling was given based on qiyas in one case and istihsan in another.
D) The answer was given from the angle of the ruling versus that of precaution.

Key Takeaway
When confronting conflicting Hanafi narrations, first check whether the narrator is single or multiple: if single, treat the conflict as an ikhtilaf al-qawl, not a transmission error.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate

Five Faces of Ikhtilaf in Riwaya
Classifies reasons for conflicting narrations from Hanafi imams

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify the five faces of ikhtilaf al-riwaya and apply them to determine which narration is a qawl (relied-upon statement) and which is a riwaya (weaker narration) in Hanafi fatwa methodology.

Core Idea
Ikhtilaf al-riwaya has five causes; a mufti must identify the applicable cause to know which narration to adopt as the madhhab's position.

When a mufti encounters two conflicting narrations attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa or his major students, he must determine which one represents the madhhab's relied-upon position. The classical Hanafi work Uqud Rasm al-Mufti classifies the reasons for such conflicts into five categories. This classification is essential for a mufti to properly evaluate reports and assign weight to them. In this lesson, we will examine these five faces, the objection raised by Ibn Amir Hajj, and how the Hanafi tradition resolves it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five faces of ikhtilaf
Read the passage to list the five reasons why two narrations may appear from the same source: error in hearing, retraction of a previous opinion, conflict between qiyas and istihsan, conflict between fatwa and taqwa, and the mujtahid's hesitation due to conflicting evidences.
2
Distinguish ikhtilaf al-riwaya from ikhtilaf al-qawl
Understand that the first face is specific to difference in narration, the second is shared, and the last two (and the fifth) pertain to difference in opinion. This distinction affects how a mufti treats the conflicting reports.
3
Apply the classification to a single-narrator scenario
Consider the case where a single reliable narrator (like Imam Muhammad) transmits both reports. Which faces are plausible? The passage argues that the first two faces are improbable, leaving the last two (and the fifth) as the likely causes.
This step addresses the objection by Ibn Amir Hajj.
4
Recognize the terminological distinction: 'inda vs 'an
Note that when the preponderant view is identified, it is called a qawl and referred to with the preposition 'inda (according to). The weaker report is called a riwaya and is referred to with 'an (from). This terminological convention guides citation in fatwa literature.

وجوه اختلاف الرواية
شرح عقود رسم المفتی جهة المنقول عنه أيضًا ؛ لابتناء الاختلاف فيهما على اختلاف القولين المرويّين، فيكونان من باب واحد (۱) . ويؤيده أن ناقل الروايتين قد يكون واحداً، فإن إحدى الروايتين قد تكون فى كتاب من كتب الأصول والأخرى في كتب النوادر ، بل قد يكون كل منهما في كتب الأصول، والكل من جمع واحد وهو الإمام محمد رحمه الله تعالى، وهذا ينافي الوجه الأول(؟)، ويبعد الوجه الثاني (۳)، فالأظهر الاقتصار على الوجهين الأخيرين،
لا يخفى أن المراد ما فيه روايتان لا يخرج عن أحد هذه الموارد، لا أن كلا مما فيه ذلك يتخرج على كل منهما، وحينئذ لا بأس بعدم اطراد كل في كل ما فيه روايتان ؛ فإن الظاهر أن كل ما فيه روايتان صالح لأحدهما، وهو المطلوب، والله سبحانه أعلم .

فتبصر .

وقد يقال : إن من وجوه الاختلاف أيضا، تردّد المجتهد في الحكم لتعارض الأدلة عنده بلا مرجح ، أو لاختلاف رأيه في مدلول الدليل الواحد، فإن الدليل قد يكون محتملا لوجهين أو أكثر، فيبنى على كل واحد جواباً ، ثم قد يترجح عنده أحدهما ، فينسب إليه، ولهذا تراهم يقولون : "قال أبو حنيفة كذا ، وفي رواية عنه كذا" ، وقد لا يترجح عنده أحدهما، فيستوى رأيه فيها ، ولذا تراهم يحكون عنه في مسألة القولين على وجه يفيد تساويهما امتها
كان دائم الصحبة لم يفارق شيخه مثل الإمام محمد رحمه الله تعالى .
(۱) قوله : لكن هذا إنما يتأتى " أى الاقتصار على الوجهين الأخيرين إنما يتمشى فيما يوجد فيه قياس واستحسان، أو فتوى وتقوى، وما لم يوجد فيه التعارض بينهما يجرى فيه أحد من الوجهين الأولين بشرط تعدد الراوي .

(مظ) وحاصل الجواب عن إيرادى الشارح : أن تقسيم الإمام البليغي شامل لاختلاف القول والرواية، وأعم من أن يجرى في المختلف فيه أحد هذه الوجوه أو الأكثر ولو أردنا أن نفرق بين وجوه اختلاف القول والرواية قلنا : إن الوجه الأول يختص باختلاف الرواية، والثانى مشترك بين اختلاف الرواية والقول والوجهين الأخيرين يختصان باختلاف القول، وكذا الوجه الخامس الذي ذكره الشارح - وهو تردد المجتهد في الحكم ؛ لتعارض الدليلين أو احتمال الدليل معنيين من باب اختلاف القول أيضاً (۳) قوله : "فينسب إليه " أى ينسب الراجح إليه ويبقى المرجوح رواية، وهذا يشير إلى الفرق الآخر بين القول والرواية ، وهو : أن الراجح قول والمرجوح رواية. الأول يذكر بـ " عند" والثاني بـ " عن " .
Faces of Difference in Narration. Commentary on Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: And also from the aspect of what is transmitted from him; because the difference in them [i.e., qawl and riwaya] is built upon the difference of the two transmitted statements, so they are of one type (1). And it is supported that the transmitter of two narrations may be one person, for one of the two narrations may be in a book of the Usool and the other in the books of Nawadir, nay, each may be in the books of Usool, and all are from one compiler, Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him), and this contradicts the first face (?) and makes the second face remote (3), so the apparent view is to restrict [the division] to the last two faces. It is not hidden that what is meant is that what contains two narrations does not exit from one of these resources, not that every such case is derived from each one. Then there is no harm in the lack of universality of each in every case having two narrations; for the apparent is that every case having two narrations is suitable for one of them, and that is the objective. And Allah Most High knows best. Refer to: al-Taqrir wa al-Tahbir 3/425-426, Dar al-Kutub al-Ilmiyya, Beirut. (1) His saying: 'they become of one type' means difference of statement and difference of narration become one species, and no contradiction between them is established based on the last three faces, contrary to what Ibn Amir Hajj claimed. Thus ends the first objection. And his saying: 'And it is supported that the transmitter of two narrations may be one person' is the first premise for the second objection, and its second premise is his saying: 'And this contradicts the first face and makes the second face remote.' The sum of this objection is that these faces do not all apply except when the narrator is multiple; if the narrator is one, then only the last two faces apply. So the researcher must say: the narrator is either one or multiple. If one, then the difference in statements is for two faces: conflict of qiyas and istihsan, and fatwa and taqwa. If multiple, then the difference is from four faces. The answer to this objection has passed and will also come, so be attentive. (2) His saying: 'And this contradicts the first face' i.e., the narrator of two narrations being one contradicts the first face, which is error in hearing; because this face only applies when one person narrates a narration and another narrates another. As for one person narrating two narrations, it is not correct to say that he made an error in hearing in one of them, especially if he is precise and reliable like Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him). (3) His saying: 'And it makes the second face remote' i.e., lack of knowledge of the retraction; because when one person narrates two narrations, it is far-fetched to say that one of them is retracted and the narrator did not know of the retraction, especially if he constantly accompanied his teacher and never left him like Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him). [Page 89] Commentary on Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: Faces of Difference in Narration. But not in every branch where narration differs, rather some of that may be for one and some for the other. But this only applies where qiyas and istihsan, or caution and other things, exist. Yes, the first two faces apply when the narrator differs. And it may be said: Among the faces of difference also is the mujtahid's hesitation in the ruling due to conflict of evidences without a preponderant, or his varying opinion regarding the meaning of a single evidence; for the evidence may be susceptible to two or more interpretations, so a response is built on each one, then one may become preponderant with him, so it is attributed to him. That is why they say: 'Abu Hanifa said such-and-such, and in a narration from him such-and-such.' And if neither preponderates, his opinion is equal regarding them. That is why they relate from him in a matter two statements in a way that indicates their equality… he was constantly in companionship and never separated from his teacher like Imam Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him). (1) His saying: 'But this only applies' i.e., restricting to the last two faces only applies where qiyas and istihsan, or fatwa and taqwa exist; where no conflict between them exists, one of the first two faces applies on condition of multiplicity of narrators. (2) His saying: 'Yes, the first two faces apply' i.e., error in hearing and that he had a statement then retracted it. (Mazhar) The sum of the answer to the commentator's objection: that the division of Imam al-Balighi includes difference of statement and narration, and is general in that either one or more of these faces applies to the disputed matter. If we wished to distinguish between the faces of difference of statement and narration, we say: the first face is specific to difference of narration, the second is shared between difference of narration and statement, and the last two faces are specific to difference of statement. Likewise the fifth face which the commentator mentioned – i.e., the mujtahid's hesitation in the ruling due to conflicting evidences or the evidence carrying two meanings – is also from the category of difference of statement. (3) His saying: 'so it is attributed to him' i.e., the preponderant is attributed to him and the preponderated remains as a narration. This points to the other difference between statement and narration: that the preponderant is a statement and the preponderated is a narration. The first is mentioned with 'inda (according to) and the second with 'an (from).
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti addresses the classification of reasons (wujuh) for conflicting narrations (riwayat) attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa and his major students, particularly Imam Muhammad. The discussion is fundamental for a mufti to determine which narration to rely upon in fatwa. The text first presents a five-fold division of why two narrations may exist from a single imam: (1) error in hearing, (2) a previous opinion later retracted, (3) conflict between qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference), (4) conflict between fatwa (legal ruling) and taqwa (piety), and (5) the mujtahid's own hesitation when evidences conflict without a clear preponderance. The commentator addresses an objection by Ibn Amir Hajj that this classification is problematic when a single reliable narrator (like Imam Muhammad) transmits both conflicting reports; in such a case, the first two faces (error and retraction) are improbable. The solution offered is that the classification is general: each case of ikhtilaf may be explained by one of these faces, not necessarily all five. The discussion also distinguishes between ikhtilaf al-riwaya (difference in narration) and ikhtilaf al-qawl (difference in opinion). The key practical takeaway: when a preponderant opinion exists within the madhhab, it is considered a 'qawl' (statement) and is used in fatwa; the weaker report is called a 'riwaya' (narration). This distinction is marked terminologically: 'عند' (according to) indicates a qawl, while 'عن' (from) indicates a riwaya.

Key Terms
اختلاف الرواية (ikhtilaf al-riwaya): Conflict in transmitted reports from an imam, where two different rulings are narrated from the same source.
قول (qawl): A statement or opinion of the imam that is the preponderant and relied-upon view in the madhhab.
راوي (rawi): The transmitter/narrator who reports the statements or rulings of the imam.
الغلط في السماع (ghalat al-sama'): Error in hearing – the narrator misheard the ruling, leading to a flawed transmission.
تردد المجتهد (taraddud al-mujtahid): The mujtahid's hesitation in giving a ruling due to conflicting evidences or multiple possible interpretations of a single evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which face of ikhtilaf is specific to difference in narration (ikhtilaf al-riwaya) and does NOT apply to difference in statement (ikhtilaf al-qawl)?
A) Error in hearing (ghalat al-sama')
B) Conflict between qiyas and istihsan
C) Conflict between fatwa and taqwa
D) The mujtahid's hesitation

Key Takeaway
The five faces of ikhtilaf al-riwaya provide a systematic framework for a mufti to analyze conflicting reports; each case fits one face, and the preponderant view becomes the qawl for fatwa.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
advanced
8 min
Distinguishing Qawl from Riwaya and Attributing Two Opinions
Expanding causes of ikhtilaf and rule for attributing two qawls

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between qawl (preponderant opinion) and riwaya (non-preponderant narration) in Hanafi usul and apply the rule for attributing two contradictory opinions to a mujtahid when evidence is equal.

Core Idea
The preponderant is the qawl; the non-preponderant is the riwaya; only when evidence is equal may both be attributed to the mujtahid.

A mufti often encounters multiple narrations from a single mujtahid on the same issue. Understanding the difference between a qawl (statement) and a riwaya (narration) is essential for correct fatwa practice. This lesson clarifies that the preponderant view becomes the qawl, while the weaker remains a riwaya. It also addresses the rare case where a mujtahid hesitates due to equal evidence—both opinions may then be attributed to him.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Five Faces of Variance
List the five causes of difference between qawl and riwaya: error in hearing, retraction, qiyas/istihsan, ihtiyat, and hesitation due to conflicting proofs.
The first two belong to riwaya; the middle two are shared; the last belongs to qawl.
2
Classify Each Face as Qawl or Riwaya
Assign each face to either qawl (preponderant, definitive) or riwaya (non-preponderant, transmitted). This classification determines how the ruling is attributed.
For example, error in hearing is a riwaya issue, while hesitation is a qawl issue.
3
Distinguish Between Qawl and Riwaya by Tarjih
The preponderant (rajih) after tarjih becomes the qawl; the non-preponderant (marjuh) remains a riwaya. The qawl is introduced by 'inda (at) and the riwaya by 'an (from).
This directly impacts fatwa: the mufti must follow the qawl unless tarjih is impossible.
4
Understand the Rule for Equal Evidence
When a mujtahid faces truly equal evidence and cannot tarjih, both opinions are equally attributed to him. The mufti may then choose either.
This is the exception that allows two contradictory opinions to be attributed to a single mujtahid.
5
Apply the Rule to Fatwa Practice
In issuing a fatwa, the mufti must first determine which opinion is rajih. If none is rajih due to equal evidence, he may adopt either and attribute it to the mujtahid.
This prevents the mufti from rejecting a valid report or assuming the madhhab is inconsistent.

وجوه اختلاف الرواية لكن لا في كل فرع اختلفت فيه الرواية، بل بعض ذلك قد يكون لأحدهما، والبعض الآخر للآخر . لكن هذا إنما يتأتى) فيما يصلح أن يكون فيه قياس واستحسان، أو احتياط وغيره، نعم يتأتى الوجهان الأولان (۳) فيما إذا اختلف الراوي .
وقد يقال : إن من وجوه الاختلاف أيضا، تردّد المجتهد في الحكم لتعارض الأدلة عنده بلا مرجح ، أو لاختلاف رأيه في مدلول الدليل الواحد، فإن الدليل قد يكون محتملا لوجهين أو أكثر، فيبنى على كل واحد جواباً ، ثم قد يترجح عنده أحدهما ، فينسب إليه، ولهذا تراهم يقولون : "قال أبو حنيفة كذا ، وفي رواية عنه كذا" ، وقد لا يترجح عنده أحدهما، فيستوى رأيه فيها ، ولذا تراهم يحكون عنه في مسألة القولين على وجه يفيد تساويهما امتها [?] كان دائم الصحبة لم يفارق شيخه مثل الإمام محمد رحمه الله تعالى .

(مظ) وحاصل الجواب عن إيرادى الشارح : أن تقسيم الإمام البليغي شامل لاختلاف القول والرواية، وأعم من أن يجرى في المختلف فيه أحد هذه الوجوه أو الأكثر ولو أردنا أن نفرق بين وجوه اختلاف القول والرواية قلنا : إن الوجه الأول يختص باختلاف الرواية، والثانى مشترك بين اختلاف الرواية والقول والوجهين الأخيرين يختصان باختلاف القول، وكذا الوجه الخامس الذي ذكره الشارح - وهو تردد المجتهد في الحكم ؛ لتعارض الدليلين أو احتمال الدليل معنيين من باب اختلاف القول أيضاً
فينسب إليه أى ينسب الراجح إليه ويبقى المرجوح رواية، وهذا يشير إلى الفرق الآخر بين القول والرواية ، وهو : أن الراجح قول والمرجوح رواية. الأول يذكر بـ " عند" والثاني بـ " عن " .
نسبة القولين إلى المجتهد
وقد قدمنا عن الإمام القرافي : أنه لا يحل الحكم والإفتاء بغير الراجح لمجتهد أو مقلد، إلا إذا تعارضت الأدلة عند المجتهد، وعجز عن الترجيح
أي فإن له الحكم بأيهما شاء ؛ لتساويهما عنده وعلى هذا) فيصح نسبة كل من القولين إليه، لا كما يقوله بعض الأصوليين من أنه لا ينسب إليه شيء منهما، وما يقوله بعضهم من اعتقاد أن الأصوليين اختلفوا في نسبة الأقوال المتعددة إلى المجتهد على ثلاثة مذاهب الأول : يصح نسبة كل من القولين إليه، ويحمل على تردّده ؛ لتعادل الدليلين عنده . الثاني : لا يصح نسبة شيء منهما إليه ذكره الإمام الرازي وأتباعه، وفائدة القولين حينئذ : أن لا يتوهم من أراد من المجتهدين الذهاب إلى أحدهما أنه خارق للإجماع، وقيل : التنبيه على أن ما سواهما لا يؤخذ به فيطلب ترجيح أحدهما . الثالث : يجب اعتقاد نسبة أحدهما إليه والرجوع عن الآخر غير عين، دون نسبتهما جميعا، ويمتنع العمل بهما حينئذ حتى يتبين كالنصين إذا علمنا نسخ أحدهما غير معين، وهذا قول الآمدي والشارح رجّح القول الأول ونفى الأخيرين كما ترى . راجع التقرير والتحبير : ٤٢٦/٣ ، والإحكام للآمدي : ٤٢٧/٤ ، وأصول الفقه للزحيلي: ۰۱۱۱۲/۲
The faces of narration differences: but not in every branch where the narration differs; rather some of that may be for one of them, and the other part for the other. But this only occurs in what is suitable for qiyas and istihsan, or ihtiyat and other. Yes, the first two faces occur when the narrator differs. And it may be said that among the faces of difference also is the mujtahid's hesitation in the ruling due to conflicting evidences without a preponderant, or the difference of his view in the indication of a single proof. For the proof may be probable for two or more faces, so on each one a response is built. Then sometimes one becomes preponderant with him, so it is attributed to him. For this reason you see them saying: 'Abu Hanifa said such, and in a narration from him such.' And sometimes neither preponderates with him, so his view is equal regarding them. So you see them narrating from him in a matter two statements in a way that indicates their equality, except [?] that he was always in companionship and never left his shaykh like Imam Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on him. Yes, the first two faces occur: error in hearing, and that he had a statement and then returned from it. The summary of the answer to the commentator's objection: that the classification of Imam al-Balighi encompasses difference of statement and narration, and is more general than that in the differed matter one of these faces or many occur. Even if we wanted to distinguish between the faces of difference of statement and narration, we say: the first face is specific to difference of narration, the second is shared between difference of narration and statement, and the last two faces are specific to difference of statement. Likewise the fifth face mentioned by the commentator—i.e., the mujtahid's hesitation in the ruling due to conflicting evidences or the indication of a proof having two meanings—is also from the category of difference of statement. 'Then it is attributed to him' means the preponderant is attributed to him and the non-preponderant remains a narration. This points to another difference between statement and narration: that the preponderant is a statement and the non-preponderant is a narration. The first is mentioned with 'inda' and the second with 'an'. Attribution of two statements to the mujtahid: And we have previously presented from Imam al-Qarafi: that it is not permissible for a mujtahid or a muqallid to issue a ruling or fatwa with other than the preponderant, except when the evidences conflict for the mujtahid and he is incapable of tarjih. That is, he may rule with whichever he wishes because they are equal in his view. And on this, it is correct to attribute each of the two statements to him, not as some usuliyyun say that nothing of them is attributed to him. And what some say about belief that the usuliyyun differed regarding attribution of multiple statements to the mujtahid into three schools: first, it is correct to attribute each of the two statements to him, and it is interpreted as his hesitation due to equal proofs. Second, it is not correct to attribute either to him; this is mentioned by Imam al-Razi and his followers. The benefit of the two statements then is that one who wishes from among the mujtahids to adopt one of them should not be thought to be breaking consensus, and it is said: a warning that what is other than them is not to be taken, so one seeks tarjih of one of them. Third, it is necessary to believe attribution of one of them to him and that he has returned from the other without specification, without attributing both together; action upon them both is then forbidden until it becomes clear, like two texts when we know one is abrogated without specification. This is the view of al-Amadi. The commentator preponderated the first view and negated the last two as you see. Refer to al-Taqrir wa al-Tahbir 3/426, al-Ihkam by al-Amadi 4/427, and Usul al-Fiqh by al-Zuhayli 2/1112.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage, from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti, discusses the classification of differences between qawl (statement/opinion) and riwaya (narration/transmitted report) and the rules for attributing contradictory opinions to a mujtahid. The author first explains that the five causes of variance (error in hearing, retraction, qiyas/istihsan, ihtiyat, and hesitation due to conflicting proofs) apply differently to qawl and riwaya: the first two (error and retraction) belong to riwaya, the middle (qiyas/istihsan and ihtiyat) are shared, and the last (hesitation) belongs to qawl. He then distinguishes between them by stating that the preponderant view (rajih) is called qawl, while the non-preponderant (marjuh) is called riwaya; thus one says 'at Abu Hanifa' (with 'inda) for the rajih, and 'from him' (with 'an) for the marjuh. The passage then addresses the issue of a mujtahid having two contradictory opinions. Citing Imam al-Qarafi, it clarifies that one cannot issue a fatwa with a non-rajih opinion unless the evidences are truly equal and tarjih is impossible. In that case, both opinions are equally attributable to the mujtahid. The author rejects the views of some usuliyyun who deny such attribution or require belief in abrogation of one without specification. The preferred Hanafi position is that both opinions are validly attributed, reflecting the mujtahid's genuine hesitation.

Key Terms
الراجح (al-rajih): The preponderant view after weighing evidence; attributed as qawl.
المرجوح (al-marjuh): The non-preponderant view; attributed as riwaya.
القول (al-qawl): The direct statement of the mujtahid after tarjih; introduced by 'inda.
الرواية (al-riwaya): A transmitted report from the mujtahid that is not his final preponderant view; introduced by 'an.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the difference between a qawl (statement) and a riwaya (narration) from a mujtahid?
A) Qawl is the weaker opinion, riwaya is the stronger.
B) Qawl is the preponderant opinion (rajih), riwaya is the non-preponderant (marjuh).
C) Qawl is transmitted by a single narrator, riwaya by multiple.
D) Qawl is based on ijtihad, riwaya on taqlid.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the preponderant opinion is the qawl, attributed with 'inda; the weaker is the riwaya, attributed with 'an. Only when evidence is equal can both be fully attributed to the mujtahid.

Attribution of Multiple Opinions to the Mujtahid
Ruling on attributing two opinions to a mujtahid despite retraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rasm rule that a mujtahid's retracted opinion remains attributable, and identify the conditions under which both opinions may be cited in fatwa.

Core Idea
Retraction does not erase a mujtahid's earlier opinion; both remain attributable despite ruju'.

This passage addresses a critical aspect of mufti hierarchy: when a mujtahid has expressed two opinions and later retracts one, can the retracted opinion still be attributed to him? The Hanafi position, grounded in the principle that retraction does not remove past disagreement, affirms that both opinions remain attributable. This rule has direct implications for muftis who must navigate conflicting reports from a single authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main ruling
What is the rule being established? It is that both opinions can be attributed to the mujtahid even after complete retraction.
2
Examine the dalil
What proof is given? The proof is that retraction does not remove the earlier disagreement; the historical fact of the opinion remains.
Also, later consensus does not remove earlier disagreement.
3
Contrast with opposing views
What do some usuliyun say? They deny attribution entirely or require specification of which is retracted.
Imam al-Qarafi's rule about preponderance is also presented.
4
Apply the nuances
What if one opinion is preponderant? The preponderant is his current opinion; the other is a narration. If he completely abandons the other, it is no longer his opinion but still attributable as a past report.
This preserves the historical record.
5
Trace the thamarah
What is the practical consequence for a mufti? He may cite both opinions unless there is consensus on one, giving flexibility but requiring care.
Fatwa must follow preponderant unless evidence is equal.

Source Text

مطلب
يصح نسبة القولين إلى المجتهد ولو رجع أحدهما بالكلية؛ لعدم رفع الخلاف بالرجوع عنـه.
وقد قدمنا عن الإمام القرافي: أنه لا يحل الحكم والإفتاء بغير الراجح لمجتهد أو مقلد، إلا إذا تعارضت الأدلة عند المجتهد، وعجز عن الترجيح، أي فإن له الحكم بأيهما شاء؛ لتساويهما عنده. وعلى هذا فيصح نسبة كل من القولين إليه، لا كما يقوله بعض الأصوليين من أنه لا ينسب إليه شيء منهما، وما يقوله بعضهم من اعتقاد نسبة أحدهما إليه والرجوع عن الآخر غير معين، دون نسبتهما جميعا، ويمتنع العمل بهما حينئذ حتى يتبين كالنصين إذا علمنا نسخ أحدهما غير معين.
نعم إذا ترجح عنده أحدهما مع عدم إعراضه عن الآخر ورجوعه عنه، ينسب إليه الراجح عنده ويذكر الثانى رواية عنه، أما لو أعرض عن الآخر بالكلية، لم يبق قولا له، بل يكون قوله هو الراجح فقط، لكن لا يرتفع الخلاف في المسألة بعد الرجوع، كما قاله بعض الشافعية، وأيده بعضهم بأن أهل عصر إذا أجمعوا على قول بعد اختلافهم، فقد حكى الأصوليون قولين في ارتفاع الخلاف السابق، فما لم يقع فيه إجماع أولى.
Topic: It is valid to attribute two opinions to the mujtahid even if he retracted one completely, because disagreement is not removed by retraction. And we have previously mentioned from Imam al-Qarafi: It is not permissible for a mujtahid or muqallid to give a ruling or fatwa with other than the preponderant opinion, except when evidences are contradictory and he is unable to weigh them, then he may rule with whichever he wishes because they are equal in his view. On this basis, it is valid to attribute each of the two opinions to him, not as some usuliyun say that nothing is attributed to him, nor what some say that one must believe that one opinion is attributed to him and the other retracted in an unspecified manner, without attributing both, and acting on both is prohibited until clarification, like two texts when we know one is abrogated but not specified. Indeed, if one opinion becomes preponderant in his view with no abandonment of the other and retraction from it, the preponderant is attributed to him and the second is mentioned as a narration from him. But if he completely abandons the other, it is no longer his opinion; rather his opinion is only the preponderant. However, the disagreement in the issue does not cease after retraction, as some Shafi'is said, and some supported it by saying: If the people of an era agree on an opinion after their disagreement, the usuliyun have narrated two opinions on whether the previous disagreement is removed. So if no consensus occurs, it is more fitting that disagreement is not removed.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a rasm rule concerning the attribution of multiple opinions to a mujtahid. The author establishes that it is permissible to attribute both opinions to the mujtahid even if he later completely retracts one. The reason is that retraction does not erase the fact that he once held that opinion, nor does it remove the earlier disagreement. The passage begins by citing Imam al-Qarafi's rule that a mujtahid or muqallid must rule by the preponderant opinion unless the evidences are equal, in which case he may choose. This serves as context: when both opinions are equal in his view, attributing both is valid. The author then refutes two other positions: first, that of some usuliyun who say nothing is attributed to him; second, that one must believe one of the opinions is his and the other is retracted but unspecified. The author endorses the position that both are attributed. He then clarifies the case when one opinion is preponderant: the preponderant is clearly his opinion, and the other is reported as a narration from him. Even if he completely abandons the weaker opinion, that opinion does not cease to be attributable to him, because the fact that he previously held it is not nullified. The disagreement in the issue remains, as the disagreement of earlier scholars is not lifted by later agreement, as analogized from the case of consensus after disagreement. This is supported by Shafi'i scholars.

Key Terms
قول (qawl): An opinion or ruling issued by a mujtahid on a legal question.
رجوع (ruju'): Retraction of a previous opinion by the mujtahid.
ترجيح (tarjih): Preponderance of one opinion over another based on evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when does it become permissible to attribute both opinions to a mujtahid even after he retracts one?
A) Only when the retraction is explicit and specified.
B) Always, because retraction does not remove the earlier disagreement.
C) Never, because retraction nullifies the earlier opinion.
D) Only if the mujtahid's two opinions are exactly equal in strength.

Key Takeaway
A mujtahid's retraction does not nullify the attribution of his earlier opinion; both opinions may be cited, but fatwa must follow the preponderant unless evidence is equal.

Attributing Two Opinions to a Mujtahid After Retraction
Rules for attributing statements to a mujtahid after retraction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of attribution to determine whether a retracted opinion remains a valid qawl of a mujtahid.

Core Idea
A mujtahid can be attributed two opinions even after retraction; retraction does not nullify the earlier statement.

This lesson examines how Hanafi usul treats multiple statements (aqwal) from a single mujtahid. When a mujtahid issues two conflicting opinions and later retracts one, does the earlier opinion still belong to him? The answer depends on whether he completely disavowed it or merely preferred the other. Understanding this rule is essential for a mufti when citing positions from earlier imams.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The mujtahid may have two opinions that are equal in his view, or one becomes preponderant without complete disavowal, or he completely retracts and disavows the other.
Each scenario affects attribution differently.
2
Apply the rule of attribution
In the first two scenarios, both opinions can be attributed. In the third scenario, the retracted opinion can still be attributed because retraction does not nullify the earlier statement.
The analogy with later consensus (ijma') not removing earlier disagreement supports this.
3
Analyze the evidence
The author argues from the principle that later consensus does not remove earlier disagreement; if even consensus cannot remove it, a simple retraction is even less effective.
This is a rational proof (talil).
4
Apply to a mufti's practice
When a mufti encounters conflicting narrations from an imam, he must check if one was retracted and how. If retracted without disavowal, both remain valid; if completely disavowed, the earlier is still a narration but not an active opinion.
This helps in choosing the mufta bihi position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نسبة القولين إلى المجتهد

نسبة أحدهما إليه ؛ لأن رجوعه (1) عن الآخر غير معين ؛ إذ الفرض تساويهما في رأيه، وعدم ترجح أحدهما على الآخر.

نعم إذا ترجح عنده (2) أحدهما مع عدم إعراضه عن الآخر ورجوعه عنه ، ينسب إليه الراجح عنده ويذكر الثانى رواية عنه، أما لو أعرض عن الآخر بالكلية، لم يبق قولا له، بل يكون قوله هو الراجح فقط ، لكن لا يرتفع الخلاف في المسألة بعد الرجوع (3) ، كما قاله بعض الشافعية، وأيده بعضهم بأن أهل عصر إذا أجمعوا على قول بعد اختلافهم، فقد حكى الأصوليون قولين (4) في ارتفاع الخلاف السابق ) ، فما لم يقع فيه إجماع أولى .

(1) قوله : "لأن رجوعه عن الآخر " دليل لنفى القول الثالث .
(2) قوله : نعم إذا ترجح عنده " اعلم أن القولين إذا صدرا عن المجتهد، فإما أن يتساويا عنده ولم يرجح أحدهما، أو رجّح أحدهما لكن لم يعرض عن الآخر بالكلية، أو أعرض عن الآخر بالكلية ورجع عنه، وعلى كل من التقديرات يصح نسبة كل من القولين إليه على ما حققه الشارح، أما على الأولين فظاهر، وأما على الثالث فلأن الرجوع لا يُعدم القول السابق ولا يجعله ساقط الاعتبار، فلا يكون المرجوع عنه ساقط النسبة بالكلية؛ لما تقرر أن الإجماع اللاحق لا يرفع الخلاف السابق، فما لم يقع فيه إجماع أولى أن لا يرتفع فيه الخلاف السابق.
(3) قوله : "لكن لا يرتفع الخلاف في المسألة بعد الرجوع فإذا لم يرتفع ، يصح نسبة القول المرجوع عنه إلى المجتهد، ولو بعد ما أعرض عنه بالكلية، وهذا هو المطلوب.
(4) قوله : "قولين في ارتفاع الخلاف السابق " قال في "البحر " : وهو مبنى على أن الإجماع المتأخر هل يرفع الخلاف المتقدم؟ فعندهما لا يرفع ؛ لما فيه من تضليل بعض الصحابة رضى الله عنهم. (البحر الرائق، كتاب العتق باب الاستيلاد : ٤٥٣/٤) . وقال في " تسهيل الوصول إلى علم الأصول" : "اختلف القائلون بحجية إجماع من بعد الصحابة في شرط، وهو عدم الاختلاف السابق للإجماع اللاحق، مثاله : أن يختلف أهل عصر في مسألة ، واستقر خلافهم بحيث صار أحد القولين مذهبا لبعض والآخر مذهبا لغيره كبيع أم الولد فإنه لا يجوز عند عمر رضي الله عنه ويجوز عند على رضى الله عنه ، فهذا الخلاف هل يمنع انعقاد الإجماع في العصر الذي بعده؟ ذهب أكثر الشافعية إلى أنه يمنع، وتبقى المسألة مجتهداً فيها كما كانت ، لا مجمعاً عليها . واختلف مشايخنا في ذلك فقال أكثرهم - وهو الأصح - إنه لا يمنع ويرتفع الخلاف بالإجماع، وتكون المسألة مجمعا عليها عند علمائنا الثلاثة؛ لأن المعتبر اتفاق مجتهدى العصر وقد وجد . وقيل : فيه اختلاف بين علمائنا الثلاثة، فعند أبي حنيفة يمنع الخلاف السابق انعقاد الإجماع اللاحق، وعند محمد لا يمنع وأبويوسف مع أبي حنيفة في رواية ومع محمد في رواية" . (١٧٠-١٧١) : المكتبة الصديقية (ملتان)

(1) قوله : "لكن ما ذكر في كتب الأصول " استدراك على ما ذكر من صحة نسبة القولين إلى المجتهد، ولو كان رجع عن أحدهما، وحاصله : أن المذكور في كتب الأصول خلاف ذلك ؛ فإنهم قالوا لا يمكن أن يكون للمجتهد قولان في زمان واحد ؛ لأن اختلاف القولين إنما يكون لتعارض الأدلة عنده، وما من تعارض إلا وله مخلص، إما حديث أو قول الصحابة أو القياس أو التحرى . فإذا وجد المخلص لكل تعارض لا يمكن أن يكون له قولان في مسألة واحدة في زمان واحد . نعم يمكن هذا عند الشافعي ؛ فإن عنده يوجد المخلص لكل تعارض إلا تعارض القياسين ؛ فإنهما إذا تعارضا لا يصار عنده إلى التحرى كما هو مذهبنا ، بل يعمل المجتهد بأيهما شاء من غير تحر، فيمكن أن يكون له قولان أو أكثر في مسألة واحدة في زمان واحد، أما عندنا فيصار عند تعارض القياسين إلى التحرى، فلا يمكن تعدد الأقوال لأصحابنا في وقت واحد . وما نقل فيه روايتان عنهم، فمحمول على اختلاف الزمان وعدم
Attribution of two statements to the mujtahid

Attribution of one of them to him is valid, because his retraction (1) from the other is not definite, since the assumption is that both are equal in his view, and neither is preponderant over the other.

Yes, if one of them becomes preponderant in his view (2) without his completely turning away from the other and retracting it, then the preponderant one is attributed to him, and the second is mentioned as a narration from him. But if he completely turns away from the other, then it is no longer a statement of his; rather, his statement is only the preponderant one. However, the disagreement in the issue does not disappear after retraction (3), as some Shafi'is stated, and some of them supported it: that if the people of an era agree on a statement after their disagreement, the usuliyyun have reported two views (4) regarding the removal of the previous disagreement. So if consensus does not remove it, then what is less than consensus is more appropriate.

(1) His saying: 'because his retraction from the other' is evidence for negating a third statement.
(2) His saying: 'Yes, if one becomes preponderant in his view': Know that when two statements issue from a mujtahid, either they are equal in his view and he does not deem one preponderant, or he deems one preponderant but does not completely turn away from the other, or he completely turns away from the other and retracts it. In all these cases, it is valid to attribute each of the two statements to him, according to what the commentator (sharh) has verified. As for the first two cases, it is apparent. As for the third case, because retraction does not nullify the previous statement nor render it devoid of consideration, so the retracted statement is not completely severed from attribution, based on the established principle that later consensus does not remove the previous disagreement. So if consensus does not remove it, then what is less than consensus is more appropriate that the previous disagreement is not removed.
(3) His saying: 'However, the disagreement in the issue does not disappear after retraction': Since it is not removed, it is valid to attribute the retracted statement to the mujtahid, even after he completely turned away from it. This is the desired point.
(4) His saying: 'two views regarding the removal of the previous disagreement': He said in al-Bahr: 'This is based on whether later consensus removes the earlier disagreement. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it does not remove it, because it would entail declaring some Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) to be in error.' (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Kitab al-'Itq, Bab al-Istilad: 4/453). And in Tashil al-Wusul ila 'Ilm al-Usul: 'Those who uphold the authority of consensus after the Companions differed over a condition, namely the absence of previous disagreement for the later consensus. Example: the people of an era differed over an issue, and their disagreement became established such that one of the two statements became the madhhab of some and the other the madhhab of others, like the sale of umm al-walad: it is not permitted according to 'Umar (RA) and is permitted according to 'Ali (RA). Does this disagreement prevent the formation of consensus in the subsequent era? The majority of Shafi'is held that it does prevent, and the issue remains one of ijtihad as it was, not a matter of consensus. Our scholars (mashayikhuna) differed on this; most of them—and this is the correct view—said it does not prevent and the disagreement is removed by consensus, and the issue becomes a matter of consensus according to our three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), because the agreement of the mujtahids of the era is considered, and it has occurred. It is also said there is disagreement among our three imams: according to Abu Hanifa, the previous disagreement prevents the formation of later consensus; according to Muhammad, it does not prevent; and Abu Yusuf is with Abu Hanifa in one narration and with Muhammad in another.' (170-171: al-Maktaba al-Siddiqiyya, Multan)

(1) His saying: 'But what is mentioned in the books of usul' is a corrective to what was mentioned regarding the validity of attributing both statements to the mujtahid, even if he retracted one. The summary: what is mentioned in the books of usul is contrary to that, for they said it is not possible for a mujtahid to have two statements at one time, because the difference of two statements arises only from the conflict of evidences according to him, and there is no conflict except that it has a resolution, either a hadith, a saying of a Companion, analogy, or tahari (preference). So when a resolution exists for every conflict, it is not possible for him to have two statements in one issue at one time. Yes, this is possible according to al-Shafi'i, because according to him there is a resolution for every conflict except the conflict of two analogies; when they conflict, according to him one does not resort to tahari as in our madhhab, but the mujtahid acts on whichever he wishes without tahari, so it is possible for him to have two or more statements in one issue at one time. According to us, when two analogies conflict, one resorts to tahari, so multiplicity of statements for our scholars is not possible at one time. What is reported as two narrations from them is interpreted as due to difference in time and the absence of
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage, from a commentary on Rasm al-Mufti, addresses two interrelated issues: the attribution of two contradictory opinions (qawl) to a mujtahid, and the possibility of a mujtahid having multiple opinions simultaneously. The author begins by establishing the rule that a mujtahid can be attributed two opinions even if he has retracted one, because the retraction does not definitively cancel the earlier opinion unless he completely disavows it. This is analogous to the principle that a later consensus (ijma') does not necessarily remove an earlier disagreement (khilaf). The passage then delves into a more theoretical discussion: according to Hanafi usul, a mujtahid cannot have two contradictory opinions at the same time because every conflict of evidence has a resolution (through stronger proof, preference, etc.). However, Imam al-Shafi'i permits this in cases of conflicting analogies (qiyasayn), where he allows the mujtahid to choose either without preference (tahari). The Hanafi position is that such choice is not allowed; instead, the mujtahid must seek preference, thus eliminating the possibility of multiple simultaneous opinions. Any apparent multiple narrations from a single Hanafi imam are attributed to different time periods or circumstances. The passage also covers a dispute among the three Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) regarding whether earlier disagreement prevents later consensus. Abu Hanifa holds that it prevents, Muhammad holds it does not, and Abu Yusuf has two narrations. The majority of later Hanafi scholars adopt the view that later consensus does remove earlier disagreement, as it is the agreement of the current mujtahids that matters.

Key Terms
قَوْل (qawl): A legal opinion or statement issued by a mujtahid on a particular issue.
مُجْتَهِد (mujtahid): A jurist qualified to derive rulings from primary sources (Quran, Sunnah, consensus, analogy).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which scenario is it NOT possible to attribute a retracted opinion to the mujtahid?
A) When both opinions are equal in his view.
B) When one becomes preponderant but he does not completely disavow the other.
C) When he completely turns away from the other and retracts it.
D) In all scenarios, it is possible to attribute both opinions.

Key Takeaway
A mujtahid can be attributed two opinions even after retraction; the earlier opinion remains a valid qawl for attribution.

Later Consensus and the Possibility of Multiple Opinions
Dispute on later consensus and possibility of multiple opinions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi stance on whether later consensus removes earlier disagreement and evaluate the possibility of a mujtahid having multiple contradictory opinions.

Core Idea
Later consensus can remove previous disagreement according to the majority of Hanafis, but a mujtahid cannot have two contradictory opinions at the same time in Hanafi usul.

This lesson delves into two connected issues: does a later consensus (ijma') overrule an earlier disagreement (khilaf), and can a mujtahid hold two contradictory opinions simultaneously? The passage presents the disagreements among the three Hanafi imams and contrasts the Hanafi view with that of al-Shafi'i. Understanding these principles is crucial for a mufti when dealing with seemingly conflicting fatwas from the same imam.

Guided Path
1
State the problem of later consensus and earlier disagreement
When earlier mujtahids differed on an issue, and later mujtahids all agree on one view, does that consensus remove the earlier disagreement?
This affects whether the earlier differing opinion remains valid for fatwa.
2
Identify the positions of the three Hanafi imams
Abu Hanifa holds that later consensus is prevented by earlier disagreement. Muhammad holds it does not prevent. Abu Yusuf has two narrations.
The majority of later Hanafi mashayikh adopt Muhammad's view.
3
Analyze the proof for the majority view
They argue that the agreement of mujtahids of the current era constitutes ijma', and this ijma' removes the earlier disagreement because the earlier opinions are no longer considered valid evidence. It does not imply the earlier scholars were in error; their ijtihad was valid in their time.
This is a rational proof balancing respect for earlier scholars and the authority of consensus.
4
Address the possibility of multiple simultaneous opinions
According to Hanafi usul, a mujtahid cannot have two contradictory opinions at the same time because every conflict of evidence has a resolution (tahari or preference). Multiple opinions attributed to one imam are due to different times or circumstances.
Al-Shafi'i allows multiple opinions in cases of conflicting analogies (qiyasayn).
5
Apply to mufti's practice
If a later consensus has formed on an issue, the earlier differing opinion is no longer a valid basis for fatwa. If two opinions from one imam appear contradictory, the mufti must understand they were from different contexts, not simultaneous.
This prevents misapplication of earlier rulings.
