نسبة القولين إلى المجتهد

نسبة أحدهما إليه ؛ لأن رجوعه (1) عن الآخر غير معين ؛ إذ الفرض تساويهما في رأيه، وعدم ترجح أحدهما على الآخر.

نعم إذا ترجح عنده (2) أحدهما مع عدم إعراضه عن الآخر ورجوعه عنه ، ينسب إليه الراجح عنده ويذكر الثانى رواية عنه، أما لو أعرض عن الآخر بالكلية، لم يبق قولا له، بل يكون قوله هو الراجح فقط ، لكن لا يرتفع الخلاف في المسألة بعد الرجوع (3) ، كما قاله بعض الشافعية، وأيده بعضهم بأن أهل عصر إذا أجمعوا على قول بعد اختلافهم، فقد حكى الأصوليون قولين (4) في ارتفاع الخلاف السابق ) ، فما لم يقع فيه إجماع أولى .

(1) قوله : "لأن رجوعه عن الآخر " دليل لنفى القول الثالث .
(2) قوله : نعم إذا ترجح عنده " اعلم أن القولين إذا صدرا عن المجتهد، فإما أن يتساويا عنده ولم يرجح أحدهما، أو رجّح أحدهما لكن لم يعرض عن الآخر بالكلية، أو أعرض عن الآخر بالكلية ورجع عنه، وعلى كل من التقديرات يصح نسبة كل من القولين إليه على ما حققه الشارح، أما على الأولين فظاهر، وأما على الثالث فلأن الرجوع لا يُعدم القول السابق ولا يجعله ساقط الاعتبار، فلا يكون المرجوع عنه ساقط النسبة بالكلية؛ لما تقرر أن الإجماع اللاحق لا يرفع الخلاف السابق، فما لم يقع فيه إجماع أولى أن لا يرتفع فيه الخلاف السابق.
(3) قوله : "لكن لا يرتفع الخلاف في المسألة بعد الرجوع فإذا لم يرتفع ، يصح نسبة القول المرجوع عنه إلى المجتهد، ولو بعد ما أعرض عنه بالكلية، وهذا هو المطلوب.
(4) قوله : "قولين في ارتفاع الخلاف السابق " قال في "البحر " : وهو مبنى على أن الإجماع المتأخر هل يرفع الخلاف المتقدم؟ فعندهما لا يرفع ؛ لما فيه من تضليل بعض الصحابة رضى الله عنهم. (البحر الرائق، كتاب العتق باب الاستيلاد : ٤٥٣/٤) . وقال في " تسهيل الوصول إلى علم الأصول" : "اختلف القائلون بحجية إجماع من بعد الصحابة في شرط، وهو عدم الاختلاف السابق للإجماع اللاحق، مثاله : أن يختلف أهل عصر في مسألة ، واستقر خلافهم بحيث صار أحد القولين مذهبا لبعض والآخر مذهبا لغيره كبيع أم الولد فإنه لا يجوز عند عمر رضي الله عنه ويجوز عند على رضى الله عنه ، فهذا الخلاف هل يمنع انعقاد الإجماع في العصر الذي بعده؟ ذهب أكثر الشافعية إلى أنه يمنع، وتبقى المسألة مجتهداً فيها كما كانت ، لا مجمعاً عليها . واختلف مشايخنا في ذلك فقال أكثرهم - وهو الأصح - إنه لا يمنع ويرتفع الخلاف بالإجماع، وتكون المسألة مجمعا عليها عند علمائنا الثلاثة؛ لأن المعتبر اتفاق مجتهدى العصر وقد وجد . وقيل : فيه اختلاف بين علمائنا الثلاثة، فعند أبي حنيفة يمنع الخلاف السابق انعقاد الإجماع اللاحق، وعند محمد لا يمنع وأبويوسف مع أبي حنيفة في رواية ومع محمد في رواية" . (١٧٠-١٧١) : المكتبة الصديقية (ملتان)

(1) قوله : "لكن ما ذكر في كتب الأصول " استدراك على ما ذكر من صحة نسبة القولين إلى المجتهد، ولو كان رجع عن أحدهما، وحاصله : أن المذكور في كتب الأصول خلاف ذلك ؛ فإنهم قالوا لا يمكن أن يكون للمجتهد قولان في زمان واحد ؛ لأن اختلاف القولين إنما يكون لتعارض الأدلة عنده، وما من تعارض إلا وله مخلص، إما حديث أو قول الصحابة أو القياس أو التحرى . فإذا وجد المخلص لكل تعارض لا يمكن أن يكون له قولان في مسألة واحدة في زمان واحد . نعم يمكن هذا عند الشافعي ؛ فإن عنده يوجد المخلص لكل تعارض إلا تعارض القياسين ؛ فإنهما إذا تعارضا لا يصار عنده إلى التحرى كما هو مذهبنا ، بل يعمل المجتهد بأيهما شاء من غير تحر، فيمكن أن يكون له قولان أو أكثر في مسألة واحدة في زمان واحد، أما عندنا فيصار عند تعارض القياسين إلى التحرى، فلا يمكن تعدد الأقوال لأصحابنا في وقت واحد . وما نقل فيه روايتان عنهم، فمحمول على اختلاف الزمان وعدم
Attribution of two statements to the mujtahid

Attribution of one of them to him is valid, because his retraction (1) from the other is not definite, since the assumption is that both are equal in his view, and neither is preponderant over the other.

Yes, if one of them becomes preponderant in his view (2) without his completely turning away from the other and retracting it, then the preponderant one is attributed to him, and the second is mentioned as a narration from him. But if he completely turns away from the other, then it is no longer a statement of his; rather, his statement is only the preponderant one. However, the disagreement in the issue does not disappear after retraction (3), as some Shafi'is stated, and some of them supported it: that if the people of an era agree on a statement after their disagreement, the usuliyyun have reported two views (4) regarding the removal of the previous disagreement. So if consensus does not remove it, then what is less than consensus is more appropriate.

(1) His saying: 'because his retraction from the other' is evidence for negating a third statement.
(2) His saying: 'Yes, if one becomes preponderant in his view': Know that when two statements issue from a mujtahid, either they are equal in his view and he does not deem one preponderant, or he deems one preponderant but does not completely turn away from the other, or he completely turns away from the other and retracts it. In all these cases, it is valid to attribute each of the two statements to him, according to what the commentator (sharh) has verified. As for the first two cases, it is apparent. As for the third case, because retraction does not nullify the previous statement nor render it devoid of consideration, so the retracted statement is not completely severed from attribution, based on the established principle that later consensus does not remove the previous disagreement. So if consensus does not remove it, then what is less than consensus is more appropriate that the previous disagreement is not removed.
(3) His saying: 'However, the disagreement in the issue does not disappear after retraction': Since it is not removed, it is valid to attribute the retracted statement to the mujtahid, even after he completely turned away from it. This is the desired point.
(4) His saying: 'two views regarding the removal of the previous disagreement': He said in al-Bahr: 'This is based on whether later consensus removes the earlier disagreement. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) it does not remove it, because it would entail declaring some Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) to be in error.' (al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, Kitab al-'Itq, Bab al-Istilad: 4/453). And in Tashil al-Wusul ila 'Ilm al-Usul: 'Those who uphold the authority of consensus after the Companions differed over a condition, namely the absence of previous disagreement for the later consensus. Example: the people of an era differed over an issue, and their disagreement became established such that one of the two statements became the madhhab of some and the other the madhhab of others, like the sale of umm al-walad: it is not permitted according to 'Umar (RA) and is permitted according to 'Ali (RA). Does this disagreement prevent the formation of consensus in the subsequent era? The majority of Shafi'is held that it does prevent, and the issue remains one of ijtihad as it was, not a matter of consensus. Our scholars (mashayikhuna) differed on this; most of them—and this is the correct view—said it does not prevent and the disagreement is removed by consensus, and the issue becomes a matter of consensus according to our three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), because the agreement of the mujtahids of the era is considered, and it has occurred. It is also said there is disagreement among our three imams: according to Abu Hanifa, the previous disagreement prevents the formation of later consensus; according to Muhammad, it does not prevent; and Abu Yusuf is with Abu Hanifa in one narration and with Muhammad in another.' (170-171: al-Maktaba al-Siddiqiyya, Multan)

The summary: what is mentioned in the books of usul is contrary to that, for they said it is not possible for a mujtahid to have two statements at one time, because the difference of two statements arises only from the conflict of evidences according to him, and there is no conflict except that it has a resolution, either a hadith, a saying of a Companion, analogy, or tahari (preference). So when a resolution exists for every conflict, it is not possible for him to have two statements in one issue at one time. Commentary
This passage, from a commentary on Rasm al-Mufti, addresses two interrelated issues: the attribution of two contradictory opinions (qawl) to a mujtahid, and the possibility of a mujtahid having multiple opinions simultaneously. The author begins by establishing the rule that a mujtahid can be attributed two opinions even if he has retracted one, because the retraction does not definitively cancel the earlier opinion unless he completely disavows it. This is analogous to the principle that a later consensus (ijma') does not necessarily remove an earlier disagreement (khilaf). The passage then delves into a more theoretical discussion: according to Hanafi usul, a mujtahid cannot have two contradictory opinions at the same time because every conflict of evidence has a resolution (through stronger proof, preference, etc.). However, Imam al-Shafi'i permits this in cases of conflicting analogies (qiyasayn), where he allows the mujtahid to choose either without preference (tahari). The Hanafi position is that such choice is not allowed; instead, the mujtahid must seek preference, thus eliminating the possibility of multiple simultaneous opinions. Any apparent multiple narrations from a single Hanafi imam are attributed to different time periods or circumstances. The passage also covers a dispute among the three Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) regarding whether earlier disagreement prevents later consensus. Abu Hanifa holds that it prevents, Muhammad holds it does not, and Abu Yusuf has two narrations. The majority of later Hanafi scholars adopt the view that later consensus does remove earlier disagreement, as it is the agreement of the current mujtahids that matters.

Key Terms
إِجْمَاع (ijma'): Consensus of the mujtahids of a particular era on a legal ruling.
تَحَرٍّ (tahari): The process of seeking the preponderant evidence when conflicting proofs exist, typically by weighing them.
قِيَاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning used to extend a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause (illa).

B) Because the agreement of mujtahids of the current era is what matters for consensus.
C) Because earlier disagreements are never considered valid.
D) Because all mujtahids must agree at all times.

Key Takeaway
Later consensus removes earlier disagreement according to the majority Hanafi view; and a mujtahid cannot have two contradictory opinions simultaneously.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
advanced
8 min
The Possibility of a Mujtahid Having Two Opinions
Critical refinement of the usul principle on the possibility of two opinions of a mujtahid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on multiple opinions of a mujtahid and apply the principle of later abrogation to attribute the authoritative ruling for fatwa.

Core Idea
A mujtahid cannot hold two contradictory opinions simultaneously; later opinions abrogate earlier ones, and simultaneous attribution is only possible when taharri fails.

This passage delves into a critical discussion in Hanafi rasm al-mufti: can a mujtahid be attributed with two contradictory opinions at the same time? The usul scholars say no, but reality shows multiple narrations. The commentator refines the principle, introducing nuance about taharri. Understanding this dispute is essential for a mufti handling conflicting narrations from early Hanafi authorities.

Guided Path
1
State the usul position
The Hanafi usul scholars hold that a mujtahid cannot have two contradictory opinions at the same time because every conflict of evidence (ta'arud) has a resolution (mukhallas) through turning to a higher source or performing taharri, leaving only one valid ruling.
2
Understand the Shafi'i alternative
The Shafi'i school permits multiple simultaneous opinions when two analogies conflict without clear preference. A Shafi'i mujtahid may choose either without taharri, so both opinions can be attributed to him simultaneously.
3
Recognize the practical problem
The usul position seems to conflict with reality where multiple conflicting narrations from the same scholar exist. If a mujtahid could only have one opinion, then two conflicting narrations could not both be valid, but in practice, both are recorded and acted upon.
4
Analyze the commentator's critique
The commentator argues that the usul reasoning is flawed because taharri does not always resolve. If the mujtahid's heart does not incline to any side, the conflict remains unresolved, making both analogies equally attributable. This allows for simultaneous multiple opinions.
5
Apply the fatwa hierarchy
The relied-upon position is that multiple opinions are from different times; the later opinion abrogates the earlier. If chronology is unknown, the mufti must carefully examine the narrations or perform taharri.

Source Text

ما ذكره الأصوليون من عدم إمكان قولين لمجتهد مشكل؛ فإن الواقع خلافه. لكن ما ذكر فى كتب الأصول عندنا من أنه لا يمكن أن يكون للمجتهد قولان، كما مر - ينافي ذلك؛ لأنه مبنى - فيما يظهر - على ما ذكروا في تعارض الأدلة: أنه إذا وقع التعارض بين آيتين يصار إلى الحديث، فإن الذي بعده؟ ذهب أكثر الشافعية إلى أنه يمنع، وتبقى المسألة مجتهداً فيها كما كانت، لا مجمعاً عليها. واختلف مشايخنا في ذلك فقال أكثرهم - وهو الأصح - إنه لا يمنع ويرتفع الخلاف بالإجماع، وتكون المسألة مجمعا عليها عند علمائنا الثلاثة؛ لأن المعتبر اتفاق مجتهدى العصر وقد وجد. وقيل: فيه اختلاف بين علمائنا الثلاثة، فعند أبي حنيفة يمنع الخلاف السابق انعقاد الإجماع اللاحق، وعند محمد لا يمنع وأبويوسف مع أبي حنيفة في رواية ومع محمد في رواية. (المكتبة الصديقية ملتان)

لكن لا يخفى أن ما ذكروه في بحث تعارض الأدلة مشكل؛ لأنه يلزم معرفة المتأخر، أو على كون الثانية في غير كتب الأصول. ثم أجاب الشارح عن هذا الاستدراك بقوله الآتي: لكن لا يخفى ... إلخ.

إشكال على حصر الأصوليين سبب الاختلاف في تعارض الأدلة ثم بناءهم عدم إمكان تعدد الأقوال عليه بيانه: أن ما ذكره الأصوليون من عدم إمكان قولين لمجتهد في زمان واحد بناء على أن اختلاف الأقوال إنما يكون بسبب تعارض الأدلة، ولكل تعارض مخلص، فإذا لم يبق التعارض لا يبقى الاختلاف - مشكل لا يكاد يصح؛ لأنه يلزم منه أمران الواقع خلافهما: عدم جواز العمل بشيء من الروايتين المختلفتين، وعدم صحة نسبتهما إلى المجتهد، ولما كان اللازم خلاف الواقع فالملزوم مثله، فالصحيح إمكان تعدد الأقوال وصحة نسبة أكثر من قول إلى المجتهد. وأما ما ذكروا من أنه لا يمكن ذلك؛ لأن اختلاف الأقوال مبنى على تعارض الأدلة والتعارض يزول بالصيرورة إلى دليل آخر فيزول الاختلاف المبنى عليه، فجوابه: أن حصر سبب اختلاف الأقوال في تعارض الأدلة ممنوع؛ فإن للاختلاف وجوها أخرى، كما مر عن الإمام البليغى مع زيادة وجه آخر عن المصنف، وكذا لا نسلّم أن التحرى مخلص لتعارض القياسين دائما؛ لأنه يمكن أن لا يميل قلبه إلى شيء ولا يشهد له برجحان أحد القياسين، فيستويان عنده ويصح نسبة كل منهما إليه.
What the usul scholars mentioned about the impossibility of a mujtahid having two sayings is problematic, because the reality contradicts it. But what is mentioned in the books of usul according to us, that it is impossible for a mujtahid to have two sayings, as previously stated, contradicts that; because it is based—as it appears—on what they mentioned about the conflict of evidence: that if there is a conflict between two verses, one turns to the hadith, then if that leads to? Most Shafi'is went that it prevents, and the issue remains a matter of ijtihad as it was, not consensus. And our scholars differed on that; most of them—and it is the most correct—said that it does not prevent, and the disagreement is removed by consensus, and the issue becomes a matter of consensus according to our three imams because the agreement of the mujtahids of the era is considered, and it has occurred. And it was said: there is a difference among our three imams: according to Abu Hanifa, the previous disagreement prevents the later consensus from taking effect; according to Muhammad, it does not prevent; and Abu Yusuf is with Abu Hanifa in one narration and with Muhammad in another.

But it is not hidden that what they mentioned in the discussion of conflicting evidence is problematic because it necessitates knowing the later [opinion] or that the second [narration] is in non-usul books. Then the commentator replied to this objection with his following saying: "But it is not hidden... etc."

An objection to the usul scholars limiting the cause of disagreement to conflicting evidence and then building on it the impossibility of multiple sayings. Its clarification: that what the usul scholars mentioned about the impossibility of a mujtahid having two sayings at one time, based on the fact that differences of opinion arise only from conflicting evidence, and every conflict has a resolution, so when the conflict is removed, the difference is removed—this is problematic and barely correct because it entails two things that contradict reality: the impermissibility of acting on any of the two conflicting narrations, and the invalidity of attributing them to the mujtahid. Since the necessary consequence contradicts reality, the antecedent is likewise false. Thus the correct view is the possibility of multiple sayings and the validity of attributing more than one saying to the mujtahid. As for what they mentioned, that this is impossible because differences of opinion are based on conflicting evidence and the conflict is removed by turning to another evidence, thus the difference based on it is removed, the answer is: the claim that the cause of difference of opinion is limited to conflicting evidence is rejected; for there are other aspects of difference, as mentioned by Imam al-Balighi with an additional aspect from the author. Likewise, we do not concede that taharri always resolves the conflict of two analogies; because it is possible that his heart does not incline to anything and does not witness a preference for either of the two analogies, so they become equal to him, and attributing each of them to him is valid.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on a rasm al-mufti rule concerning the attribution of two contradictory opinions to a single mujtahid. The text begins by stating that the usul scholars' claim that a mujtahid cannot have two sayings is problematic because reality shows otherwise. It then reviews the usul position: that a mujtahid cannot have two opinions at the same time because every conflict of evidence has a resolution, leading to a single ruling. The Shafi'i school, however, allows multiple simultaneous sayings because when two analogies conflict, the mujtahid may choose either without preference. The Hanafi scholars introduced an exception: if later consensus (ijma') forms, it can abrogate previous disagreement, but there is a difference among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on whether the later consensus prevents the earlier ikhtilaf. The commentator then critiques the usul scholars' reasoning: if a mujtahid cannot have two sayings, then the two narrations attributed to him would be invalid, but in practice, both are considered and acted upon. Therefore, multiple sayings are possible, either from different times or because the mujtahid's heart did not incline to one of the conflicting analogies. The final position is that attributing two sayings to a mujtahid is valid, but careful scrutiny is needed to identify the later, more correct opinion for fatwa.

Key Terms
قولان (qawlān): Two contradictory legal opinions attributed to the same mujtahid on a single issue.
مجتهد (mujtahid): A jurist qualified to perform ijtihad, deriving rulings from the sources.
تعارض الأدلة (ta'arud al-adillah): Conflict between two or more pieces of legal evidence that appear contradictory.
تحري (taharri): Seeking the preponderant evidence by way of inner conviction and heart preference when analogies conflict without external preference.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why can a mujtahid not have two contradictory opinions at the same time?
A) Because the mujtahid must follow the majority opinion.
B) Because every conflict of evidence has a resolution, so only one ruling remains.
C) Because the Shafi'i school prohibits multiple opinions.
D) Because the Quran explicitly forbids it.

Key Takeaway
A mujtahid can have multiple attributed opinions, but they arise from different times; the later opinion is authoritative for fatwa, and simultaneous attribution is only possible when taharri fails to resolve a conflict.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
advanced
10 min
Multiple Opinions of a Mujtahid and the Sahaba's Authority
Refines two usul al-iftaa principles: multiple opinions and Sahaba-authority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on multiple opinions of a mujtahid and apply the qawida that the Sahaba's statements are effectively the Imam's statements in fatwa methodology.

Core Idea
A mujtahid can hold multiple valid opinions; the Sahaba's statements carry the Imam's authority in the Hanafi school.

This lesson addresses two foundational principles in Hanafi iftaa usul. First, we explore why a mujtahid (like Imam Abu Hanifa) can have multiple, even contradictory, rulings on the same issue — a reality that the Hanafi school embraces. Second, we examine the rule that the statements of Imam Abu Hanifa's foremost students (the Sahaba) are treated as his own statements, making their fatwas authoritative for muftis today.

Guided Path
1
State the two issues
Identify the two main topics: the possibility of a mujtahid having multiple opinions, and the qawida that the Sahaba's statements are the Imam's statements.
These are linked: the qawida justifies reliance on multiple riwayat.
2
Analyze the dispute on multiple opinions
Compare the Hanafi view (multiple opinions possible due to tahaari failure, istihsan, etc.) with the opposing view (conflict must be resolved).
The passage uses a talil (reasoning) to defend the Hanafi position.
3
Identify the qawida and its scope
Extract the qawida: 'أقوال أصحاب الإمام في الحقيقة أقواله' and understand that it applies in usul al-iftaa.
This is a wusta-rank kulliya qawida.
4
Apply the qawida to furuat
Use the two furuat given: following a Sahaba's opinion when it differs from a direct narration, and adopting their tarjih.
This shows how the qawida works in practice.
5
Note the istithna'
The qawida does not apply when a Sahaba's opinion contradicts a definitive nass from the Imam.
The governing qawida then is 'النصوص مقدمة على الاجتهاد'.

وأما ما ذكروا من أنه لا يمكن ذلك ؛ لأن اختلاف الأقوال مبنى على تعارض الأدلة والتعارض يزول بالصيرورة إلى دليل آخر فيزول الاختلاف المبنى عليه، فجوابه : أن حصر سبب اختلاف الأقوال في تعارض الأدلة ممنوع ؛ فإن للاختلاف وجوها أخرى، كما مر عن الإمام البليغى مع زيادة وجه آخر عن المصنف، وكذا لا نسلّم أن التحرى مخلص لتعارض القياسين دائما ؛ لأنه يمكن أن لا يميل قلبه إلى شيء ولا يشهد له برجحان أحد القياسين، فيستويان عنده ويصح نسبة كل منهما إليه .
الأخذ بأقوال أصحاب الإمام منه أن يكون ما فيه روايتان عن الإمام، لا يجوز فيه العمل بواحدة منهما ؛ لعدم العلم بالصحيحة من الباطلة منهما ، وأنه لا ينسب إليه شيء منهما ، كما مر عن بعض الأصوليين، مع أن ذلك واقع في مسائل لا تحصى، ونراهم يرجحون إحدى الروايتين على الأخرى، وينسبونها إليه . فالذي يظهر ، ما مر عن الإمام البليغى من بيان تعدد الأوجه في اختلاف الرواية عن الإمام، مع زيادة ما ذكرناه من تردّده في الحكمين، واحتمال كل منهما في رأيه مع عدم مرجح عنده لأحدهما من دليل، أو تحر، أو غيره . فتأمل. ثم لا يخفى أن هذا الوجه الذي قلناه أكثر اطرادًا من الأوجه الأربعة المارة فى اختلاف الروايتين؛ لشموله ما فيه استحسان، أو احتياط، وغيره.
مطلب : أقوال أصحاب الإمام في الحقيقة
أقواله ، فمن قلدهم لا يخرج عن تقليده
إذا تقرر ذلك فاعلم (١) : أن الإمام أبا حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى من شدة احتياطه وورعه وعلمه بأن الاختلاف (٢) من آثار الرحمة (٣)، قال لأصحابه :
(١) قوله : "إذا تقرر ذلك فاعلم شروع فى بيان القاعدة التي مهد له المصنف بترجيح صحة تعدد أقوال المجتهد، وسيزيد المصنف فى هذه القاعدة أموراً ثلاثة فانتظرها .
(٢) قوله : " بأن الاختلاف " أى بين المجتهدين في الفروع لا مطلق الاختلاف .
(٣) قوله : " من آثار الرحمة" فإن اختلاف أئمة الهدى توسعة للناس كما في أول " التتارخانية". وهذا يشير إلى الحديث المشهور على ألسنة الناس، وهو اختلاف
Attributing two sayings to a mujtahid: [if they conflict, then] to the sayings of the Companions; if they conflict, then to analogy; if two analogies conflict and there is no preponderance, then he exercises juristic preference (tahaari) and acts by the witness of his heart; if he acts by one, he may not act by the other except with evidence above tahaari. They said: Al-Shafi'i said: 'He acts by whichever he wishes without tahaari.' And for this reason he has two or more sayings in a matter. As for two narrations from our companions in one matter, they were only in two times, so one is correct without the other, but the later one is not known. End. Hence what is said: 'There are two narrations from the Imam' is due to not knowing the later one; and what is said: 'In a narration from him such' is either because they know it is his first saying, or because this narration was narrated from him in non-usul books. This is closer. But it is not hidden that what they mentioned (1) in the discussion of conflicting evidences is problematic, because it necessitates knowing the later one, or that the second is in non-usul books. Then the commentator replied to this objection with his following statement: 'But it is not hidden...' etc. (1) His statement: 'But it is not hidden...' is an objection to the usuliyyun limiting the cause of disagreement to conflicting evidences and then building upon it the impossibility of multiple sayings. Explanation: What the usuliyyun mentioned about the impossibility of two sayings for a mujtahid at one time, based on that disagreement of sayings only arises due to conflicting evidences, and for every conflict there is a resolution, so when the conflict remains no disagreement – is problematic and hardly correct, because it entails two things contrary to reality: the impermissibility of acting with any of the two differing narrations, and the incorrectness of attributing them to the mujtahid. Since the consequent is contrary to reality, the antecedent is likewise. So the correct is the possibility of multiple sayings and the correctness of attributing more than one saying to the mujtahid. As for what they mentioned that this is impossible because disagreement of sayings is based on conflict of evidences and conflict is removed by resorting to another evidence, thus the disagreement based on it is removed – the answer: that limiting the cause of disagreement of sayings to conflict of evidences is denied; for disagreement has other aspects, as mentioned by al-Balighi with an additional aspect from the author, and likewise we do not concede that tahaari always resolves conflict of two analogies, because it is possible that his heart does not incline to anything and does not witness for him preponderance of one of the two analogies, so they are equal in his view, and attributing each of them to him is correct. Taking the sayings of the companions of the Imam means: if there are two narrations from the Imam, it is not permissible to act by either of them due to not knowing the correct from the false, and that nothing is attributed to him from them, as mentioned by some usuliyyun, while that occurs in countless issues, and we see them preponderating one of the two narrations over the other and attributing it to him. So what appears is what is mentioned by al-Balighi of explaining multiple aspects in disagreement of narration from the Imam, with addition of what we mentioned of his hesitation in the two rulings, and the possibility of each in his view without a preponderant in his view from evidence, or tahaari, or other. So reflect. Then it is not hidden that this aspect we say is more prevalent than the four previous aspects in the disagreement of two narrations, because it includes what contains istihsan, or ihtiyat, and other. Section: The statements of the companions of the Imam are in reality his statements, so whoever follows them does not exit from following him. When this is established, then know (1): that Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, due to his extreme caution, piety, and knowledge that disagreement (2) is among the effects of mercy (3), said to his companions: (1) His statement: 'When this is established, then know' is a start in explaining the rule for which the author prepared by preponderating the correctness of multiple sayings of a mujtahid; the author will add in this rule three matters, so wait for them. (2) His statement: 'that disagreement' i.e. between mujtahids in subsidiary matters, not absolute disagreement. (3) His statement: 'from the effects of mercy' for disagreement of the imams of guidance is an expansion for people as in the beginning of al-Tatarkhaniyya. This refers to the famous hadith on people's tongues: 'Disagreement...'
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is a legal definition and refinement of two key principles in Hanafi usul al-iftaa: (1) the possibility of a mujtahid having multiple, even contradictory, opinions at the same time, and (2) the rule that the statements of Imam Abu Hanifa's foremost students (the Sahaba) are effectively his own statements, so following them is equivalent to following him. The author begins by discussing the procedure when attributing conflicting opinions to a mujtahid: the priority goes to the Companions' sayings, then to analogy; if two analogies conflict, the mujtahid exercises tahaari (seeking truth by his heart). He contrasts the view of some, attributed to al-Shafi'i, that the mujtahid may choose arbitrarily without tahaari, which is why al-Shafi'i is said to have two opinions in a matter. For the Hanafis, two narrations from the Imam in one matter are believed to be from two different times, only one being correct, but if the later one is unknown, both are attributed. The commentator then objects to the usuliyun's claim that a mujtahid cannot have two opinions simultaneously because disagreement stems from conflicting evidence which can be resolved. He argues that this leads to the impossible consequence of invalidating all such narrations. In reality, multiple opinions are possible due to other causes: the mujtahid may genuinely hesitate between two rulings, neither preponderating. This view is more comprehensive than the four earlier reasons given for contradictory narrations. The passage then establishes the key rule: the statements of the Sahaba (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, etc.) are in reality the Imam's statements. This is supported by the Imam's own practice of encouraging his students to bring evidence, and by the fact that their disagreements are a mercy for the community. The commentator notes that this principle is fleshed out by the author in three forthcoming points. The overall aim is to provide a theoretical foundation for why Hanafi muftis can rely on the Sahaba's opinions as authoritative, and to justify the existence of multiple rulings in the school without undermining the attribution to the founder.

Key Terms
رواية (riwaya): A transmitted report of a ruling attributed to a mujtahid or imam, often varying in content or wording.
مجتهد (mujtahid): A qualified jurist who can derive rulings directly from primary sources (Quran, Sunnah, ijma, qiyas).
تحري (tahaari): A process of seeking the correct ruling by inner contemplation and inclination of the heart when evidence is balanced.
تعارض (ta'aarud): The situation where two pieces of evidence or two rulings appear to contradict each other.
قول (qaul): An opinion or statement of a mujtahid constituting a legal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is it possible for a mujtahid to have two contradictory opinions in a single matter?
A) Because he might forget his earlier opinion.
B) Because his opinion changes with the times, and the later opinion abrogates the earlier.
C) Because he may genuinely hesitate between two rulings without a clear preponderant, or other causes like istihsan create multiple valid views.
D) Because the Quran and Sunnah contain contradictions.

Key Takeaway
A mujtahid can have multiple valid opinions, and the Sahaba's statements are considered the Imam's own, providing a flexible yet authoritative framework for Hanafi fatwa.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate

The Sayings of the Imam's Companions Are His Sayings
Establishing that the opinions of Abu Hanifa's students are effectively his own through historical evidence and consultative methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the opinions of Abu Hanifa's students are considered his own and apply this principle when evaluating the authority of fatwa positions within the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
The opinions of Abu Hanifa's students are in reality his own — following them is following the Imam.

This lesson examines a foundational principle in Hanafi iftaa: the legal opinions of Abu Hanifa's senior students (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar) are not independent schools but extensions of the Imam's own ijtihad. Understanding this principle is crucial for a mufti when navigating apparent disagreements within the Hanafi corpus, as it determines which opinions are considered authoritative within the madhhab.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
State clearly what the passage claims about the relationship between the statements of Abu Hanifa and the statements of his students. The author asserts that the students' statements are in reality the Imam's statements.
This is the central claim that underlies the permissibility of following the Sahibayn's views.
2
Examine the supporting evidence
Review the quotes from Abu Yusuf and Zufar. Abu Yusuf says he never differed from Abu Hanifa except on something Abu Hanifa had already said. Zufar says any difference was on a matter Abu Hanifa had said and then retracted. These show the students' rulings are within the Imam's ijtihad.
The evidence is narrative (dalil naqli) from early authorities.
3
Analyze the consultative method (shura)
Understand the story from al-Tahawi: Abu Hanifa consulted with his students, debated, and only after consensus was a position recorded. This indicates that the final rulings were collective, not his sole dictation.
This establishes the methodology by which the Imam's own views evolved.
4
Apply the principle
Consider a scenario: a mufti finds two opinions in the school — one from Abu Hanifa directly, another from Abu Yusuf. According to this principle, both are from the Imam. The mufti may choose the stronger or more suitable opinion without leaving the madhhab.
The practical outcome is increased flexibility within the school.
5
Distinguish from common misunderstanding
Clarify that this does not mean every student's opinion is automatically the Imam's; rather, the senior students who studied with him and followed his methodology produced opinions that are considered part of his ijtihad.
Avoid the misconception that all later Hanafi opinions are retroactively attributed to Abu Hanifa.

وسبب ذلك ما نقله العلامة الطحطاوي في حاشيته على الدر المختار عن مسند الخوارزمى عن سيف الأئمة السابلي ما نصه : اشتهر و استفاض أن أبا حنيفة رحمه الله تعالى تلمذ لأربعة آلاف من شيوخ أئمة التابعين وتفـقـه عنـد أربعة آلاف، فلم يفت بلسانه ولا بقلمه حتى أمروه ، فجلس في مجلس في جامع الكوفة فاجتمع معه ألف من أصحابه أجلهم وأفضلهم أربعون قد بلغوا حد الاجتهاد، فقربهم وأدناهم وقال لهم : أنتم أجلة أصحابي، ومسار قلبي، وجلاء أحزاني، وإني ألجمت هذا الفقه وأسرجته لكم، فأعينوني ؛ فإن الناس قد جعلوني جسراً على النار ؛ فإن المنتهى لغيري والعب على ظهري ، فكان رحمه الله إذا وقعت واقعة شاورهم وناظرهم، وحاورهم، وسألهم، فيسمع ما عندهم من الأخبار والآثار، ويقول ما عنده ويناظرونه شهراً أو أكثر حتى يستقر آخر الأقوال، فيثبته أبو يوسف، حتى أثبت الأصول على هذا المنهاج ،شورى، لا أنه تفرد بذلك كغيره من الأئمة .
Then know that all that he mentioned regarding the validity of attributing two or more statements to a mujtahid, then the criticism and response to it, is a prelude to the following principle: that the statements of the companions of the Imam are in reality his statements. So whoever follows them does not depart from following him. For this principle is hardly established except after conceding the possibility of multiple statements from a mujtahid and the validity of attributing them to him. So understand.
When this is established, then know (1): that Imam Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him, due to his extreme caution, piety, and knowledge that disagreement (2) is among the effects of mercy (3), said to his companions: 'If a proof appears to you (1), then speak by it (2).' So each would take a narration from him (3) and prefer it, as is mentioned in al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
And in al-Walwaljiyya from the Book of Criminal Law: Abu Yusuf said: 'I have not said a statement in which I differed from Abu Hanifa except a statement that he had [already] said.' And it is narrated from Zufar that he said: 'I have not differed from Abu Hanifa in anything except that he had said it and then retracted it.' This indicates that...
And the reason for that is what al-Tahawi transmitted in his gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtar from Musnad al-Khwarizmi from Sayf al-A'imma al-Sabli, the text of which: It became widely known that Abu Hanifa studied under four thousand of the leading scholars of the Tabi'in and learned fiqh from four thousand, and he did not give fatwa with his tongue nor with his pen until they ordered him. Then he sat in a gathering in the Great Mosque of Kufa, and a thousand of his companions gathered with him, the most eminent and best of them being forty who had reached the level of ijtihad. He brought them close and said: 'You are my eminent companions, the light of my heart, the clarity of my sorrows. I have bridled this fiqh and saddled it for you, so help me; for people have made me a bridge over the fire; indeed the final destination is for someone else, so play on my back.' And whenever an event occurred, he would consult them, debate with them, discuss with them, and ask them. He would listen to what they had of reports and traditions, and he would say what he had, and they would debate with him for a month or more until the final opinion settled. Then Abu Yusuf would record it, until the principles were established upon this method of consultation, not that he alone was unique in that like other imams.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the methodology of attributing fatwa positions within the Hanafi school. The author explains that the statements of Abu Hanifa's senior students (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar) are in reality considered statements of the Imam himself. The reasoning is that Abu Hanifa taught them and authorized them to follow evidence even if it contradicts his apparent position. The text cites evidence from al-Durr al-Mukhtar and al-Walwaljiyya that Abu Yusuf and Zufar only differed from Abu Hanifa on matters where he himself had expressed an opinion that he later retracted, meaning their opinions are not outside his ijtihad. A lengthy transmitted story supports this by describing Abu Hanifa's consultative method (shura) with his students, showing that the final opinions were the result of collective ijtihad, not his sole dictation. The key principle for the mufti is: following the sayings of the Sahibayn or other students is still following Abu Hanifa's school.

Key Terms
أقوال أصحاب الإمام (aqwal ashab al-imam): The legal opinions of the primary students of Abu Hanifa (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar, etc.) as recorded in Hanafi texts.
تعدد الأقوال (ta'addud al-aqwal): The phenomenon of a single mujtahid having multiple, contradictory statements on the same issue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the opinions of Abu Hanifa's senior students?
A) They are independent rulings that form separate schools.
B) They are in reality the statements of Abu Hanifa himself.
C) They are only valid if they agree with the Imam's statement.
D) They are less authoritative than the Imam's direct sayings.

Key Takeaway
The opinions of Abu Hanifa's students are considered part of his own ijtihad, so a mufti may confidently act upon them within the Hanafi school.

Adopting the Sayings of the Imam's Companions
Explaining the status of companions' opinions vis-à-vis the Imam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the authoritative status of the companions' opinions in the Hanafi school and apply the maxim 'al-madhhab al-shura'i' to fatwa practice.

Core Idea
The Hanafi school is consultative: following a companion's opinion is valid, but caution and priority to the Imam's rulings apply.

This passage addresses a fundamental question for every Hanafi mufti: can you rely on the opinion of Abu Yusuf or Muhammad al-Shaybani instead of Abu Hanifa? The passage explores two competing views: one claiming all companion statements are narrations from the Imam, and another asserting they are independent mujtahids. The resolution lies in understanding the Hanafi school as a consultative (shura'i) madhhab, where the companions' opinions are included by the Imam's permission and based on his principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two extreme positions
On one side, al-Hawi al-Qudsi claims all companion sayings are narrations from Abu Hanifa. On the other, critics argue they are independent and not attributable to the Imam.
Note the evidence cited: oaths vs. transmission and reason.
2
Analyse the objections
Examine the critique: the chains are weak, companions often adopted positions not narrated, and they were independent mujtahids. This challenges the claim that their opinions are the Imam's.
3
Locate the mediating qawida
The commentator resolves the tension by introducing the maxim 'al-madhhab al-Hanafi madhhab shura'i la shakhsi' — the Hanafi school is consultative, not personal. This means the companions' opinions are included because they were issued with the Imam's permission and built on his principles.
4
Apply to fatwa practice
A mufti may follow a companion's opinion if it appears stronger or if custom dictates, but the primary reliance is on the Imam's well-known rulings from zahir al-riwaya. The consultative nature allows flexibility without leaving the school.

Source Text

الأخذ بأقوال أصحاب الإمام
إن توجه لكم دليل، فقولوا به. فكان كل يأخذ برواية عنه ويرجحها، كما حكاه في "الدر المختار".
وفى "الولوالجية" من كتاب الجنايات: قال أبو يوسف: "ما قلت قولا خالفت فيه أبا حنيفة إلا قولا قد كان قاله. وروى عن زفر أنه قال: ما خالفت أبا حنيفة في شيء إلا قد قاله ثم رجع عنه". فهذا إشارة إلى أنهم ما سلكوا طريق الخلاف، بل قالوا ما قالوا عن اجتهاد ورأى؛ اتباعا لما قاله أستاذهم أبو حنيفة. اهـ.
وفى آخر "الحاوى القدسي": وإذا أخذ بقول واحد منهم، يعلم قطعًا أنه يكون به آخذا بقول أبي حنيفة؛ فإنه روى عن جميع أصحابه الكبار كأبي يوسف، ومحمد، وزفر، والحسن، أنهم قالوا: "ما قلنا في مسألة قولا إلا وهو روايتنا عن أبي حنيفة"، وأقسموا عليه أيمانا غلاظا، فلم يتحقق إذن في الفقه جواب ولا مذهب إلا له كيف ما كان وما نسب إلى غيره إلا بطريق المجاز؛ للموافقة. اهـ.
فإن قلت: إذا رجع المجتهد عن قول لم يبق قولا له؛ لأنه صار كالحكم المنسوخ، كما سيأتي، وحينئذ فما قاله أصحابه مخالفين له فيه، ليس مذهبه، بل صارت أقوالهم مذاهب لهم، فكيف تنسب إليه؟ والحنفى إنما قلد أصحابه. (مظ)
(۱) قوله: " فإنه روى عن جميع أصحابه " لقائل أن ينكر هذا رواية ودراية. أما رواية؛ فلأن ثبوت هذه الأقوال بالسند الصحيح مشكل، وأيضا لا يخفى على من طالع كتب الفقه أن الأصحاب كثيراً ما يأخذون بأقوال لا يوافقها رواية عن الإمام، وأما دراية؛ فلأن الأصحاب خالفوا الإمام فى كثير من القواعد والاتفاق في جميع الفروع مع الاختلاف في الأصول بعيد، ولأن الصاحبين بلغا مبلغ الاجتهاد المطلق، وبعيد من المجتهد أن لا يقول قولا إلا ما وافق قول شيخه، وعلى هذا فأقوال الأصحاب تنسب إليهم وتصير مذاهب لهم، ولا يسوغ لمن قلد أبا حنيفة أن يعمل عليها. اللهم إلا أن يقال: إن المذهب الحنفى مذهب شورائي لا شخصی، فمن عمل على أقوال أصحاب أبي حنيفة لا يخرج عن كونه حنفيا؛ لأنها داخلة في المذهب الحنفى من وجه لصدورها عن إذن من صاحب المذهب وأمره، ولا بتناءها في الأكثر على القواعد التي أسسها لهم، أو أذن لهم فى تأسيسها، ولعرضها عليه في مجلس قامت لاستخراج مسائل فقهية، فافهم.
(۲) قوله: "لم يبق قولا له بل صرح في قضاء البحر: بأن ما خرج عن ظاهر الرواية فهو مرجوع عنه، وإن المرجوع عنه ليس قولا له. وفي الحاوى القدسي" نقلا عن
Adopting the Sayings of the Imam's Companions
"If a proof becomes apparent to you, then act upon it." So each of them would take a narration from him and give it preference, as mentioned in al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
In al-Walwaljiyah from the Book of Criminal Law: Abu Yusuf said: "I never said a statement in which I opposed Abu Hanifa except a statement that he himself had said." And it is narrated from Zufar that he said: "I never opposed Abu Hanifa in anything except that he had said it and then retracted it." This indicates that they did not tread the path of disagreement; rather, they said what they said based on ijtihad and opinion, following what their teacher Abu Hanifa said. End.
At the end of al-Hawi al-Qudsi: "If one adopts the saying of one of them, he absolutely knows that by doing so he adopts the saying of Abu Hanifa; for it is narrated from all his senior companions like Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Hasan that they said: 'We never said a statement in a matter except that it is our narration from Abu Hanifa,' and they swore strong oaths upon it. So in fiqh, no answer and no madhhab is verified except his, however it may be, and what is attributed to others is only by way of metaphor, due to agreement. End.
If you say: When a mujtahid retracts a statement, it no longer remains his statement, because it becomes like an abrogated ruling, as will come. In that case, what his companions said in opposition to him is not his madhhab; rather, their statements become their own madhhabs. So how is it attributed to him? And the Hanafi only follows his companions. (Muz)
(1) His statement: "For it is narrated from all his companions" — one may deny this in terms of transmission and reason. As for transmission: because the establishment of these statements with a sound chain is problematic, and also it is not hidden from one who studies books of fiqh that the companions often adopt statements that do not agree with a narration from the Imam. As for reason: because the companions opposed the Imam in many rules, and agreement in all branches with differences in principles is unlikely, and because the two companions reached the level of absolute ijtihad, and it is unlikely for a mujtahid to not say a statement except that which agrees with his teacher's statement. On this basis, the statements of the companions are attributed to them and become their own madhhabs, and it is not permissible for one who follows Abu Hanifa to act upon them. Unless it is said that the Hanafi school is a consultative school, not a personal one, so whoever acts upon the statements of Abu Hanifa's companions does not exit from being a Hanafi, because they are included in the Hanafi school in a sense, due to their issuing by permission and command of the school's founder, and their being built mostly upon the principles he established for them or allowed them to establish, and their being presented to him in the council established to extract fiqh issues. So understand.
(2) His statement: "it no longer remains his statement" — rather, it is stated in Qada' al-Bahr: that whatever is outside the zahir al-riwaya is retracted, and the retracted is not his statement. And in al-Hawi al-Qudsi, quoting from...
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage discusses a key principle of rasm al-mufti: the status of the opinions of the companions of Abu Hanifa (such as Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, etc.) in relation to the Imam himself. The passage begins by quoting Abu Hanifa's directive to his students: 'If a proof becomes apparent to you, then act upon it.' This indicates that the companions were not bound to follow the Imam if they found stronger evidence, thus allowing them to issue rulings that differed from his.

Abu Yusuf and Zufar are reported to have said that their differences were only based on statements the Imam had made at some point, suggesting that their opinions are ultimately rooted in his teachings. The text then cites al-Hawi al-Qudsi, which claims that all senior companions swore that every statement they made was a narration from Abu Hanifa, implying that their opinions are effectively his.

However, the author raises a critical objection: if a mujtahid retracts a statement, it no longer stands. So how can the companions' differing opinions be attributed to Abu Hanifa? Moreover, the claim that all their statements are from him is questionable both in transmission (unsound chains) and in reason (the companions were independent mujtahids and often adopted positions not narrated from the Imam). Therefore, the author argues that strictly, the companions' opinions are their own madhhabs, and a Hanafi muqallid should not act on them unless the Hanafi school is understood as a consultative (shura-based) school rather than a purely personal one. In this view, since the companions acted with the Imam's permission and based on his principles, their rulings are considered part of the Hanafi school, and acting on them does not take one out of the school.

The passage thus establishes that while the relied-upon position is that of Imam Abu Hanifa (especially from zahir al-riwaya), the opinions of the companions carry significant weight and can be followed in certain contexts, making the school broader than the Imam's individual sayings.

Key Terms
أصحاب الإمام (ashab al-imam): The senior students of Abu Hanifa who reached the level of ijtihad, such as Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Hasan
الأخذ بأقوال أصحاب الإمام (al-akhdh bi aqwal ashab al-imam): The principle of adopting the legal opinions of the Imam's companions as valid expressions of the Hanafi school
المذهب الشورائي (al-madhhab al-shura'i): A school of law developed through consultation and collective ijtihad rather than solely the personal opinion of the founder

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for considering the opinions of Abu Hanifa's companions as part of the Hanafi school?
A) They were all infallible and their every word is directly from the Quran and Sunnah.
B) Abu Hanifa ordered them to follow their own ijtihad if they found evidence, and they built upon his principles with his permission.
C) They never disagreed with Abu Hanifa, so their opinions are identical to his.
D) The later scholars forced the muftis to accept the companions' opinions regardless.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school is consultative: a mufti may follow a companion's opinion but must prioritize the Imam's well-known ruling unless evidence or circumstance dictates otherwise.

Attribution of Disciples' Opinions and the Authentic Hadith Rule
Explaining how disciples' opinions are attributed to Abu Hanifa and the rule for authentic hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the Hanafi madhhab incorporates disciples' opinions and apply the rule 'If the hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab' with its two conditions.

Core Idea
The Hanafi madhhab is consultative; disciples' rulings are included, and an authentic hadith overrides the madhhab ruling.

This passage addresses two interconnected yet distinct issues in Hanafi usul al-iftaa: the status of the disciples' (ashāb) opinions and the famous principle that an authentic hadith becomes the madhhab. While at first glance these seem separate, they share a common thread—the flexibility and evidence-based nature of the Hanafi school, which is not rigidly bound to the personal views of Abu Hanifa alone. Understanding these rules is crucial for a mufti who must navigate between established positions and stronger evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two issues
The passage discusses (a) attribution of disciples' opinions to Abu Hanifa, and (b) the rule 'idha saha al-hadith fa-huwa madhhabi'. Understand how they are related.
Though distinct, both deal with expanding the scope of the madhhab beyond the Imam's own statements.
2
Analyze the objection and response regarding disciples
The objection: if a mujtahid retracts, his opinion is void; so how can disciples' conflicting opinions be attributed? The response: the madhhab is consultative (shūrā'ī), and disciples' opinions are based on the Imam's principles or permission.
The margin note provides critique of the narration, but the author (Ibn 'Abidin) upholds the consultative model.
3
Derive the two conditions for the hadith rule
From the text: the hadith must be authentic and must contradict the established madhhab. Only then does it become the Imam's madhhab.
The rule is precautionary—not any hadith, but only those meeting both conditions.
4
Apply the principles to fatwa practice
A mufti may follow a disciple's opinion if it is consistent with the Imam's principles, and must follow a sahih hadith even if it contradicts the madhhab, while remaining Hanafi.
The passage explicitly states that the follower does not cease to be Hanafi.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأخذ بأقوال أصحاب الإمام ما سلكوا طريق الخلاف، بل قالوا ما قالوا عن اجتهاد ورأى ؛ اتباعا لما قاله أستاذهم أبو حنيفة . اهـ .
وفى آخر "الحاوى القدسي " : وإذا أخذ بقول واحد منهم، يعلم قطعًا أنه يكون به آخذا بقول أبي حنيفة ؛ فإنه روى عن جميع أصحابه (۱) الكبار كأبي يوسف، ومحمد ،وزفر، والحسن، أنهم قالوا : " ما قلنا في مسألة قولا إلا وهو روايتنا عن أبي حنيفة" ، وأقسموا عليه أيمانا غلاظا، فلم يتحقق إذن في الفقه جواب ولا مذهب إلا له كيف ما كان وما نسب إلى غيره إلا بطريق المجاز ؛ للموافقة . اهـ .
فإن قلت : إذا رجع المجتهد عن قول لم يبق قولا له (۲) ؛ لأنه صار كالحكم المنسوخ، كما سيأتي، وحينئذ فما قاله أصحابه مخالفين له فيه، ليس مذهبه، بل صارت أقوالهم مذاهب لهم، فكيف تنسب إليه؟ والحنفى إنما قلد أصحابه . (مظ)
(۱) قوله : " فإنه روى عن جميع أصحابه " لقائل أن ينكر هذا رواية ودراية . أما رواية ؛ فلأن ثبوت هذه الأقوال بالسند الصحيح مشكل ، وأيضا لا يخفى على من طالع كتب الفقه أن الأصحاب كثيراً ما يأخذون بأقوال لا يوافقها رواية عن الإمام، وأما دراية؛ فلأن الأصحاب خالفوا الإمام فى كثير من القواعد والاتفاق في جميع الفروع مع الاختلاف في الأصول بعيد، ولأن الصاحبين بلغا مبلغ الاجتهاد المطلق، وبعيد من المجتهد أن لا يقول قولا إلا ما وافق قول شيخه، وعلى هذا فأقوال الأصحاب تنسب إليهم وتصير مذاهب لهم، ولا يسوغ لمن قلد أبا حنيفة أن يعمل عليها . اللهم إلا أن يقال: إن المذهب الحنفى مذهب شورائي لا ،شخصی، فمن عمل على أقوال أصحاب أبي حنيفة لا يخرج عن كونه حنفيا؛ لأنها داخلة في المذهب الحنفى من وجه لصدورها عن إذن من صاحب المذهب وأمره، ولا بتناءها في الأكثر على القواعد التي أسسها لهم، أو أذن لهم فى تأسيسها، ولعرضها عليه في مجلس قامت لاستخراج مسائل فقهية، فافهم.
(۲) قوله : "لم يبق قولا له بل صرح في قضاء البحر : بأن ما خرج عن ظاهر الرواية فهو مرجوع عنه، وإن المرجوع عنه ليس قولا له . وفي الحاوى القدسي" نقلا عن أبا حنيفة، ولذا نسب إليه دون غيره .
قلت : قد كنت استشكلت ذلك، وأجبت عنه في حاشيتي رد المحتار على الدر المختار " : بأن الإمام لما أمر أصحابه بأن يأخذوا من أقواله بما يتجه لهم منها الدليل عليه صار ما قالوه قولا له ؛ لابتناءه على قواعده أسسها لهم، فلم يكن مرجوعا عنه من كل وجه .
مطلب
إذا صح الحديث فهو مذهب أبي حنيفة بشرطين
رام
ونظير هذا ما نقله العلامة البيرى فى أول شرحه على الأشباه عن شرح الهداية " لابن الشحنة الكبير، والد شارح الوهبانية وشيخ ابن الهمام، ونصه : إذا صح الحديث، وكان على خلاف المذهب عمل بالحديث (۲) ، ويكون ذلك مذهبه، ولا يخرج مقلده عن كونه حنفيا بالعمل
التوشيح " : أن ما رجع عنه المجتهد لا يجوز الأخذ به . (مظ)
(۱) قوله : " لابتناءه على قواعده " أى لابتناء أكثره على قواعده، أو لابتناءه على القواعد التي أذن لهم في تأسيسها، ففى الكلام ،مجاز، أو المراد من القاعدة قوله : إن توجه لكم دليل فقولوا به ، أو قوله : "إذا صح الحديث فهو مذهبي"، وإنما قلنا هذا؛ لأن الأصحاب يخالفونه في الأصول أيضا، وقد جمع الإمام أبو زيد عبيد الله : ابن عمر الدبوسي في كتابه المشهور "تأسيس النظر " الأصول التي اختلف فـيـهـا أبو حنيفة وصاحباه أو أصحابه الآخرون، وقسمها على ثمانية أقسام بحيث لم يسبقه أحد في هذا المنهج، وكذا لا يصح أن يقال في حقهم : إن جميع أقوالهم مأخوذة من أقوال الإمام المتروكة المرجوعة عنها ؛ لأن الواقع أنهم اختاروا ما هو خلاف مذهبه رأساً، وخارج عن أقواله أصلا .
Taking the opinions of the disciples of the Imam: they did not follow the path of disagreement, but rather they said what they said based on ijtihad and opinion, following what their teacher Abu Hanifa said. End. And in the end of 'Al-Hawi al-Qudsi': If one takes a single opinion from them, he knows certainly that he is thereby taking the opinion of Abu Hanifa; because it is narrated from all his senior disciples (1) like Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Hasan, that they said: 'We have not said a single opinion in any issue except that it is our narration from Abu Hanifa,' and they swore strong oaths upon that. So there is no verified opinion or madhhab in fiqh except his, however it may be, and what is attributed to others is only by way of figurative speech due to agreement. End. If you say: If a mujtahid retracts an opinion, it no longer remains his opinion (2); because it becomes like abrogated ruling, as will come, and then what his disciples say opposing him in it is not his madhhab, but rather their opinions become madhhabs for them, so how is it attributed to him? The Hanafi only follows his disciples. (margin note) (1) His saying: 'It is narrated from all his disciples' - someone can deny this, both in terms of narration and knowledge. As for narration, because the establishment of these statements with authentic chain is problematic; also it is not hidden from those who study books of fiqh that the disciples often adopt opinions that are not supported by a narration from the Imam. As for knowledge, because the disciples disagreed with the Imam in many principles, and agreement in all subsidiary issues despite disagreement in principles is improbable; and because the two senior disciples reached the level of absolute ijtihad, and it is unlikely for a mujtahid to only say what agrees with his teacher's opinion. On this basis, the disciples' opinions are attributed to them and become madhhabs for them, and it is not permissible for someone who follows Abu Hanifa to act upon them. Unless it is said: The Hanafi madhhab is a consultative madhhab, not personal; so whoever acts upon the opinions of Abu Hanifa's disciples does not cease to be a Hanafi, because they are included in the Hanafi madhhab in a way, due to their being issued with the permission and order of the founder of the madhhab, and most of them are based on the principles he established for them, or he authorized them to establish, and because they were presented to him in the assembly established for extracting fiqhi issues. So understand. (2) His saying: 'It no longer remains his opinion' - rather it is clarified in Qada' al-Bahr that whatever is outside the zahir al-riwayah is retracted, and the retracted is not his opinion. And in Al-Hawi al-Qudsi, quoting from Abu Hanifa, and therefore attributed to him and not to others. I said: I had previously found this problematic, and I answered it in my footnotes Radd al-Muhtar 'ala al-Durr al-Mukhtar: That when the Imam ordered his disciples to take from his opinions what is in accordance with the evidence that is apparent to them, what they said became his opinion, because it is based on his principles that he established for them, so it is not retracted from all aspects. Topic: If the hadith is authentic, then it is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa with two conditions. And similar to this is what Allamah al-Biri narrated in the beginning of his commentary on al-Ashbah from Sharh al-Hidayah of Ibn al-Shahnah al-Kabir, father of the commentator of al-Wahbaniyyah and teacher of Ibn al-Humam, and its text: If the hadith is authentic and contradicts the madhhab, one acts upon the hadith (2), and that becomes his madhhab, and his follower does not cease to be Hanafi by acting upon it. Al-Tawshih: That what the mujtahid retracts is not permissible to take. (margin note) (1) His saying: 'Because it is based on his principles' - meaning most of it is based on his principles, or based on the principles he authorized them to establish. So there is a metaphor in the statement, or the intended meaning of 'principle' is his saying: 'If an evidence is apparent to you, then say it,' or his saying: 'If the hadith is authentic, then it is my madhhab.' We only said this because the disciples also disagree with him in principles. Imam Abu Zayd Ubaidallah ibn Umar al-Dabusi has collected in his famous book Ta'sis al-Nazar the principles in which Abu Hanifa and his two companions or other disciples disagreed, and divided them into eight sections in a way no one preceded him in this method. Likewise, it is not correct to say about them that all their opinions are taken from the abandoned and retracted opinions of the Imam; because the reality is that they chose what is completely against his madhhab and outside his opinions entirely.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on how the opinions of Abu Hanifa's disciples (ashāb) are considered within the Hanafi madhhab, and it introduces the famous principle 'If the hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab.' The text begins by asserting that the disciples did not merely follow Abu Hanifa blindly; they exercised ijtihad and expressed their own opinions, but those opinions are ultimately traced back to his teachings. A quote from Al-Hawi al-Qudsi claims that every opinion of the senior disciples is actually a narration from Abu Hanifa, and they swore oaths to this effect. This would mean that any opinion attributed to a disciple is effectively the Imam's own view. However, the author immediately presents an objection: if a mujtahid retracts an opinion, it ceases to be his; so how can the disciples' differing opinions be attributed to him? A marginal note (1) provides a critical analysis, denying the claim on both narrative and rational grounds. It points out that the disciples often held views contrary to the Imam, and they were themselves absolute mujtahids. The note suggests that the disciples' opinions are their own madhhab, not Abu Hanifa's. However, it offers a resolution: the Hanafi madhhab is 'consultative' (shūrā'ī), not personal. Since the disciples worked with the Imam's permission and based their rulings on his principles, their opinions are considered part of the broader Hanafi tradition. Acting on them does not make one a non-Hanafi. The author (Ibn 'Abidin) then responds to the objection by explaining that the Imam instructed his disciples to adopt his opinions that were supported by evidence, so their rulings are built on his principles and are not entirely retracted. The passage then shifts to a related rule: 'If the hadith is authentic, it is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa with two conditions.' This rule, attributed to the Imam himself, means that when an authentic hadith contradicts the established madhhab, the hadith is to be followed, and that becomes the Imam's madhhab. A Hanafi follower who acts on such a hadith remains a Hanafi. A marginal note clarifies that 'based on his principles' may refer to the Imam's directive to follow the evidence or the hadith rule itself, but notes that the disciples did disagree with the Imam in principles, as documented by al-Dabusi. Thus, it is incorrect to claim that all disciples' opinions are derived from the Imam's abandoned views; they sometimes chose entirely different positions.

Key Terms
أصحاب (ashāb): The disciples of Abu Hanifa, especially Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, and al-Hasan, who were his senior students and mujtahids.
مذهب شورائي (madhhab shūrā'ī): A consultative madhhab, meaning it incorporates opinions formed through consultation and collective ijtihad, not merely the personal views of one individual.
إذا صح الحديث فهو مذهبي (idhā ṣaḥḥa al-ḥadīthuhuwa madhhabī): A ruling from Abu Hanifa that an authentic hadith contrary to the established madhhab becomes his madhhab, and followers may act on it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two conditions for the rule 'If the hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab'?
A) The hadith must be authentic and must be from the Sahihayn.
B) The hadith must be authentic and must contradict the established madhhab.
C) The hadith must be mutawatir and must be acted upon by the majority.
D) The hadith must be authentic and must be approved by the disciples.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab is a consultative school that incorporates disciples' ijtihad, and an authentic hadith overrides the established position, becoming the true madhhab of Abu Hanifa.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
8 min
The Maxim 'If a Hadith is Authentic, It is My Madhhab' with Conditions
Establishing a maxim with qualifications for its application in fatwa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rasm al-mufti rule regarding the maxim 'If a hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab' by identifying the conditions under which a fatwa may depart from the apparent madhhab and evaluating whether a given scenario qualifies.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa's maxim authorizes following authentic hadith, but only for qualified muftis under strict conditions.

This lesson unpacks one of the most powerful yet misunderstood maxims in Hanafi fatwa methodology. The maxim 'If a hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab' is not a license for laypersons or even junior scholars to cherry-pick hadith. Rather, it is a carefully qualified rule that permits departure from the transmitted ruling only when the hadith is unequivocally authentic, clear, and free from any indication that the Imam might have left it due to a valid obstacle. We will trace its basis in the statements of Abu Hanifa, see how it justifies the work of the Sahibayn, and lay out the conditions that must be satisfied for a fatwa to follow the hadith over the apparent madhhab.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its legal operation
The maxim 'If a hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab' is a rasm al-mufti rule that permits a mufti to depart from the transmitted ruling of the madhhab when an authentic hadith contradicts it.
This does not mean every authentic hadith overrides the madhhab; conditions apply.
2
Understand the justification for the Sahibayn's rulings
Ibn Abidin explains that Abu Hanifa authorized his companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) to adopt evidence-based opinions that derive from his principles, thus making their rulings part of the madhhab.
This answers why the Sahibayn's views are considered Hanafi despite occasional differences.
3
Extract the conditions from the footnote (Ibn al-Salah)
The hadith must be sahih, clear (sarih), not opposed (mu'arid), not interpreted (mu'awwal), not abrogated (nasikh), not specified (mukhasis), not restricted (muqayyad), and no hidden obstacle (mani') must be known.
These conditions ensure the hadith is unequivocal and that the Imam would have followed it had he known of its applicability.
4
Apply the restriction of agreement within the madhhab
The author adds that the new ruling must agree with some statement within the madhhab; ijtihad outside the school's agreed principles is not permitted.
This prevents arbitrary departure from core Hanafi doctrines.
5
Recognize the qualification for application
Only those qualified to examine texts, distinguish abrogated from unqualified, and understand the madhhab's principles can apply this maxim. Laypersons are explicitly barred by Imam Abu Yusuf's statement.
The muqallid (follower) cannot independently act upon a hadith he hears.

أبا حنيفة، ولذا نسب إليه دون غيره .

إذا صح الحديث فهو

رام
ونظير هذا ما نقله العلامة البيرى فى أول شرحه على الأشباه عن شرح الهداية " لابن الشحنة الكبير، والد شارح الوهبانية وشيخ ابن الهمام، ونصه : إذا صح الحديث، وكان على خلاف المذهب عمل بالحديث (۲) ، ويكون ذلك مذهبه، ولا يخرج مقلده عن كونه حنفيا بالعمل
به، فقد صح عن أبي حنيفة أنه قال : "إذا صح الحديث، فهو مذهبي " ، وقد حكى ذلك الإمام ابن عبد البر عن أبي حنيفة وغيره من الأئمة " . اهـ . ونقله أيضاً الإمام الشعراني عن الأئمة الأربعة .

وأقول أيضاً : ينبغى تقييد ذلك بما إذا وافق قولا في المذهب ؛ إذ لم يأذنوا في الاجتهاد فيما خرج عن المذهب بالكلية مما اتفق عليه أئمتنا ؛ لأن اجتهادهم
(۲) قوله : "عمل بالحديث " أى إذا كان صحيحاً صريحاً غير معارض ولا مؤوّل ولم يوجد له ناسخ ولا مخصص ولا مقيد، ولم يطلع على مانع ترك الإمام بسببه هذا الحديث. قال الإمام عمرو بن الصلاح في كتابه "فتاوى ابن الصلاح" : "روينا عن الشافعى رضى الله عنه أنه قال : "إذا وجدتم في كتابي خلاف سنة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، فقولوا بسنة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، ودعوا ما قلته " . وهذا وما هو في معناه مشهور عنه . وليس هذا بالهين، فليس كل فقيه يسوغ له أن يستقل بالعمل بما يراه حجة من الحديث ؛ لإمكان أن الشافعى تركه عمداً على علم منه بصحته لمانع اطلع عليه وخفى على غيره، ككونه منسوخا عنده أو غير ذلك" . انتهى بتلخيص وتغيير . (٥٣/١-٥٥) وفى "الكفاية شرح "الهداية " : "قال الإمام أبو يوسف رحمه الله تعالى : العامي إذا سمع حديثا ليس له أن يأخذ بظاهره ؛ لجواز أن يكون مصروفا عن ظاهره أو منسوخا . (٢٩٤/٣)

If the hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab. (Attributed to) Abu Hanifa, and thus it is attributed to him and not others. I said: I used to find this problematic, and I answered it in my gloss 'Radd al-Muhtar 'ala al-Durr al-Mukhtar': because when the Imam ordered his companions to take from his statements that which they find evidence for, their statements became his statements, because they are based on his principles that he established for them. So it is not a complete abandonment of his view. 

Section: If the hadith is authentic, it is the madhhab of Abu Hanifa with two conditions. 

And similar to this is what al-Allama al-Bayri transmitted in the beginning of his commentary on al-Ashbah from Sharh al-Hidayah of Ibn al-Shihna al-Kabir, father of the commentator of al-Wahbaniyya and teacher of Ibn al-Humam. Its text: If the hadith is authentic and contrary to the madhhab, act by the hadith, and that becomes his madhhab. And his follower does not cease to be a Hanafi by acting upon it. It has been authentically reported from Abu Hanifa that he said: 'If the hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab.' Imam Ibn Abd al-Barr reported this from Abu Hanifa and other imams. End quote. It was also transmitted by Imam al-Sha'rani from the four imams. 

I said: It is obvious that this is for one who is qualified to examine the texts and know their unqualified from abrogated. So when the people of the madhhab examine the evidence and act upon it, its attribution to the madhhab is valid because it originates with the permission of the founder of the madhhab; there is no doubt that if he knew the weakness of his evidence, he would retract it and follow the stronger evidence. Hence, the muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humam objected to the mashayikh who gave fatwa with the opinion of the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) that one does not deviate from the Imam's opinion except due to the weakness of his evidence. 

And I also say: It should be restricted to when it agrees with a statement in the madhhab; because they did not permit ijtihad in matters completely outside the madhhab that our imams have agreed upon; for their ijtihad... 

(Footnote 2): His statement 'act by the hadith' means if it is authentic, clear, not opposed or interpreted, and there is no abrogator, specificator, or restrictor, and no obstacle is known why the Imam left this hadith. Imam Amr ibn al-Salah said in his book Fatawa Ibn al-Salah: We have narrated from al-Shafi'i that he said: 'If you find in my book something contrary to the Sunnah of the Messenger of Allah, then follow the Sunnah and leave what I said.' This and what is similar is well-known from him. But this is not easy; not every jurist is permitted to independently act upon what he considers a proof from hadith, because it is possible that al-Shafi'i deliberately left it despite knowing its authenticity due to an obstacle he knew that is hidden from others, such as it being abrogated in his view or something else.' End quote with summary and modification. (1/53-55) In al-Kifaya sharh al-Hidaya: Imam Abu Yusuf said: The layman, if he hears a hadith, is not allowed to take its apparent meaning; because it may be diverted from its apparent meaning or abrogated. (3/294) 

(Footnote 1): His statement 'except due to the weakness of his evidence' because if he knew the weakness of his evidence, he would have retracted his opinion, so it is permissible for us also to deviate from his opinion then, and this is not considered leaving the madhhab because it originates from permission of the founder. Similarly, it is permissible to deviate from his opinion due to change of era and time or...
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational rasm al-mufti rule: the famous maxim 'If a hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab' attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa. The commentary begins by addressing a common problem: how can the statements of the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) be considered part of the madhhab when they sometimes contradict Abu Hanifa? The author explains that Abu Hanifa authorized his students to adopt whatever evidence they found strongest, and thus their rulings are based on his principles, not a complete departure. The passage then introduces a new section specifying that this maxim applies with two conditions (though the exact conditions are not enumerated here, the text elaborates on them). It cites al-Bayri's transmission from Ibn al-Shihna: if a sahih hadith contradicts the madhhab, one acts on the hadith and it becomes the madhhab, and the follower remains a Hanafi. This is supported by reports from Ibn Abd al-Barr and al-Sha'rani. The author ('I') then clarifies that this rule is only for those qualified to examine texts and distinguish abrogated from unqualified. When the people of the madhhab examine and act on evidence, it is validly attributed to the madhhab because the Imam would retract if his evidence were weak. Therefore, the muhaqqiq Ibn al-Humam objected to those who issue fatwa with the Sahibayn's view over Abu Hanifa's without showing the Imam's evidence is weak. Finally, the author adds an important restriction: this departure from the apparent madhhab is only allowed if the new ruling agrees with some statement within the madhhab, because ijtihad beyond the madhhab's agreed principles is not permitted. The passage includes two lengthy footnotes: one explaining the conditions of the hadith (sahih, clear, without opposition, abrogation, specification, or restriction, and no hidden obstacle) citing Ibn al-Salah, and a quote from Abu Yusuf that a layman cannot take a hadith's apparent meaning without knowledge. The second footnote elaborates that deviation from the Imam's opinion is permissible when his evidence is weak, and also due to change of era and time.

Key Terms
إذا صح الحديث فهو مذهبي (idha sahha al-hadithu fahuwa madhhabi): A maxim indicating that if an authentic hadith contradicts the apparent ruling of the madhhab, the hadith is to be followed and is considered the true madhhab of the imam.
مقلد (muqallid): A follower of a madhhab who does not perform independent ijtihad but follows the rulings of the mujtahid imam.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The hadith must be sahih, clear, and free from abrogation or contradiction.
B) Any layperson may act on it if it seems authentic.
C) It must be reported by Ibn Abd al-Barr.
D) It must agree with the opinion of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'If a hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab' permits departure from the transmitted madhhab only when the hadith is clear, unconditional, and the mufti is qualified, and the new ruling aligns with a statement within the school.

Four Categories Rulings Not Outside Hanafi Madhhab
Rulings on fatwa methodology: four categories inside madhhab and the role of hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four categories of rulings that remain within the Hanafi madhhab even when differing from Imam Abu Hanifa's explicit statements, justify their inclusion using the hypothetical reasoning, and correctly phrase fatwas for such rulings.

Core Idea
Rulings from custom, necessity, as-hab, and takhrij remain Hanafi because they follow the Imam's method, not his isolated words.

The Hanafi madhhab is not limited to the explicit statements of Imam Abu Hanifa. Later scholars developed a framework allowing the madhhab to adapt to new circumstances while remaining faithful to its core principles. This passage from 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti explains four types of rulings—statements of the companions (as-hab), issues based on custom, necessity, and takhrijat (derivations)—that are fully part of the madhhab. It also clarifies the role of authentic hadith in unaddressed issues and provides guidance on how a mufti should phrase his fatwas to maintain precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four categories
The passage lists four types of rulings that are not outside the Hanafi madhhab: (1) statements of the companions of the Imam (as-hab), (2) issues built on custom (urf), (3) issues built on necessity (darurah), and (4) takhrijat by the mashayikh based on the Imam's principles. Each category allows the madhhab to address new situations while remaining within its framework.
The as-hab sometimes differed from the Imam, but their views are considered part of the madhhab.
2
Understand the hypothetical reasoning
The justification for accepting these rulings is that if Imam Abu Hanifa were alive and faced the same custom or necessity, he would have adopted the same ruling. This reasoning is also applied to takhrijat and as-hab's views, as long as they align with his principles.
This reasoning preserves the madhhab's coherence while allowing evolution.
3
Address the objection about authentic hadith
A potential objection is raised: why are custom and necessity given broader scope than authentic hadith? The passage responds emphatically that authentic hadith is equally part of the madhhab. However, it specifies that hadith can only be used to derive rulings for issues that are not explicitly addressed (maskut 'anhu) and must meet strict conditions (authenticity, no opposition, no abrogation, etc.).
The restriction on hadith applies only to already-addressed issues; it is not a downgrade of hadith.
4
Apply the precaution in phrasing fatwas
When issuing a fatwa using derived rulings (custom, necessity, takhrij, as-hab), the mufti should not say 'Abu Hanifa said such-and-such' unless there is an explicit report. Instead, he should phrase it as 'the requirement of the madhhab of Abu Hanifa is such-and-such'.
This preserves the integrity of the Imam's transmitted statements.

Source Text
