ا حكم التخريجات وأقوال الأصحاب
شرح عقود رسم المفتی مر فى مسألة الاستئجار على تعليم القرآن ونحوه من الطاعات التي في ترك الاستئجار عليها ضياع الدين، كما قررناه سابقا ، فحينئذ يجوز الإفتاء بخلاف قولهم، كما نذكره قريبا عن "الحاوى القدسى " ، وسيأتي بسطه أيضا آخر الشرح عند الكلام على العرف .
مطلب
أقوال أصحاب الإمام ، والمسائل المبنية على العرف والضرورة ، وتخريجات المشايخ على قواعد الإمام
غير خارجة عن المذهب الحنفى
والحاصل: أن ما خالف فيها الأصحاب إمامهم الأعظم، لا يخرج
لأنه لو حدثت هذه الضرورة في زمانه أو أدرك هذا العرف الحادث، لذهب إلى ما ذهب إليه المتأخرون .
فإن قلت : فهل نزل الحديث عن مرتبة الضرورة والعرف حتى اعتبروها مسوغا للأخذ بما خالف المذهب ، ولم يعتبروا الحديث الصحيح كذلك، حتى قيدوا الثابت بالحديث أن يوافق قولا في المذهب ولم يقيدوا ما اقتضته الضرورة والعرف؟ قلت : حاشا أن يكون الأمر كذلك، بل ما ثبت بالحديث يكون أيضا جزء للمذهب الحنفى ولو لم يوافق قولا في المذهب، وهذا ؛ لأن المعنى الصحيح لقولهم : إذا صح الحديث فهو مذهبي " أن هذا طريقة اجتهادهم، وأن المسائل التي لم يتكلم فيها المتقدمون وبقيت مسكوتة عنها في كتب المذهب، إذا وجد فيها حديث، يؤخذ به ويعمل عليه، بشرط أن يكون الحديث صحيحًا غير معارض ولا مؤوّل، ولم يوجد له ناسخ ولا مخصص ولا مقيد، ونظر في سنده و متنه من هو أهل للنظر . فعلى هذا ما ثبت بالحديث الصحيح في المسائل المسكونة عنها يكون داخلا في المذهب الحنفى أيضا ؛ لأن الإمام لو كان حيا واطلع على هذا الحديث، لعمل عليه وحكم بموجبه، فافهم وتدبّر .
شرح عقود رسم المفتی ا حكم التخريجات وأقوال الأصحاب عن مذهبه إذا رجحه المشايخ المعتبرون، وكذا ما بناه المشايخ على العرف الحادث لتغيّر الزمان أو للضرورة ونحو ذلك ، لا يخرج عن مذهبه أيضاً ؛ لأن ما رجحوه لترجح دليله عندهم مأذون به من جهة الإمام، وكذا ما بنوه على تغيّر الزمان والضرورة، باعتبار أنه لو كان حيا لقال بما قالوه ؛ لأن ما قالوه إنما هو مبنى على قواعده أيضاً ، فهو مقتضى مذهبه .
لكن ينبغي أن لا يقال : قال أبو حنيفة كذا ، إلا فيما روى عنه صريحاً، وإنما يقال فيه : "مقتضى مذهب أبي حنيفة كذا"، كما قلنا ومثله تخريجات المشايخ بعض الأحكام من قواعده ، أو بالقياس على قوله، ومنه
The ruling of takhrijat and the statements of the companions. Explanation of 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: It passed in the issue of hiring for teaching the Qur'an and similar acts of obedience where abandoning hiring for them causes loss of religion, as we previously established. So then it is permissible to give fatwa contrary to their statement, as we will mention soon from 'al-Hawi al-Qudsi'. And its expansion will also come at the end of the commentary when discussing custom. A request: The statements of the companions of the Imam, and issues based on custom and necessity, and takhrijat of the mashayikh on the principles of the Imam are not outside the Hanafi madhhab. The conclusion: That in which the companions (as-hab) differed from their greatest Imam does not leave (the madhhab), because if this necessity had occurred in his time or he had perceived this new custom, he would have gone to what the later scholars went to. If you say: So has the hadith descended from the rank of necessity and custom such that they considered it a justification for adopting what contradicts the madhhab, and they did not consider the authentic hadith likewise, even they restricted what is established by hadith to agreeing with a statement in the madhhab and did not restrict what is required by necessity and custom? I say: Far be it that the matter be so. Rather, what is established by hadith is also part of the Hanafi madhhab even if it does not agree with a statement in the madhhab. This is because the correct meaning of their statement: "When the hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab" is that this is their method of ijtihad, and that issues which the earlier scholars did not discuss and remained unspoken in the books of the madhhab, if a hadith is found concerning them, it is taken and acted upon, on condition that the hadith is authentic, without opposition or allegorical interpretation, and there is no abrogator, no specifier, nor restrictor for it, and those qualified for consideration have examined its chain and text. So upon this, what is established by authentic hadith in the issues that are unaddressed is also included within the Hanafi madhhab, because if the Imam were alive and had come across this hadith, he would have acted upon it and judged by it. So understand and reflect. Explanation of 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: The ruling of takhrijat and the statements of the companions regarding his madhhab: when the trustworthy mashayikh have preferred it, likewise what the mashayikh built upon a new custom due to change of time or necessity and the like, it does not leave his madhhab also, because what they preferred due to its evidence being stronger in their view is permitted by the Imam. Likewise what they built upon change of time and necessity, considering that if he were alive he would say what they said, because what they said is only based on his principles too, so it is a requirement of his madhhab. But it should not be said: "Abu Hanifa said such-and-such" except in what is explicitly reported from him. Rather, it should be said: "The requirement of Abu Hanifa's madhhab is such-and-such" as we said. And similar to this is the takhrijat of the mashayikh of some rulings from his principles, or by analogy to his statement, and from it (is derived).
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on fatwa methodology regarding the scope of the Hanafi madhhab. The author clarifies that four categories of rulings remain part of the Hanafi school even when they appear to contradict the direct statements of Imam Abu Hanifa: (1) the rulings of the companions of the Imam (as-hab) who sometimes differed from him, (2) issues built on custom (urf), (3) issues built on necessity (darurah), and (4) takhrijat (derivations) by the mashayikh based on the Imam's principles. The reasoning is that if Imam Abu Hanifa were alive and faced the same custom or necessity, he would have agreed with those rulings. The passage then addresses a potential objection: why is authentic hadith not given the same weight as custom and necessity as a justification for departing from the madhhab? The author refutes this, explaining that authentic hadith is indeed part of the Hanafi madhhab when it concerns issues not explicitly addressed in the madhhab; the principle 'when the hadith is authentic, it is my madhhab' applies to such unaddressed cases, subject to conditions (authenticity, no contradiction, no abrogation). Therefore, rulings based on authentic hadith in silent issues are also within the madhhab. The author then notes a practical precaution: when referring to such rulings, one should not attribute them directly to Abu Hanifa ("Abu Hanifa said") but rather say "the requirement of Abu Hanifa's madhhab is such-and-such," except when there is an explicit report from him. This distinction preserves the integrity of the Imam's transmitted statements while allowing the madhhab to adapt.

Key Terms
تخريج (takhrij): Derivation of rulings from the principles and statements of Imam Abu Hanifa for issues not explicitly addressed, or expansion of an ambiguous statement with two possible interpretations.
مسكوت عنه (maskut 'anhu): An issue not explicitly found in the early Hanafi texts, but which may be addressed through ijtihad based on authentic hadith or qiyas.
أصحاب (as-hab): Companions of Imam Abu Hanifa, primarily his senior students like Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, whose views may differ from the Imam but are still part of the madhhab.
عرف (urf): Custom: established practice in a society that can influence fiqh rulings, especially when no explicit text exists or when applying the rule would cause hardship.
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity: a situation where failure to take a certain action would lead to harm or loss of religion, life, etc., allowing departure from the normal ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition must authentic hadith meet to be used for deriving a ruling in a silent issue (maskut 'anhu)?
A) The hadith must agree with an existing statement in the madhhab.
B) The hadith must be authentic, without opposition or allegorical interpretation, and with no abrogator, specifier, or restrictor.
C) The hadith must be from the six major hadith collections.
D) The hadith must be approved by a living mujtahid.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab is not static; it includes derived rulings from custom, necessity, as-hab, takhrij, and authentic hadith in unaddressed issues, provided they align with the Imam's principles and are phrased correctly.

Two Additional Categories of Hanafi Rulings
Adds silent issues and necessity-based fatwa from other madhhabs as Hanafi categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two additional categories of Hanafi rulings (maskut 'anhu and necessity-based fatwa with another madhhab) and apply the conditions that keep such a fatwa within the Hanafi madhhab.

Core Idea
The Hanafi madhhab accommodates new cases and other schools under necessity without losing its identity.

This passage expands the traditional categories of Hanafi rulings by adding two more: silent issues consistent with Hanafi principles, and fatwas from other madhhabs under severe need. It shows how the madhhab remains dynamic yet principled.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two new categories
The first category is 'maskut 'anhu' – issues not explicitly regulated in Hanafi sources but consistent with Hanafi principles. The second is necessity-based fatwa from another madhhab due to severe need or widespread affliction.
2
Understand the condition for the first category
Such silent issues must be consistent with Hanafi qawa'id (principles). The author gives an example from al-Durr al-Mukhtar: irrigation with purchased water, which is not explicitly addressed but allowed because it aligns with Hanafi principles.
3
Understand the condition for the second category
The mufti must have severe need (haja shadida) or widespread affliction (umum al-balwa). He must also observe four conditions (not detailed here, but referenced from later discussion).
4
Grasp the consequence
A fatwa given under these conditions is not completely outside the Hanafi madhhab; it is considered part of the madhhab 'in some respects.'
5
Note the status of the Maliki madhhab
The author cites that the Maliki madhhab is considered closest to Abu Hanifa, and scholars like Abu al-Layth al-Samarqandi recommend turning to it when no Hanafi statement exists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلت : وينبغى أن يزاد عليه نوعان آخران : الأول : ما كان مسكوتا عنه في المذهب، مبحوثا عنه في المذاهب الأخرى، موافقاً لقواعد مذهبنا ومثل هذه المسائل مذكورة في الدر المختار " في مواضع شتى، كقوله في باب العشر : "أو سقاه بماء اشتراه وقواعدنا لا تأباه . (٣٩٥/٢)
الثاني : إذا احتاج المفتى إلى الإفتاء بمذهب الغير لحاجة شديدة أو عموم البلوى، وراعي فيه الشرائط الأربعة التي ذكرها الفقهاء وسيأتى فى هذه الحاشية عند البحث على القضاء بمذهب الغير - فما أفتى به في هذه الحالة لا يكون خارجا عن المذهب بالكلية، بل يعد من المذهب من وجه . قال المصنف في حاشيته على "الدر المختار في باب الرجعة فالأولى الجمع بين المذهبين ؛ لأن مالكا كالتلميذ لأبي حنيفة، ولذا مال أصحابنا إلى بعض أقواله ضرورة، كما في ديباجة المصفى قهستاني وفي حاشية الفتال وذكر الفقيه أبو الليث في تأسيس النظائر " أنه إذا لم يوجد في مذهب الإمام قول في مسألة ، يرجع إلى مذهب مالك ؛ لأنه أقرب المذاهب إليه . (٤١١/٣)
I say: It is fitting to add two other types: The first: That which is unspoken in the madhhab, researched in other madhhabs, and consistent with the principles of our madhhab. Such issues are mentioned in al-Durr al-Mukhtar in various places, like his statement in the chapter of 'ushr: "Or he irrigated it with water he purchased; and our principles do not reject it." (2/395) The second: When the mufti needs to give fatwa with another madhhab due to severe need or widespread affliction, and he observes the four conditions mentioned by the jurists - which will come in this footnote when discussing judging by another madhhab - then what he gives fatwa with in this situation is not completely outside the madhhab; rather, it is considered from the madhhab in some respects. The author said in his footnote on al-Durr al-Mukhtar in the chapter of raj'a: "So it is better to combine the two madhhabs, because Malik is like a student of Abu Hanifa, therefore our companions inclined to some of his statements out of necessity, as in the preface of al-Musaffa al-Qahistani and in the footnote of al-Fattal. And al-Faqih Abu al-Layth mentioned in Ta'sis al-Nazair that if there is no statement in the madhhab of the Imam on an issue, one should turn to the madhhab of Malik, because it is the closest madhhab to him." (3/411)
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the boundaries of the Hanafi madhhab, adding two more categories to the four already discussed. The first category covers issues that are not explicitly addressed in the Hanafi texts (maskut 'anhu) but are debated in other madhhabs; however, if they agree with Hanafi principles (qawa'id), they can be adopted. The author gives an example from al-Durr al-Mukhtar regarding irrigation with purchased water: the ruling is not explicitly found but is consistent with Hanafi principles. The second category addresses cases of extreme need (haja shadida) or widespread calamity (umum al-balwa) where a mufti must give a fatwa according to another madhhab. He must satisfy four conditions (to be discussed later). Such a fatwa, though from another school, is still considered part of the Hanafi madhhab 'in some respects' because necessity makes it acceptable, and Imam Malik's madhhab is considered closest to Abu Hanifa's. The author cites various sources supporting this, including that Malik is like a student of Abu Hanifa, so recourse to his madhhab is justified. The overall principle is that the Hanafi madhhab is not rigid but accommodates new situations through established rules, provided the core principles are not violated.

Key Terms
مسكوت عنه (maskut 'anhu): An issue that is not explicitly regulated in the early Hanafi sources but is addressed in other madhhabs and is compatible with Hanafi principles.
عموم البلوى (umum al-balwa): A widespread situation affecting many people, making strict adherence to the original ruling difficult and justifying recourse to another madhhab.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Hanbali madhhab
C) Maliki madhhab
D) Zahiri madhhab

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi madhhab recognizes two additional categories: silent issues consistent with its principles and necessity-based fatwas from other schools, both remaining within the madhhab's framework.

Hierarchy of Rulings and Attribution in Hanafi Ifta
Defining the hierarchy of derived rulings and their proper attribution to the madhhab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the hierarchy of rulings in the Hanafi madhhab and apply the proper attribution (muqtada al-madhhab vs. explicit statement) when issuing a fatwa.

Core Idea
Takhrijat on Abu Hanifa's principles are closer to his madhhab than the statements of his companions.

In the Hanafi school, not every ruling attributed to the madhhab comes directly from Abu Hanifa. Many are derived from his principles (takhrijat) or from his companions' opinions. This lesson unpacks the hierarchical relationship between these sources and provides guidance on how a mufti should attribute rulings correctly, ensuring the integrity of the madhhab's methodology.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish explicit statements from derivations
Identify that 'قال أبو حنيفة' is only used for explicitly transmitted statements. Derivations are termed 'مقتضى مذهب أبي حنيفة' (the requirement of his madhhab).
This is a key terminological rule for fatwa writing.
2
Recognize the sources of derived rulings
List the categories: preferred opinions of muhaqqiqun, rulings built on custom or necessity, and takhrijat on the Imam's principles and statements.
The text adds two more: issues from other madhhabs consistent with Hanafi principles, and fatwas from other madhhabs under necessity.
3
Compare takhrijat and aqwal al-as-hab
Understand the principle that takhrijat are closer to Abu Hanifa's madhhab than the statements of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad because takhrijat are built on his principles, while the companions sometimes departed from them.
This hierarchy is crucial for tarjih (preference) when conflicting views exist.
4
Apply the rule to a case
Given a scenario (e.g., a new issue), determine whether to attribute the ruling as a direct statement or as muqtada al-madhhab, and which source takes precedence.
Prioritize: explicit text > takhrij > aqwal al-as-hab.

Commentary on 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: The ruling of takhrijat (derived rulings) and the statements of the companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) regarding his madhhab: When the reliable scholars give weight to a ruling, and similarly what the scholars build upon emergent custom due to change of time or necessity and the like, it does not exit from his madhhab either; because what they gave weight to due to the strength of its evidence in their view is authorized by the Imam, and similarly what they built upon change of time and necessity, on the consideration that if he were alive he would say what they said; because what they said is also built upon his principles, so it is the requirement of his madhhab. But it should not be said: 'Abu Hanifa said such-and-such' except in what is explicitly narrated from him. Rather, one should say: 'The requirement of the madhhab of Abu Hanifa is such-and-such.' Similarly, the takhrijat of the scholars deriving some rulings from his principles, or by analogy to his statement, falls under this. And from it: the preferred opinions, rulings built on custom, rulings built on necessity, and takhrijat of the scholars on the Imam's principles and statements. I say: Two other types should be added: First: what is not spoken of in the madhhab but researched in other madhhabs, agreeing with our madhhab's principles. Such issues are mentioned in al-Durr al-Mukhtar in various places, like his statement in the chapter of 'Ushr: 'or he irrigated with water he bought, and our principles do not reject it.' Second: when the mufti needs to give fatwa according to another madhhab due to severe need or widespread difficulty, and he observes the four conditions mentioned by the jurists (and will come later in this gloss on the discussion of judging by another madhhab) — then what he gives fatwa with in this case is not entirely outside the madhhab, but is considered part of the madhhab from one aspect. The author said in his gloss on al-Durr al-Mukhtar in the chapter of Raj'a: 'The best is to combine the two madhhabs, because Malik is like a student of Abu Hanifa, and therefore our scholars inclined to some of his statements out of necessity, as in the preface of al-Mustafa al-Qahistani and in the gloss of al-Fattal.' And al-Faqih Abu al-Layth mentioned in Ta'sis al-Naza'ir that if there is no statement in the Imam's madhhab on an issue, one refers to the madhhab of Malik because it is the closest of the madhhabs to him. (Footnote 1: His statement: 'And similarly the takhrijat of the scholars deriving some rulings from his principles'—it is understood from this that takhrij has two meanings: detailing an ambiguous statement that has two aspects, and branching off from the Imam's principles and statements in issues not explicitly addressed. The second type is found in Fath al-Qadir at the statement of the author of al-Hidayah: 'And if a junub immerses himself in the well to retrieve the bucket...' his text: 'This...' Grade of takhrijat and statements of the companions. Their statement: 'And by analogy to his statement, such-and-such is so.' All of this should not say: 'Abu Hanifa said,' but rather it is correct to call it his madhhab in the sense that it is the statement of the people of his madhhab, or the requirement of his madhhab. And because of this, when the author of al-Durar wa al-Ghurar said in the chapter of Judging: 'If a judge rules in a matter of ijtihad contrary to his madhhab, it does not take effect'—he said: 'That is, the original madhhab, like a Hanafi judging according to the Shafi'i madhhab or similar, or vice versa. But if a Hanafi judges according to the madhhab of Abu Yusuf or Muhammad or similar from the Imam's companions, it is not a ruling contrary to his opinion.' (Footnote 2: His statement: 'contrary to his madhhab'—the ba' is related to 'qada al-qadi', as in al-Durar.) (Footnote 3: His statement: 'it is not a ruling contrary to his opinion'—because the Imam's companions did not say a statement except that the Imam had also said it.) Section: Takhrijat of the scholars on the Imam's principles are closer to his madhhab than the statements of the companions. And it is apparent that the attribution of the derived issues to his madhhab is closer than the attribution of issues stated by Abu Yusuf or Muhammad to him, because the derived ones are built on his principles and fundamentals, whereas the issues stated by Abu Yusuf and similar from the Imam's companions are often built on principles of their own in which they opposed the Imam's principles; because they did not adhere to all his principles, as known by one familiar with the books of usul. The issue which Abu Bakr al-Razi extracted from the disagreement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the cause for using water is from this. (Page 79/3) (Footnote 1: 'His statement: 'contrary to his madhhab'—the ba' is attached to 'qada al-qadi', as in al-Durar.) (Footnote 2: 'He said: i.e., the author of al-Ghurarr in his commentary.) (Footnote 3: 'His statement: 'it is not a ruling contrary to his opinion'—because the Imam's companions did not say a statement except that the Imam had also said it.)

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti is a detailed rasm al-mufti discussion on the hierarchy and proper attribution of rulings within the Hanafi madhhab. It opens by establishing that when later reliable scholars prefer a ruling or derive rulings based on custom, necessity, or on the Imam's principles, these rulings are still considered part of the madhhab, because they are built upon his foundations. However, the author emphasizes a crucial distinction in attribution: one should only say 'Abu Hanifa said' for what is explicitly transmitted from him. For derivations and analogical extensions, the proper phrase is 'the requirement of the madhhab of Abu Hanifa is such-and-such.' The margin adds two more categories: (1) issues not found in the Hanafi madhhab but researched in other madhhabs that are consistent with Hanafi principles (e.g., a ruling in al-Durr al-Mukhtar about irrigating with purchased water), and (2) cases where a mufti must give fatwa from another madhhab due to severe need, provided the four conditions are met—such fatwas are not entirely outside the Hanafi madhhab. The text then cites the opinion that when no Hanafi ruling exists, one should refer to Malik's madhhab as it is closest to Abu Hanifa. On page 102, the discussion sharpens: takhrijat—derivations on the Imam's principles—are closer to the madhhab than the direct statements of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, because the latter often depart from Abu Hanifa's principles in some areas. This is a key principle for muftis in determining which rulings most truly represent the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
التخريجات (al-takhrijat): Derived rulings that are extracted from the Imam's principles or by analogy to his statements, either to clarify an ambiguous statement or to address issues not explicitly mentioned in the madhhab.
مقتضى المذهب (muqtada al-madhhab): The requirement of the madhhab based on its principles and foundations, as opposed to an explicit statement from the Imam.
أقوال الأصحاب (aqwal al-as-hab): The statements of the companions of Abu Hanifa, primarily Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, which may sometimes contradict the Imam's principles.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, which is considered closer to Abu Hanifa's madhhab: a takhrij derived from his principles or a direct statement from his companion Abu Yusuf?
A) The direct statement from Abu Yusuf
B) The takhrij derived from the Imam's principles
C) Both are equally close
D) Neither; only explicit statements from Abu Hanifa himself count

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi madhhab, derived rulings (takhrijat) on Abu Hanifa's principles are more representative of his school than the opinions of his companions, and should be attributed as 'muqtada al-madhhab' rather than direct statements.

Takhrijat Closer to Imam's Madhhab than Companions' Statements
Explaining why takhrijat based on imam's principles are closer to his madhhab than direct statements of his students.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule that takhrijat based on Imam Abu Hanifa's principles are preferred over aqwal al-ashab when conflicting, and explain the reasoning using qawida and furu.

Core Idea
Takhrijat built on the Imam's principles are closer to his madhhab than his companions' statements.

In the Hanafi school, not every statement attributed to Abu Hanifa's immediate students carries equal weight. This passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti establishes a critical hierarchy: when a late scholar derives a ruling (takhrij) from the Imam's principles, that ruling is more representative of his madhhab than a direct statement of a companion that contradicts those principles. Understanding this rule is essential for muftis and judges when choosing between competing opinions within the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify source of ruling
Determine whether the ruling at hand is a direct statement from a companion (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, etc.) or a takhrij derived later from the Imam's principles.
2
Evaluate alignment with Imam's principles
Check if the companion statement aligns with or deviates from the well-known principles of Imam Abu Hanifa. Companion statements may be based on their own ijtihad and may contradict the Imam's usul.
3
Apply the tarjih rule
If the companion statement conflicts with the Imam's principles, prefer the takhrij that is built on those principles, as it is closer to the Imam's madhhab.
This rule prioritizes consistency with the foundational usul.
4
Consider judicial enforcement
A Hanafi judge may enforce a ruling based on a correct takhrij just as he would enforce a correct companion statement. The judgment is valid and not considered contrary to the madhhab.

The degree of derivations (takhrijat) and the statements of the companions: their statement: 'and on the analogy of his saying such and such, it would be such and such' — all of this one does not say about it: 'Abu Hanifa said.' Yes, it is correct to call it his madhhab in the sense that it is the statement of the people of his madhhab, or the implication of his madhhab. And because of this, when the author of al-Durar wa al-Ghurar said in the Book of Judgments: 'If the judge judges in a matter subject to ijtihad contrary to his madhhab, it is not executed' — he said: 'Meaning the root of the madhhab, like the Hanafi if he judges according to the madhhab of al-Shafi'i or similar, or vice versa. But if the Hanafi judges according to the madhhab of Abu Yusuf, or Muhammad, or similar from the companions of the Imam, it is not a judgment contrary to his view.' Topical heading: The derivations of the scholars based on the Imam's principles are closer to his madhhab than the statements of the companions. And it is apparent that the attribution of derived issues to his madhhab is closer than the attribution of issues that Abu Yusuf or Muhammad stated to him; because the derived ones are based on his principles and foundations. As for the issues that Abu Yusuf and his like from the companions of the Imam stated, many of them are based on principles of their own in which they opposed the Imam's principles; because they did not adhere to all his principles, as is known to one who has knowledge of the books of usul. The issue that Abu Bakr al-Razi derived from the disagreement of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad regarding the cause for using water from it (79/3). (1) His saying: 'contrary to his madhhab' — the ba' is attached to his saying 'the judge judged', as in al-Durar. (2) His saying: 'he said' i.e., the author of al-Ghurar in his commentary. (3) His saying: 'it is not a judgment contrary to his view' because the companions of the Imam did not say any statement except that the Imam had said it. Yes, it may be said: if their statements are narrations from him as passed, then those principles are also for him; because those statements are based on them. And on this basis, the attribution of derivations to his madhhab is closer; because they are based on his principles that he preferred and built his statements upon. So if the judge judges with what is correct from them, his judgment is executed, just as it is executed with what is correct from the statements of the companions. This is what appeared to me to set forth in this chapter from Fath al-Malik al-Wahhab, and Allah Almighty knows best what is correct, and to Him is the return and the abode.
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti is a rasm rule concerning the ranking of legal opinions within the Hanafi school. It opens by clarifying the terminology: when later jurists say 'on the analogy of his saying it would be such,' this should not be directly attributed to Imam Abu Hanifa, but it can be called his madhhab in the sense that it is the stated view of his followers or the logical consequence of his principles. The author then cites a passage from al-Durar wa al-Ghurar: if a Hanafi judge rules in a matter of ijtihad contrary to his madhhab, his judgment is void. But this applies only if he rules according to an entirely different school, like the Shafi'i school. If he rules according to the view of Abu Yusuf or Muhammad, that is not considered contrary to his madhhab because those opinions are still part of the Hanafi school. The passage then presents the main rule: takhrijat (derivations) by later scholars based on the Imam's established principles are closer to his madhhab than the direct statements of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad. The reasoning is that these derivations are built on the Imam's own rules and usul, while many of Abu Yusuf's and Muhammad's statements are based on their own principles where they deviated from the Imam's. Therefore, when a judge rules based on a valid takhrij, his judgment is enforceable just as if he ruled based on a correct statement of a companion. This establishes a hierarchy: well-founded takhrijat take precedence over the individual opinions of the companions that contradict the Imam's principles.

Key Terms
تخريجات (takhrijat): Legal rulings derived by later Hanafi scholars by extrapolating from Imam Abu Hanifa's established principles and rules, even if not explicitly stated by him.
أقوال الأصحاب (aqwal al-ashab): The direct statements and rulings of Imam Abu Hanifa's prominent students, particularly Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani, Zufar, and Ibn Ziyad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) It is invalid because it is not a direct statement of the Imam.
B) It is enforceable just like a ruling based on a correct statement of a companion.
C) It is weaker and should not be followed.
D) It is only valid if Abu Yusuf also agreed with it.

Key Takeaway
Takhrijat based on the Imam's principles are considered closer to his madhhab than the statements of his companions when those statements deviate from his usul.

Hierarchy of Opinions When Imam's Choice is Unknown
Setting forth the hierarchy of opinions when the imam's own preference is unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the default hierarchy of Hanafi opinions (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, Ibn Ziyad) and apply the rule that a mujtahid mufti may prefer the stronger evidenciary opinion.

Core Idea
When Abu Hanifa's choice is unknown, follow Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, but a mujtahid mufti may prefer stronger proof.

This poem sets out a foundational rule for muftis: when the Imam's own preferred opinion (ikhtiyar) is not recorded, the default order is the statements of his senior students. However, a later view—deemed most correct for a mujtahid—permits preferring the opinion with stronger evidence. Understanding this hierarchy prevents blind adherence and allows proper tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default hierarchy
Lines 26-27 state: when Abu Hanifa's choice is unknown, adopt the statement of Abu Yusuf (Ya'qub), then Muhammad al-Shaybani, then Zufar, then Ibn Ziyad. This is the starting point for any mufti.
This hierarchy is based on the students' closeness to the Imam and their mastery of his principles.
2
Recognize the ikhtilaf when Sahibayn oppose the Imam
Line 28 introduces a disagreement: if both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad disagree with the Imam, some scholars say the mufti has free choice (takhyir) among their opinions.
Free choice means any of the companion opinions may be followed without preference.
3
Apply the stronger evidence rule for mujtahids
Line 29 presents an alternative: the mufti should prefer the opinion whose evidence (dalil) is stronger. This is said to be the most correct (al-ashah) for a mufti capable of ijtihad.
This view allows departure from the rigid hierarchy when the mujtahid can weigh proofs.
4
Understand the commentary on takhrijat
The footnote explains that takhrijat (derivations by later scholars) can be closer to the Imam's madhhab than the companions' statements, because they are built on preponderant principles of the Imam.
This nuance shows that not all companion statements carry equal weight; methodology matters.

(Y) - The Imam. (1) His saying: 'those principles are also for him' — i.e., how can the derivations of the scholars be closer to the Imam's madhhab than the statements of the companions? And he answered it with his saying: 'and on this basis too... etc.', meaning that the derivations of the scholars are built on the principles that are preponderant according to the Imam, while the statements of the companions are built on principles that are weak according to him, so necessarily the derivations are closer to his madhhab than what the companions said, even if their statements are narrations from him. (2) His saying: 'the most correct is the predicate of his saying: 'this for a mufti who has ijtihad', and the construal of the phrase: and this is the most correct concerning a mufti who is a mujtahid.

Commentary
This passage presents a rasm rule in verse form concerning the hierarchy of opinions within the Hanafi school when the preferred view of Imam Abu Hanifa is not known. The poem establishes: first, the statement of Imam Abu Yusuf (Ya'qub) is adopted; second, the statement of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani; third, the statements of Zufar and Ibn Ziyad. Then it notes two differing views when both Abu Yusuf and Muhammad oppose the Imam: one view says the mufti has free choice (takhyir), another says the mufti should prefer the opinion with the stronger evidence. The latter is considered the more correct position for a mufti who has the ability to weigh evidences (ijtihad). The following footnotes clarify that this rule about takhrijat being closer to the Imam's madhhab is because takhrijat are based on the Imam's preponderant principles, whereas the companions' statements often rely on principles the Imam considered weak.

Key Terms
اختيار (ikhtiyar): The preferred or chosen opinion of Imam Abu Hanifa on a given issue.
الأصح (al-ashah): The most correct opinion according to later Hanafi scholars, used to indicate the relied-upon position in tarjih.
تخريج (takhrij): A derivation of a ruling by later scholars based on the Imam's principles, even if not explicitly narrated from him.

Key Takeaway
The default hierarchy (Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, Ibn Ziyad) applies when Imam's choice is unknown, but a mujtahid mufti may prefer the stronger proof.

The Closer Path: Why Takhrijat May Outweigh Direct Narrations
Explaining why takhrijat may be closer to Imam's madhhab

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the hierarchical weight of takhrijat versus aqwal al-as'hab and apply the rule that sound takhrijat can be used in fatwa and qada.

Core Idea
Takhrijat based on the Imam's principles can be closer to his madhhab than direct statements of his disciples.

In the Hanafi school, the rulings of the immediate disciples (aqwal al-as'hab) are primary sources, but they are not always final. This passage from a rasm al-mufti text explains why takhrijat—rulings derived by later scholars from the Imam's foundational principles—may actually be more representative of his intent. Understanding this hierarchy is crucial for the mufti when confronted with conflicting transmissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two sources compared
The passage contrasts takhrijat (derived rulings) with aqwal al-as'hab (direct statements of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad).
2
Trace the chain back to the Imam
The author argues that the disciples' statements are narrations from Abu Hanifa, but the underlying principles (qawaid) are also his. Takhrijat are based on those preferred principles.
3
Assess the proximity to the madhhab
Because takhrijat are built on the Imam's chosen rules, they may be closer to his true madhhab, even if not directly transmitted.
4
Apply the practical consequence
A judge's execution of a sound takhrij is valid, just as with a direct statement from a disciple. This elevates the status of well-grounded derivations.

نعم قد يقال : إذا كانت أقوالهم روايات عنه على ما مر - تكون تلك القواعد له أيضا (۱)؛ لابتناء تلك الأقوال عليها . وعلى هذا أيضا تكون نسبة التخريجات إلى مذهبه أقرب؛ لابتناءها على قواعده التي رجحها وبنى أقواله عليها. فإذا قضى القاضى بما صح منها، نفذ قضاءه، كما ينفذ بما صح من أقوال الأصحاب. فهذا ما ظهر لى تقريره فى هذا الباب من فتح الملك الوهاب، والله تعالى أعلم بالصواب، وإليه المرجع والمآب.
Yes, it could be said: If their statements are narrations from him, as previously mentioned, then those rules belong to him as well, because those statements are based on them. And likewise, the ratio of takhrijat to his madhhab is closer, because they are based on his rules that he preferred and built his statements upon. So if a judge judges by what is sound from them, his judgment is executed, just as judgments are executed by what is sound from the statements of the companions. This is what became apparent to me to establish in this chapter from Fath al-Malik al-Wahhab. And Allah knows best the truth, and to Him is the return and final abode.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling about the weight of takhrijat (derived rulings) within the Hanafi school. The author addresses an objection: if the statements of the companions (as'hab) are narrations from the Imam, then the rules (qawaid) also belong to him, based on which those statements are built. The author then argues that takhrijat are even closer to the Imam's madhhab because they are based on the principles that the Imam preferred and on which he built his own rulings. This implies that a judge can enforce a takhrij if it is sound, just as he can enforce the companion's direct statement. The passage concludes by submitting the matter to Allah's knowledge.

Key Terms
تخريجات (takhrijat): Rulings derived by later Hanafi scholars from the principles (qawaid) of the Imam, rather than directly transmitted from him.
أقوال الأصحاب (aqwal al-as'hab): Direct statements of the immediate disciples of Abu Hanifa, such as Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.
قواعد (qawa'id): The foundational legal principles that underpin the Imam's rulings, used by later scholars to derive takhrijat.

The Fixed Hierarchy of Madhhab Opinions in Ifta
Rule establishing the hierarchical preference of Hanafi imams' opinions for ifta.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the hierarchical order of Hanafi imams for preferring opinions in ifta, and apply it correctly when given a scenario of disagreement among them.

Core Idea
When the Hanafi imams differ, the non-mujtahid mufti must follow a fixed hierarchy: Abu Hanifa, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar/Hasan.

This passage from Sharh Uqood Rasm al-Mufti establishes one of the most critical rules in Hanafi ifta: how to choose between conflicting opinions within the madhhab. A mufti who is not a mujtahid cannot simply pick whichever opinion seems most appealing; the school imposes a strict order of preference based on the seniority and authority of the founding imams. Understanding this hierarchy is essential for issuing fatwas that are consistent with the madhhab's methodology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the principle when the imams agree
The text reaffirms that matters agreed upon by all Hanafi imams cannot be overridden by any later mujtahid. This establishes the baseline authority of collective consensus within the school.
This principle prevents subjective deviation from established rulings.
2
Determine the first preference when they disagree
When the imams differ, the mufti must first look for a choice (ikhtiyar) of Imam Abu Hanifa. If found, that opinion takes precedence, regardless of whether any companion agrees with him.
This is the foundation of the hierarchy.
3
Follow the descending order if no Abu Hanifa choice exists
If no opinion is recorded from Abu Hanifa, the mufti moves to Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, then Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. The text notes a difference: some rank al-Hasan after Zufar, but the common view treats them equally.
The authority decreases with each step.
4
Note the terminological nuance
Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani refers to Abu Yusuf by his kunya 'Abu Yusuf' except when mentioning him alongside Abu Hanifa, then he says 'Ya'qub from Abu Hanifa'. This was Abu Yusuf's own instruction out of respect.
Understanding this avoids confusion in reading classical Hanafi texts.
5
Distinguish the ruling for mujtahid vs non-mujtahid mufti
The fixed hierarchy applies absolutely to non-mujtahid muftis. For a mujtahid mufti, there is a view allowing him to choose based on strength of evidence. The relied-upon position (al-ashah) is that the fixed order is still preferred for the non-mujtahid.
This balances methodological consistency with the need for ijtihad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حكم مخالفة الصاحبين الإمام
مطلب فى الترتيب بين روايات المذهب ،
والحكم فيما إذا خالف الصاحبان الإمام
قد علمت ما (١) قررناه آنفًا أن ما اتفق عليه أئمتنا، لا يجوز لمجتهد في مذهبهم أن يعدل عنه برأيه ؛ لأن رأيهم أصح.
وأشرت هنا إلى أنهم إذا اختلفوا يقدم ما اختاره أبو حنيفة، سواء وافقه أحد أصحابه أو لا، فإن لم يوجد له اختيار قدم ما اختاره يعقوب، وهو اسم أبي يوسف أكبر أصحاب الإمام، وعادة الإمام محمد أنه يذكر أبايوسف بكنيته إلا إذا ذكر معه أبا حنيفة، فإنه يذكره باسمه العلم، فيقول: "يعقوب عن أبي حنيفة"، وكان ذلك بوصية من أبي يوسف، تأدبا مع شيخه أبي حنيفة - رحمهم الله تعالى جميعا ورحمنا بهم، وأدام بهم النفع إلى يوم القيامة - وحيث لم يوجد لأبي يوسف اختيار ، قدم قول محمد بن الحسن ، أجل أصحاب أبي حنيفة بعد أبي يوسف، ثم بعده يقدم قول زفر والحسن ابن زیاد، فقولهما في رتبة واحدة، لكن عبارة "النهر (٣): ثم بقول الحسن".
وقيل (٣) : إذا خالفه أصحابه وانفرد بقول يتخير المفتى .
(١) قوله : " ما " هكذا في النسخ الثلاثة المختلفة الموجودة عندنا . فليتأمل (٢) قوله : "لكن" عبارة النهر " يعنى أنها تفيد أن رتبة الحسن بعد زفر بخلاف العبارة السابقة، فإنها تدل على أنهما في رتبة واحدة لكن العبارة السابقة هي المشهورة في
الكتب . (مظ)
(٣) قوله : "وقيل عطف على قوله : "إذا اختلفوا يقدم ما اختاره أبو حنيفة سواء وافقه أحد أصحابه أو لا " .
حكم مخالفة الصاحبين الإمام
وقيل : لا يتخير إلا المفتي المجتهد، فيختار ما كان دليله أقوى . قال في "الفتاوى السراجية (١): " ثم الفتوى على الإطلاق على قول أبي حنيفة ) ، ثم قول أبي يوسف، ثم قول محمد، ثم قول زفر والحسن ابن زياد . وقيل : إذا كان أبو حنيفة في جانب، وصاحباه في جانب، فالمفتى بالخيار . والأول أصح إذا لم يكن المفتى مجتهدا". اهـ. ومثله في متن التنوير (٣) أول كتاب القضاء . وقال في آخر كتاب "الحاوى القدسي " : " ومتى لم يوجد في المسألة عن أبى حنيفة رواية، يؤخذ بظاهر قول أبي يوسف، ثم بظاهر قول محمد، ثم بظاهر قول زفر والحسن وغيرهم، الأكبر فالأكبر إلى آخر من كان من كبار الأصحاب . وقال قبله : ومتى كان قبول أبي يوسف ومحمد موافق قوله،
(١) قوله : قال في السراجية" شروع فى نقل عبارات القوم تأييدا لما ذكره، كما هو عادته فى هذا الكتاب . وقوله : "ثم الفتوى على الإطلاق " أي سواء انفرد وحده في جانب أو لا، كما يفيده كلام "السراجية من مقابلته بالقول الثانى المفصل . فافهم. كذا في رد المحتار". وفسر الطحطاوى الإطلاق بقوله : أى من غير نظر لقوة
المدرك. وفسّر فى باب القضاء بما فسره الشامي في رد المحتار: (مظ)
(٢) قوله : "على قول أبي حنيفة أى الفتوى على الإطلاق أى سواء وافقه أحد أصحابه أم لا] على قول أبي حنيفة ؛ لأنه رأى الصحابة، وزاحم التابعين في الفتوى، كما قاله عبد الله بن المبارك، فقوله أسد وأقوى ما لم يكن اختلاف عصر وزمان. كذا في تصحيح العلامة قاسم . لكن ينقض هذا بقولهم : يعمل بقول أبي يوسف في القضاء لتجربته، ويطلقون ذلك . (مظ)
(٣) قوله : " ومثله في متن التنوير " حيث قال : ويأخذ بقول أبي حنيفة على الإطلاق ثم يقول أبي يوسف، ثم بقول محمد، ثم بقول زفر والحسن بن زياد وقوله : " مثله " أى مثل ما ذكر في "الفتاوى السراجية " مذكور في تنوير الأبصار" قلت : وكذا ذكره صاحب منية المصلى في كتاب أدب القضاء كما يظهر من الطحطاوي . (مظ)
Ruling on the opposition of the two companions to the Imam. Section on the ordering between transmissions of the madhhab, and the ruling when the two companions oppose the Imam. You already know what we established earlier: that what our Imams agreed upon is not permissible for a mujtahid in their madhhab to deviate from with his opinion, because their opinion is more correct. And I indicated here that when they differ, precedence is given to what Abu Hanifa chose, whether one of his companions agrees with him or not. If no choice of his exists, precedence is given to what Ya'qub chose—he is Abu Yusuf, the greatest of the Imam's companions. And the habit of Imam Muhammad is that he mentions Abu Yusuf by his kunya except when he mentions him with Abu Hanifa, then he mentions him by his personal name, saying: 'Ya'qub from Abu Hanifa.' And that was by the will of Abu Yusuf, out of respect for his teacher Abu Hanifa—may Allah Almighty have mercy on them all and have mercy on us through them, and perpetuate benefit through them until the Day of Resurrection. And where no choice of Abu Yusuf exists, the statement of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan is given precedence—the most eminent of Abu Hanifa's companions after Abu Yusuf. Then after him, the statement of Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad is given precedence, and their statements are of one rank, but the wording of al-Nahr: 'Then by the statement of al-Hasan.' And it is said: When his companions oppose him and he is alone in a statement, the mufti may choose. (Footnote: This is the statement that begins 'It is said' in reference to the earlier statement: 'When they differ, precedence is given to what Abu Hanifa chose, whether one of his companions agrees with him or not.') Ruling on the opposition of the two companions to the Imam. And it is said: Only the mujtahid mufti may choose, so he chooses whichever has stronger evidence. He said in al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya: 'Then the fatwa absolutely is on the statement of Abu Hanifa, then the statement of Abu Yusuf, then the statement of Muhammad, then the statement of Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. And it is said: If Abu Hanifa is on one side and his two companions on the other, the mufti has a choice. And the first is more correct when the mufti is not a mujtahid.' End quote. And similar is in the text of al-Tanwir at the beginning of the Book of Judgement. And he said at the end of the book al-Hawi al-Qudsi: 'And whenever there is no narration from Abu Hanifa on a matter, one takes the apparent meaning of Abu Yusuf's statement, then the apparent meaning of Muhammad's statement, then the apparent meaning of Zufar and al-Hasan and others, the greater then the greater, up to the last of the senior companions.' And before that he said: 'And whenever the acceptance of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agrees with his statement...'
Source pages p. 104

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Uqood Rasm al-Mufti lays down a critical methodological rule for the Hanafi mufti: the hierarchy of madhhab opinions when the founding imams disagree. The author begins by reaffirming a fundamental principle: matters agreed upon by all Hanafi imams cannot be overridden by any later mujtahid in the madhhab, because their collective opinion is more authoritative. When they differ, the rule is clear: first look for a choice (ikhtiyar) of Imam Abu Hanifa; if found, that prevails even if none of his students agree. If no opinion is recorded from Abu Hanifa, then take the choice of Abu Yusuf (Ya'qub). The text explains a terminological nuance: Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, in his writings, calls Abu Yusuf by his kunya 'Abu Yusuf' except when mentioning him with Abu Hanifa, then he says 'Ya'qub from Abu Hanifa'—this was Abu Yusuf's own instruction as a sign of respect. After Abu Yusuf, the opinion of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan is taken, then after him the opinions of Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. There is a difference: some sources (like al-Nahr) rank al-Hasan after Zufar, while the more common view treats them as equal. The passage then presents a secondary dispute: if Abu Hanifa is alone against both his companions (the Sahibayn), some say the mufti may choose freely. But the relied-upon position, as stated in al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya, is that the mufti must follow the absolute hierarchy: Abu Hanifa, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar/Hasan. The only exception is if the mufti is himself a mujtahid, in which case he may evaluate the strength of evidence. However, the correct view (al-ashah) for non-mujtahid muftis is to follow the fixed order. The passage then cites al-Hawi al-Qudsi for cases where nothing is transmitted from Abu Hanifa: one takes the apparent meaning of Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar/Hasan, and so on, always preferring the greater (senior) companion. This entire discussion is central to the theory of ifta — it defines how a mufti navigates internal madhhab disagreement without stepping outside the school.

Key Terms
رتبة (rutba): Hierarchical rank among the mujtahid imams of the madhhab for the purpose of preferring one opinion over another in ifta.
الصاحبان (al-sahiban): The two companions of Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, whose collective opinion sometimes opposes Abu Hanifa's individual view.
الإطلاق (al-itlaq): Absolute application of the ruling without qualification; here it means the mufti follows the hierarchy without considering the strength of evidence or circumstances.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon hierarchy in the Hanafi madhhab, which opinion should a non-mujtahid mufti give precedence to when there is disagreement?
A) The opinion of Abu Yusuf, because he was the most experienced in qada.
B) The opinion of Abu Hanifa, regardless of whether his companions agree.
C) The opinion of Muhammad ibn al-Hasan, since he compiled the most books.
D) Any opinion he chooses, because conflict allows ijtihad.

Key Takeaway
A non-mujtahid Hanafi mufti must follow a strict hierarchy in ifta: first Abu Hanifa, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar/Hasan, and may not choose based on personal preference.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
12 min
Fatwa Hierarchy When Sahibayn Differ from Imam Abu Hanifa
Rasm al-mufti rule on fatwa hierarchy in ikhtilaf cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi fatwa hierarchy when the Sahibayn disagree with Imam Abu Hanifa, distinguishing between the ruling for a non-mujtahid and a mujtahid mufti.

Core Idea
Sacrifice madhhab hierarchy for evidence only when you are a mujtahid; otherwise, stick to Imam's saying.

The Hanafi school is built on a cascade of authority: Imam Abu Hanifa, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then others. But what happens when the Imam's two star students unite against him? This lesson unpacks the nuanced rule that distinguishes between a mufti who simply follows and a mufti who can weigh evidence. You will learn not just the hierarchy, but the precise conditions under which a mufti may depart from it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios of disagreement
Break down the passage into the four cases: (1) Imam and Sahibayn agree, (2) one companion agrees with Imam, (3) Imam alone vs each companion separately, (4) Imam alone vs both companions agreeing on one ruling.
Scenario 4 is the most contested.
2
Distinguish between the mufti's levels
Note the key distinction: a non-mujtahid mufti must follow the hierarchy (Imam first); a mujtahid mufti may choose based on evidence strength.
Mujtahid here means a mufti capable of tarjih within the madhhab.
3
Apply the hierarchy to each scenario
For each scenario, state what the non-mujtahid mufti does and what the mujtahid mufti may do. Use the Sirajiyyah and al-Hawi as references.
Remember: 'al-amm yufidu al-wujub' does not apply here; this is a rasm al-mufti rule.
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: a non-mujtahid mufti has no choice; a mujtahid mufti can override the hierarchy if evidence is stronger.
This prevents blind taqlid when evidence is clear.
5
Formulate the fatwa hierarchy as a rule
Summarize the hierarchy: (1) Imam, (2) Abu Yusuf, (3) Muhammad, (4) Zufar and al-Hasan, with the exception that a mujtahid mufti may choose based on evidence when Sahibayn agree against Imam.
This is the mufta bihi position per al-Sirajiyyah and al-Hawi.

حكم مخالفة الصاحبين الإمام
وقيل : لا يتخير إلا المفتي المجتهد، فيختار ما كان دليله أقوى . قال في "الفتاوى السراجية (۱): " ثم الفتوى على الإطلاق على قول أبي حنيفة ) ، ثم قول أبي يوسف، ثم قول محمد، ثم قول زفر والحسن ابن زياد . وقيل : إذا كان أبو حنيفة في جانب، وصاحباه في جانب، فالمفتى بالخيار . والأول أصح إذا لم يكن المفتى مجتهدا". اهـ. ومثله في متن التنوير (۳) أول كتاب القضاء . وقال في آخر كتاب "الحاوى القدسي " : " ومتى لم يوجد في المسألة عن أبى حنيفة رواية، يؤخذ بظاهر قول أبي يوسف، ثم بظاهر قول محمد، ثم بظاهر قول زفر والحسن وغيرهم، الأكبر فالأكبر إلى آخر من كان من كبار الأصحاب . وقال قبله : ومتى كان قبول أبي يوسف ومحمد موافق قوله،
لا يتعدى عنه إلا فيما مست إليه الضرورة، وعلم أنه لو كان أبو حنيفة رأي ما رأوا ، لأفتى به . وكذا إذا كان أحدهما معه. فإن خالفاه في الظاهر، قال بعض المشايخ : يأخذ بظاهر قوله. وقال بعضهم : المفتى مخير بينهما، إن شاء أفتى بظاهر قوله، وإن شاء أفتى بظاهر قولهما. والأصح أن العبرة لقوة الدليل" . اهـ . والحاصل (۱) أنه إذا اتفق أبو حنيفة وصاحباه على جواب، لم يجز العدول عنه إلا لضرورة، وكذا إذا وافقه أحدهما . وأما إذا انفرد عنهما بجواب وخالفاه فيه، فإن انفرد كل منهما بجواب أيضا، بأن لم يتفقا على شيء واحد، فالظاهر ترجيح قوله أيضا. وأما إذا خالفاه واتفقا على جواب واحد، حتى صار هو في جانب وهما في جانب، فقيل : يرجح قوله أيضا، وهذا قول الإمام عبد الله بن المبارك . وقيل : يتخير المفتى. وقول "السراجية " : " والأول أصح إذا لم يكن المفتى مجتهداً " ، يفيد اختيار القول الثاني إن كان المفتى مجتهداً . ومعنى تخييره :(٢) أنه ينظر في الدليل، فيفتى بما يظهر له ولا يتعيّن عليه قول الإمام. وهذا الذي صححه في "الحاوى " أيضاً بقوله : " والأصح أن
Ruling on opposing the Imam by the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And it is said: The mufti does not exercise choice unless he is a mujtahid, then he chooses what has the strongest evidence. It is stated in al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyyah: 'Then fatwa in absolute terms is upon the saying of Abu Hanifa, then the saying of Abu Yusuf, then the saying of Muhammad, then the saying of Zafar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad.' And it is said: When Abu Hanifa is on one side and his two companions on the other, the mufti has the choice. The first opinion is more correct when the mufti is not a mujtahid. Similarly in Tanwir al-Absar at the beginning of the Book of Judiciary. And it is stated at the end of al-Hawi al-Qudsi: 'When there is no narration from Abu Hanifa on a matter, the apparent saying of Abu Yusuf is taken, then the apparent saying of Muhammad, then the apparent saying of Zafar and al-Hasan and others, the greater then the greater, up to the last of the senior companions.' And he said earlier: 'When the acceptance of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad is in agreement with his saying, it is not departed from except where necessity arises, and it is known that if Abu Hanifa had seen what they saw, he would have given fatwa accordingly.' Similarly when one of them is with him. If they both oppose him in apparent (ruling), some scholars said: The apparent saying of Abu Hanifa is taken. Some said: The mufti has a choice between them, if he wishes he gives fatwa according to the apparent of Abu Hanifa, and if he wishes according to the apparent of them both. The most correct view is that consideration is given to the strength of evidence. Summary: When Abu Hanifa and his two companions agree on a ruling, it is not permissible to deviate from it except for necessity, and similarly when one of them agrees with him. As for when he is alone with a ruling and they oppose him, and each of them also has an individual ruling (i.e., they do not agree on one ruling), then apparently his ruling is also given preference. As for when they oppose him and agree on one ruling, such that he is on one side and they are on the other, it is said: His ruling is still given preference—this is the saying of Imam Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak. And it is said: The mufti has a choice. The statement of al-Sirajiyyah: 'The first is more correct when the mufti is not a mujtahid' indicates the choice of the second opinion if the mufti is a mujtahid. The meaning of his choice is: He looks at the evidence and gives fatwa according to what appears to him, and the saying of the Imam is not binding upon him. This is what was deemed correct in al-Hawi as well by his statement: 'The most correct is that...'
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a rasm al-mufti rule establishing the hierarchy of Hanafi opinions when the Sahibayn differ from Imam Abu Hanifa. The author presents the standard hierarchy: absolute precedence to Abu Hanifa, then Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad, then Zufar and al-Hasan ibn Ziyad. However, he also introduces a nuance: if the mufti is a mujtahid (one capable of weighing evidence within the madhhab), he may choose the strongest evidence rather than blindly following the hierarchy. The passage then details four scenarios: (1) If Imam and Sahibayn agree, no departure except necessity. (2) If one companion agrees with Imam, same rule. (3) If Imam is alone and Sahibayn each have separate rulings, Imam is preferred. (4) If Sahibayn agree on a single ruling against Imam, there is a dispute: some say Imam is still preferred (Ibn al-Mubarak), others say the mufti has choice. The correct view according to al-Sirajiyyah is that the first (Imam's precedence) holds when the mufti is not a mujtahid; if he is a mujtahid, he may choose based on evidence. The commentary emphasizes that 'mujtahid' here means one who understands the madhhab's principles and can weigh evidence, not an absolute mujtahid. The passage concludes that this latter view is also affirmed in al-Hawi.

Key Terms
الصاحبين (al-Sahibayn): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two principal students of Imam Abu Hanifa.
مفتى مجتهد (mufti mujtahid): A mufti capable of weighing evidence within the Hanafi madhhab and preferring one opinion over another based on strength of proof.
فتوى على الإطلاق (fatwa 'ala al-itlaq): Absolute fatwa without restriction, meaning the general rule applied regardless of the mufti's level.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sirajiyyah, when a mufti is not a mujtahid, and Imam Abu Hanifa is on one side while the Sahibayn are on the other with an agreed ruling, what should the mufti do?
A) He is free to choose either opinion based on evidence.
B) He must follow the Sahibayn because they are two.
C) He must follow Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion.
D) He must refer the matter to another mufti.

Key Takeaway
A non-mujtahid mufti must follow the hierarchy (Imam first); a mujtahid mufti may prioritize evidence over hierarchy when the Sahibayn agree against Imam.

Rasm al-Mufti — Rules of Issuing Fatwa
intermediate
10 min
The Mufti's Hierarchy When Sahibayn Disagree with Imam Abu Hanifa
Rasm rule on mufti's hierarchy when Sahibayn oppose Imam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rasm rule to determine which opinion a mufti should follow when the two companions disagree with Imam Abu Hanifa, distinguishing between a mujtahid and a non-mujtahid mufti.

Core Idea
A non-mujtahid mufti must follow Imam Abu Hanifa; a mujtahid mufti weighs evidence.

This passage from Sharh 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti addresses a critical question in Hanafi fatwa methodology: what should a mufti do when his Imam's two leading disciples contradict the Imam? The answer depends on the mufti's own level of ijtihad. We will dissect the three opinions and the relied-upon position that reconciles them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenarios
List the four possible agreement/disagreement patterns between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Sahibayn). The passage covers: (1) all three agree; (2) one companion agrees with Imam; (3) Imam disagrees with both but they disagree with each other; (4) Imam disagrees with both and they agree on one answer.
The most complex scenario is (4), which triggers the ikhtilaf.
2
Recognize the Three Opinions
For scenario (4), three scholarly positions exist: (a) always follow Imam (Ibn al-Mubarak); (b) mufti has absolute choice; (c) the mufti is free to choose if he is a mujtahid, but a non-mujtahid must follow Imam.
The passage calls opinion (c) the most correct (al-asahh).
3
Apply the Mujtahid vs Non-Mujtahid Distinction
The relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that a non-mujtahid mufti must adhere to Imam Abu Hanifa's statement, while a mujtahid mufti (one who can weigh evidences) may choose the opinion with stronger evidence (qawwat al-dalil).
This distinction reconciles the earlier conflicting statements.
4
Determine the Mufti's Duty in Absence of Imam's Text
If no explicit text from Imam exists, a non-mujtahid gives precedence to Abu Yusuf, then Muhammad. A mujtahid may choose based on evidence.
This is analogous to the rule when Sahibayn disagree with Imam.

شرح عقود رسم المفتي حكم مخالفة الصاحبين الإمام لا يتعدى عنه إلا فيما مست إليه الضرورة، وعلم أنه لو كان أبو حنيفة رأي ما رأوا، لأفتى به. وكذا إذا كان أحدهما معه. فإن خالفاه في الظاهر، قال بعض المشايخ: يأخذ بظاهر قوله. وقال بعضهم: المفتى مخير بينهما، إن شاء أفتى بظاهر قوله، وإن شاء أفتى بظاهر قولهما. والأصح أن العبرة لقوة الدليل. اهـ. والحاصل (1) أنه إذا اتفق أبو حنيفة وصاحباه على جواب، لم يجز العدول عنه إلا لضرورة، وكذا إذا وافقه أحدهما. وأما إذا انفرد عنهما بجواب وخالفاه فيه، فإن انفرد كل منهما بجواب أيضا، بأن لم يتفقا على شيء واحد، فالظاهر ترجيح قوله أيضا. وأما إذا خالفاه واتفقا على جواب واحد، حتى صار هو في جانب وهما في جانب، فقيل: يرجح قوله أيضا، وهذا قول الإمام عبد الله بن المبارك. وقيل: يتخير المفتى. وقول "السراجية": "والأول أصح إذا لم يكن المفتى مجتهداً"، يفيد اختيار القول الثاني إن كان المفتى مجتهداً. ومعنى تخييره: (2) أنه ينظر في الدليل، فيفتى بما يظهر له ولا يتعيّن عليه قول الإمام. وهذا الذي صححه في "الحاوي" أيضاً بقوله: "والأصح أن العبرة لقوة الدليل"؛ لأن اعتبار قوة الدليل شأن المفتي المجتهد. فصار فيما إذا خالفه صاحباه ثلاثة أقوال: الأول: اتباع قول الإمام بلا تخيير. الثاني: التخيير مطلقا. الثالث: وهو الأصح، التفصيل بين المجتهد وغيره، وبه جزم قاضيخان كما يأتي. والظاهر أن هذا توفيق بين القولين بحمل القول باتباع قول الإمام على المفتى الذي هو غير مجتهد، وحمل القول بالتخيير على المفتى المجتهد. وإذا لم يوجد للإمام نص، يقدم قول أبي يوسف، ثم محمد، والظاهر أن هذا في حق غير المجتهد، أما المفتى المجتهد فيتخير بما يترجح عنده دليله، نظير ما قبله. مطلب يجوز اتباع الدليل وترك قول الإمام وإن وافقه أحد صاحبيه وقد علم من هذا: أنه لا خلاف في الأخذ بقول الإمام إذا وافقه أحدهما، ولذا قال الإمام قاضيخان (2): "وإن كانت المسألة مختلفا فيها بين أصحابنا، فإن كان مع أبي حنيفة أحد صاحبيه، يأخذ بقولهما، أي بقول الإمام ومن وافقه؛ لوفور الشرائط واستجماع أدلة الصواب فيها. وإن خالفه صاحباه في ذلك، فإن كان اختلافهم اختلاف عصر وزمان كالقضاء بظاهر".
Commentary on 'Uqud Rasm al-Mufti: The ruling of disagreeing with the two companions (of Imam Abu Hanifa). The Imam's opinion is not departed from except in case of necessity, and it is known that if Abu Hanifa had seen what they saw, he would have given fatwa accordingly. Likewise if one of them is with him. If they both disagree with him outwardly, some scholars said: he takes the apparent meaning of his (Imam's) statement. Some said: the mufti has a choice between them; if he wishes he gives fatwa according to the apparent meaning of Imam's statement, and if he wishes he gives fatwa according to the apparent meaning of their statement. The most correct is that consideration is given to the strength of the evidence. End. The summary (1) is that if Abu Hanifa and his two companions agree on a ruling, it is not permissible to deviate from it except for necessity, and likewise if one of them agrees with him. But if he (Imam) gives an answer alone while they disagree with him, and each of them also gives a separate answer (i.e., they do not agree on one thing), then apparently his statement is also preferred. But if they both disagree with him and agree on one answer, so that he is on one side and they are on the other, it is said: his statement is also preferred — this is the statement of Imam Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak. And it is said: the mufti has a choice. The statement of 'al-Sirajiyya': 'The first is more correct if the mufti is not a mujtahid,' indicates choosing the second opinion if the mufti is a mujtahid. The meaning of his choice: (2) he looks at the evidence and gives fatwa according to what appears to him, and the Imam's statement is not binding on him. This is what was corrected in 'al-Hawi' as well, by his statement: 'The most correct is that consideration is given to the strength of the evidence'; because considering the strength of evidence is the affair of the mujtahid mufti. So regarding when his two companions disagree with him, there are three opinions: First: following the Imam's statement without choice. Second: absolute choice. Third: and it is the most correct, the distinction between the mujtahid and others; and Qadikhan affirmed it as will come. Evidently this is a reconciliation between the two opinions by carrying the opinion of following the Imam's statement to the mufti who is not a mujtahid, and carrying the opinion of choice to the mujtahid mufti. And if there is no explicit text from the Imam, the statement of Abu Yusuf is given precedence, then Muhammad; and apparently this is for the non-mujtahid. As for the mujtahid mufti, he has choice according to what is stronger in his sight, similar to the preceding. Section: It is permissible to follow the evidence and leave the Imam's statement even if one of his two companions agrees with him. It is known from this: there is no disagreement about taking the Imam's statement when one of them agrees with him. Hence Imam Qadikhan said (2): 'If the issue is disputed among our companions, and one of the two companions is with Abu Hanifa, he takes their statement, i.e., the statement of the Imam and whoever agrees with him, because of the abundance of conditions and the gathering of the evidences of correctness in it. And if his two companions disagree with him in that, then if their disagreement is a disagreement of era and time like judging by the apparent...'
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage discusses the ruling (hukm) of a mufti when Imam Abu Hanifa's two companions, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree with him. It is a rasm rule (methodological principle) for fatwa issuance. The passage begins by stating the default: the Imam's opinion is not departed from except in necessity. If one of the companions agrees with the Imam, the combined view is authoritative. When both disagree with the Imam, early scholars differed: some held the mufti must follow the Imam's apparent statement; others allowed the mufti to choose between the Imam's statement and that of the two companions. The most correct position (al-asahh) is that the mufti should consider the strength of the evidence (qawwat al-dalil), but this only applies to a mujtahid mufti. The passage then summarizes four logical scenarios: (1) Imam and both companions agree — no deviation; (2) one companion agrees with Imam — same; (3) Imam disagrees with both, and the two companions also disagree among themselves — the Imam's statement is still preferred; (4) Imam disagrees with both, and the two companions agree on one answer — three opinions: (a) always follow Imam, (b) absolute choice, (c) the strongest: a non-mujtahid mufti follows Imam, a mujtahid mufti chooses based on evidence. The latter is the relied-upon position (al-mukhtar). The term 'mujtahid mufti' here refers to one who knows his Imam's methodology and can weigh evidences, not necessarily an absolute mujtahid. The passage also rules on when there is no explicit text from Imam: a non-mujtahid gives precedence to Abu Yusuf then Muhammad; a mujtahid chooses. Finally, a special section clarifies that even if one of the two companions agrees with Imam, the mufti follows the combined view; only when both disagree does the above analysis apply.

Key Terms
المفتى المجتهد (al-mufti al-mujtahid): A mufti who has knowledge of his Imam's methodology, principles, and can weigh evidences to prefer one opinion over another, not necessarily an absolute mujtahid.
قوة الدليل (qawwat al-dalil): The strength of evidence; the criterion for preferring one opinion over another in case of ikhtilaf among Hanafi imams.
الصاحبان (al-sahiban): The two companions of Imam Abu Hanifa: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the most correct opinion (al-asahh), when the two companions (Sahibayn) disagree with Imam Abu Hanifa and agree on a single answer, what should a mufti who is not a mujtahid do?
A) He is free to choose between Imam's opinion and the companions' opinion.
B) He must follow Imam Abu Hanifa's opinion without choice.
C) He must follow the two companions' opinion because they are the majority.
D) He should refer the matter to a higher authority.

Key Takeaway
A non-mujtahid mufti must follow Imam Abu Hanifa when both companions disagree with him; a mujtahid mufti may choose based on the strength of evidence.

Three Opinions When Sahibayn Disagree with Imam Abu Hanifa
Establishing the three opinions and the valid distinction for mufti

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three opinions when the Sahibayn disagree with Imam Abu Hanifa, apply the correct ruling based on the mufti's level (mujtahid or muqallid), and determine the precedence order when no Imam text is found.

Core Idea
Mufti's choice depends on ijtihad capacity; mujtahid follows evidence, muqallid follows Imam.

This passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti addresses a critical question in Hanafi fatwa methodology: what should a mufti do when the two main students of Imam Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree with him? The author presents three opinions in the madhhab and settles on the distinction between a mujtahid mufti and a non-mujtahid mufti. Understanding this distinction is essential for proper fatwa practice and avoiding methodological errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
The passage begins by stating that the criterion is strength of evidence (قوة الدليل). This is the overarching principle for a mujtahid mufti.
Remember that considering evidence strength is the concern of the mujtahid mufti.
2
List the three opinions
When Sahibayn disagree with Imam Abu Hanifa, there are three views: (1) follow Imam without choice, (2) absolute choice for the mufti, (3) distinction between mujtahid and non-mujtahid.
The third opinion is deemed the most correct (al-asahh) and is affirmed by Qadikhan.
3
Understand the reconciliation
The third opinion reconciles the first two: 'follow Imam' applies to non-mujtahid muftis; 'absolute choice' applies to mujtahid muftis who weigh evidence.
This distinction avoids both extremes.
4
Apply the subsidiary rule for non-mujtahid
If no explicit text from Imam Abu Hanifa is found, the non-mujtahid mufti should follow Abu Yusuf's opinion first, then Muhammad's. A mujtahid mufti, however, chooses based on his own weighing of evidence.
This rule is specifically for non-mujtahid muftis.

حكم مخالفة الصاحبين الإمام العبرة لقوة الدليل؛ لأن اعتبار قوة الدليل شأن المفتي المجتهد. فصار فيما إذا خالفه صاحباه ثلاثة أقوال: الأول: اتباع قول الإمام بلاتخيير. الثاني: التخيير مطلقا. الثالث: وهو الأصح، التفصيل بين المجتهد وغيره وبه جزم قاضيخان كما يأتي. والظاهر أن هذا توفيق بين القولين بحمل القول باتباع قول الإمام على المفتي الذي هو غير مجتهد، وحمل القول بالتخيير على المفتي المجتهد. وإذا لم يوجد للإمام نص، يقدم قول أبي يوسف، ثم محمد، والظاهر أن هذا في حق غير المجتهد، أما المفتي المجتهد فيتخير بما يترجح عنده دليله، نظير ما قبله.
The ruling of the two companions' disagreement with the Imam: The criterion is the strength of the evidence, because considering the strength of the evidence is the concern of the mujtahid mufti. Thus, regarding when the two companions disagree with him, there are three sayings: First: following the Imam's saying without choice. Second: absolute choice. Third: and it is the most correct, the distinction between the mujtahid and others, and Qadikhan affirmed this as will come. And apparently this is a reconciliation between the two sayings, by interpreting the saying of following the Imam's saying as applying to the mufti who is not a mujtahid, and interpreting the saying of choice as applying to the mujtahid mufti. And if no text is found from the Imam, the saying of Abu Yusuf is given precedence, then Muhammad, and apparently this is concerning the non-mujtahid; as for the mujtahid mufti, he chooses according to what he deems stronger in evidence, analogous to what preceded.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Uqud Rasm al-Mufti presents a rasm rule: what should a mufti do when the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) disagree with Imam Abu Hanifa? The author states the principle: the criterion is strength of evidence. Then he lists three opinions in the madhhab. The first says to follow the Imam absolutely, no choice. The second says the mufti has absolute choice. The third, deemed most correct (al-asahh), distinguishes between a mujtahid mufti and a non-mujtahid. For the non-mujtahid, he must follow the Imam; for the mujtahid, he may choose based on evidence. Qadikhan affirmed this third view. The author then adds: if no text is found from Imam Abu Hanifa, the order of precedence is Abu Yusuf then Muhammad—this is for the non-mujtahid. A mujtahid mufti weighs the evidence himself.

Key Terms
الصاحبين (al-sahibayn): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two main students of Abu Hanifa.
المفتي المجتهد (al-mufti al-mujtahid): A mufti qualified to perform ijtihad independently.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only a mujtahid mufti
D) No mufti has choice

Key Takeaway
The correct approach to Sahibayn's disagreement depends on the mufti's ijtihad capacity: non-mujtahid follows the Imam, mujtahid follows evidence.

Qadikhan's Four Scenarios for Mufti's Choice
Detailing four scenarios for mufti's choice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify Qadikhan's four scenarios for the mufti's choice when Sahibayn disagree with Imam Abu Hanifa and apply the mufta bihi ruling for each scenario.

Core Idea
When Sahibayn differ from Imam, the mufti does not always follow the Imam; context and consensus matter.

This passage from the work on fatwa methodology presents Qadikhan's detailed classification of when a mufti should follow the Imam, the Sahibayn, or exercise independent choice. It is a key text for understanding the Hanafi fatwa hierarchy in cases of internal disagreement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four scenarios in Qadikhan's text
Read the passage and list the four distinct cases Qadikhan mentions: (1) one Sahib with Imam, (2) both against Imam in time-sensitive matters, (3) both against Imam in custom-based transactions, (4) all other cases.
The fourth scenario gives the mujtahid mufti choice.
2
Determine the mufta bihi for each scenario
For each case, state which opinion the mufti should follow: (1) follow Imam and that Sahib, (2) follow Sahibayn, (3) follow Sahibayn, (4) mufti may choose.
Note the exceptions: 'urf and consensus of later scholars override.
3
Analyze the basis for each rule
Understand the reasoning: in (1) 'abundance of conditions', (2) 'change of times', (3) 'consensus of later scholars', (4) 'ijtihad'.
This shows Hanafi reliance on custom and later authority.
4
Compare with Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak's view
Contrast the nuanced approach with the blanket view of always following Imam.
This highlights the flexibility within the school.
5
Apply to a modern scenario
Given a modern issue like banking contracts, decide which scenario applies and what the mufti should do.
Use the fourth scenario if no specific consensus; else custom-based.
